t ™ Argonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
who are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
H publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
desci ibe their peculiar tinge, but an idea may be gathered
from this, our standing injunction to our contributors :
Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
sult is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader
up with the literary gossip of the day.

THE RED RAGE.
The City of Dreadful Night.
Canto IY.
He stood alone within the spacious square
declaiming from, the central grassy mound,
With head uncovered and with streaming hair,
As if large multitudes were gathered round:
A stalwart shape, the gestures full of might,
The glances burning with unnatural light :—
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: All was- black,
In heaven no single star, on earth no traok ;
A brooding hush without a stir or note,
The air so thick it clotted in my throat;
And thus for hours; then some enormous things
Swooped past with savage cries and clanking
wings:
But I strode on austere;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: Eyes of fire
Glared at me throbbing with a starved desire;
The hoarse and heavy and carnivorous breath
Was hot upon me from deep jaws of death ;
Sharp claws, swift talons, fleshless fingers cold
Plucked at me from the bushes, tried to hold :
But I strode on austere;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: Lo you, there,
That hillock burning with a brazen glare;
Those myriad dusky flames with points aglow
Which writhed and hissed and darted to and fro ;
A Sabbath of the Serpents, heaped pell-mell
For Devil’s roll-call and some fete of Hell:
Yet I strode on austere ;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: Meteors ran
And crossed their javelins on the black sky-span ;
The zenith opened to a gulf of flame,
The dreadful thunderbolts jarred earth’s fixed
frame ;
The ground all heaved in waves of fire that surged
And weltered round me there sole unsubmerged :
Yet I strode on austere;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: Air once more,
And I was close upon a wild sea-shore;
Enormous cliffs arose on either hand,
The deep tide thundered up a league-broad strand ;
White foambelts seethed there, wan spray swept
and flew;
The sky broke, moon and stars and clouds and
blue:
And I strode on austere ;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert; On the left
The sun arose and crowned a broad crag-cleft;
There stopped and burned out black, except a. rim,
A bleeding eyeless socket, red and dim ;
Whereon the moon fell suddenly south-west,
And stood above the right-hand cliffs at rest:
Still I strode on austere;
No hope could have no fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: From the right
A shape came slowly with a ruddy light;
A woman with a red lamp in her hand,
Bareheaded and barefooted on that strand ;
O desolation moving with such grace !
O anguish with such beauty in thy face 1
I fell as on my bier,
Hope travailed with such fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: I was twain,
Two selves distinct that cannot join again ;
One stood apart and knew, but could not stir,
And watched the other stark in swoon and her;
And she came on, and never turned aside,
Between such sun and moon and roaring tide :
And as she came more near
My soul grew mad with fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: Hell is mild
And piteous matched with that accursed wild ;
A large black sign was on her breast that bowed,
A broad black band ran down her snow-white
shroud ;
That lamp she held was her own burning heart,
Whose blood-drops trickled step by step aoart •
The mystery was clear ;
Mad rage had swallowed fear.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: By the sea
She knelt and bent above that senseless me;
Those lamp-drops fell upon my white brow there,
She tried to cleanse them with her tears and hair •
She murmured words of pity, love, and woe,
She heeded not the level rushing flow:
And mad with rage and foar,
I stood stonebound so near.
As I came through the desert thus it was,
As I came through the desert: When the tide
Swept up to her there kneeling by my side,
She clasped that corpse-like me, and they were
borne
Away, and this vile me was left forlorn;
I know the whole sea cannot quench that heart,
Or cleanse that brow, or wash those two apart:
They love ; their doom is drear,
Yet they nor hope nor fear;
But I, what do I here ?
———
Canto XXI.
Anear the centre of that northern crest
Stands out aJevel upland bleak and bare,
From which the city east and south and west
Sinks gently in long waves; and throned there
An Image sits, stupendous, superhuman,
The bronze colossus of a winged Woman,
Upon a graded granite base foursquare.
Low-seated she leans forward massively,
With cheek on clenched left hand, the forearm’s
might
Erect, its elbow on her rounded knee ;
Across a clasped book in her lap the right
Upholds a pairof compasses ; she gazes
With full set eyes, but warfderirig in thick mazes
Of sombre thought beholdsTio-outward sight.
Words cannot picture her ; but all men know
That solemn sketch the pure sad artist wrought
Three centuries and threescore years ago,
With phantasies of his peculiar thought:
The instruments of carpentry and science
Scattered about her feet, in strange alliance
With the keen wolf-hound*sleeping undistraughc ;
Scales, hour-glass, bell, and magic-square above:
Ihe grave and solid infant perched beside,
With open winglets that might bear a dove,
Intent upon its tablets, heavy-eyed ;
Her folded wings as of a mighty eagle,
But all too impotent to lift the regal
Robustness of her earth-born strength and pride :
And with those wings, and that light wreath
which seems
To mock her grand head and the knotted frown
Of forehead charged with baleful thoughts and
dreams,
The household bunch of keys, the housewife’s
gown
Voluminous, indented, and yet rigid
As if a shell of burnished metal frigid,
The feet thick shod to tread all weakness down;
The comet hanging o’er the waste dark seas,
The massy rainbow curved in front of it,
Beyond the village with the masts and trees :
Ihe snaky imp, dog-headed, from the Pit,
Bearing upon its batlike leathern pinions
Her name unfolded in the sun’s dominions,
The “ Melancolia ” that transcends all wit.
Thus has the artist copied her, and thus
Surrounded to expound her form sublime^
Her fate heroic and calamitous ;
Fronting the dreadful mysteries of Time,
Unvanquished in defeat and desolation,
Undaunted in the hopeless conflagration
Of the day setting on her baffled prime.
Baffled and beaten back she works on still,
Weary and sick of soul she works the more,
Sustained by her indomitable will:
The hands shall fashion and the brain shall pore
mm/ 11 u sorr °w shall be turned to labour,
I ill death, the friend-foe, piercing with his sabre
I hat mighty heart of hearts ends bitter war.
But as if blacker night could dawn on night,
With tenfold gloom on moonless night unstarred,
A sense more tragic than defeat and blight,
More desperate than strife with hope debarred.
More fatal than the adamantine Never
Encompassing her passionate endeavor,
Dawns glooming in her tenebrous regard :
The sense that every struggle brings defeat
Because I ate holds no prize to crown success ;
Ihat all the oracles are dumb or cheat
Because they have no secret to express;
That none can pierce the vast black veil uncer-
tain
Because there is no light beyond the curtain :
Ihat all is vanity and nothingness.
Titanic from her high throne in the north,
I hat City’s sombre Patroness and Queen,
In bronze sublimity she gazes forth
Over her capital of teen and threne,
Over the river with its isles and bridges,
I he marsh and moorland, to the stern rook-ridges
Confronting them with a coeval mien.
The moving moon and stars from east to west
Circle before her in the sea of air;
Shadows and gleams glide round her solemn rest.
Her subjects often gaze up to her there:
The strong to drink new strength of iron endur-
ance,
The weak new terrors ; all, renewed assuranoe
And confirmation of the old despair.
James Thomson.
“The City of Dreadful Night” is only one
among many poems by James Thomson which
justify warm praise and permanent admiration ;
but it is that one with which his name is most
often associated, and that one also which is most
thoroughly distinctive of his talent. He is as well
represented in other of his writings, but nowhere
more characteristically represented.
James Thomson, the second English poet of the
name, was by birth Scotch, and his parents were
Scotch. The father was intemperate ; the mother
a religious enthusiast. Thomson became a school-
master in the army ; gained a friend in Brad-
laugh, and a lover in an armourer-sergeant’s
daughter who died young, clouding Thomson’s
spirit with a lifelong grief. It is clear, however,
that lost love was only the secondary cause of the
melancholy which always pursued 'him, and that
his early pessimism and his later drink-craving
were the results of inherited disease.
Thomson left the army at 28 years of age ; was
for a time in a solicitor’s office ; and for years con-
tributed verse and prose to the London press—
chiefly to the National Reformer. In 1872 he
visited Colorado, America, to report on a silver'
mine, and shortly after was for two months the
New York World's correspondent in Spain, during
the Carlist war. He rar-ely succeeded in earning
more than a bare livelihood ; his nerves failed
under hardships, and he became a confirmed
drunkard ; and gradually he dragged himself to
death in 1882, in the 48th year of his struggle.
Thomson’s poems are published by Reeves and
Turner, London, in two volumes at 6s. each;
there is a third volume of his “ Biographical and
Critical ” prose. His life was written by H. S.
Salt, and J. M. Robertson edited—not ably or sym-
pathetically—a selection of “Poems, Essays, and
Fragments.” Bertram Dobell, Thomson’s friend
and admirer, whose memoir is prefixed to
the two-volume edition, is rather worshipper
than critic; but his useful devotion does
him credit. Several of Thomson’s poems were
published during his lifetime, and some of his
prose : these books and booklets are now out of
print, and are rendered unnecessary by the hand-
some collected edition—which still does not in-
clude all Thomson’s writings. Anybody who has
seen Henry Cargill, “ The Dipsomaniac ” of The
Bulletin, will have a good idea of Thomson’s
personal appearance : the resemblance is as close
as that of J. A. Andrews, “ the Australian anar-
chist’’(though he now disclaims the label), to the
portraits of Verlaine.
James Thomson is a poet who has not yet come
into his kingdom. He is assuredly entitled to
rank with the greater English poets of this cen-
tury, yet even to “ educated ” people he is hardly
known. The atmosphere in which he wrote is
chiefly responsible for this. Thomson never
caught the conventional English tone ; he
was never Respectable, his . intellect raises
him high above the level of the audience
captivated by Tennyson ; and his hatred of the
priestly Lie and the kingly Lie was so sincere and
outspoken that he was inevitably tabooed by a
“religious” and “loyal” community. Yet his
work is so splendid that it is impossible to kill it
by silence or neglect. It is surely reserved for
the noblest perpetuity.
As a poet, Thomson was too .intellectual to
reach the highest imaginative rank. He is rarely
lyrical; he warms it is less with emo-
tion than with thought so intense that it kindles
into flame. His work has the effect of sculpture
rather than of painting, though the sculpture is
?A fcen tinted into a closer approximation of
me. He ls always too human to be hard
or , yet his work would seem hard and
cold it it were not for the sincere man glowing
through it. He was a poet of the Ear rather than
or the Eye, eloquent and lucid, yet rarely potent
to picture vividly. For majestic rhetoric he is
unsurpassed. Milton has no deeper note ' even
Swinburne’s decorative resonance, poetically finer,
is not often so strongly, magnificently soncfrous
as the closing canto of “The City,” where
Thomson literally “ booms ” like surf on a
hollow sea-shore. No one has more inevitable
epithets than Thomson, or a greater mastery over
cadence ; and the loftier his subject the more
iottiiy his language rises to meet it. He is a
master of massive, slowly-moving melody.
Yet, and even at his best, it is rather an atmos-
phere than a thought which Thomson conveys :
one passes through “The City of Dreadful Night ”
and receives, not a strong idea, but a strong im-
pression. It is his mood which dominates you,
not his message. It may be argued that for this
very reason Thomson is all the more poet, and the
argument may be formidably supported. And
ydfc . . . one feels a lack. In Egypt they
?h°w you the mighty temples of a van-
lshed age, sombre, majestic, magnificent,
ihe vast halls, interminable corridors, columns
like great trees, are inexpressibly wonder-
i ’ ® l^“er torch-lighted or unburied to the
cloudless sky. They bring strange and grand
emotions, yet emotions somehow alien from the
pondering spectator. One cannot forget them ;
y® . • • • what is it they have given us ? Not a
thrill, not a thought—an intangible, inscrutable
mood.
It it often so in the best poems of James Thom-
son. So far as it is capable of definition and
analysis, their grandeur seems for the most part
barren. The thrill, the thought, do come occa-
sionally, but their characteristic impression is an
impression of solemn archways, lofty pinnacles,
magnificent chambers, and about them the stern
and gloomy silence of the desert.
* * * •
Yet there is another side to Thomson—a side
which composed verses that are graceful and
something more : like these—
THE FIRE THAT TILLED MY HEART OF OLD.
The Are that filled my heart of old
Gave lustre while it burned ;
Now only ashes grey and cold
Are in its silence urned.
Ah ! better was the_ furious flame,
The splendour with the smart :
I never cared for the singer’s fame,
But, oh ! for the singer's heart
Once more—
The burning fulgent heart !
No love, no hate, no hope, no fear,
No anguish and no mirth ;
Thus life extends from year to year,
A flat of sullen dearth.
Ah ! life’s blood creepeth cold and tame,
Life’s thought plays no new part :
I never cared for the singer’s fame,
But, oh ! for the singer’s heart
Once more—
The bleeding passionate heart!
Then Thomson was learned in French, in, Ger-
man, in Italian : no one has surpassed his transla-
tions of the untranslatable Heine. Here is his
“ Loreley ”
THE LORELEY.
I know not what evil is coming,
But my heart feels sad and cold ;
A song in my head keeps humming,
A tale from the times of old.
The air is fresh and it darkles,
And smoothly flows the Rhine ;
The peak of the mountain sparkles
In the fading sunset-shine.
The loveliest wonderful Maiden
On high is sitting there,
- With golden jewels braiden,
And she combs her golden hair.
With a golden comb sits combing,
And ever the while sings she
A marvellous song through the gloaming
Of magical melody.
It hath caught the boatman, and bound him
In the spelt of a wild sad love ;
He sees not the rocks around him,
He sees only her above.
The waves through the pass sweep swinging,
But boatman or boat i 8 none ;
And this with her mighty singing
The Lorbley hath done.
Thomson wrote epigrams, too :
How many a noble father, since Agamemnon sinned,
Has sacrificed his daughter just to raise the wind !
A good all-round verse workman, in fact, with a
mind tuned to the humorous, the pathetio, and
always to the true and noble.
» * * *
Thompson’s critical prose is hardly less good in
its way than his verse. He saw clearly, felt
keenly, and wrote eloquently. No one has extolled
poetry more fitly and suggestively.
The only true or inspired poetry is always from within,
not from without. The experience obtained in it • has
been spiritually transmuted from lead into gold. It is
severely logical, the most trivial of its adornments being
subservient to and suggested by the dominant idea, any
departure from whose dictates would be the “falsifying
of a revelation.” It Is unadulterated with worldly wisdom,
deference to prevailing opinions, mere talent or clever-
ness. Its anguish is untainted by the gall of bitterness,
its joy is never selfish, its grossness is never obscene. It
perceives always the profound identity underlying all
surface differences. It is a living organism, not a dead
aggregate, and its music is the expression of the law of
its growth ; so that it could no more be set to a different
melody than could a rose-tree be consummated with lilies
or violets. It is most philosophic when most enthusiastic,
the clearest light of it* wisdom being shed from th*
keenest fire of its love. It is a synthesis not arithmetical,
but algebraical; that is to say, its particular subjects are
universal symbols, it predicates universal laws ; hence it
is infinitely suggestive. It is ever-fresh wonder at the
infinite mystery, ever-young faith in the eternal soul.
Whatever be its mood, we feel that it is not self-possessed
but God-possessed ; whether the God came down serene
and stately as Jove, when, a swan, he wooed Leda ; or
With overwhelming might insupportably burning, as when
he consumed Semele.
And, though the Tennyson controversy is over,
see how Thomson anticipated it in 1863, when the
Commonplace Poet was at the height of his Art.
Thomson is discussing the peculiar simplicity, the
childlike emotion in - the face of the universe,
which we find in Blake and Shelley.
Tennyson has no more of this simplicity than had
Byron : his chief youthful fault was such a young-ladyish
affectation as could not exist together with it. But he is
fully aware of its value, and woos it like a lover, in vain,
as Byron wooed it in the latter parts of “ Childe Harold ”
and in “ Manfred.” Perhaps each of them should be
credited with one great exception, in addition to a few
short lyrics : Tennyson with the “ Lotus Eaters,” Byron
with the “ Dream.” Scarcely any other artist in verse of
the same rank has ever lived oh such scanty revenues ol
thought (both pure, and applied or mixed) as Tennyson.
While it cannot be pretended that he is a great sculptor,
he is certainly an exquisite carver of luxuries in
ivory ; but we must be content to admire the
caskets, for there are no jewels inside. His
meditation at the best is that of a good leading-
article ;heis a pensioner on the thought of his age. He
is continually petty with that littleness of the second
degree which makes a man brag aloud in avoiding some
well-known littleness of the first degree. His nerves are
so weak that any event —a Crimean War or a Volunteer
movement—sets him off in hysterics. Nothing gives one
a keener insight into the want of robustness in the edu-
cated English intellect of the age than the fact that nine-
tenths of our best-known literary men look upon him as
a profound philosopher. When wax flowers are oracular
oaks, Dodona may be discovered in the Isle of Wight,
but hardly until then. Mr. Matthew Arnold’s definition
of “distilled thought in distilled words" was surely sug-
gested by the processes and productions of a fashionable
perfumer. A great school of the poets is dying out; it
will die decently, elegantly, in the full odour of respect-
ability, with our Laureate.
* ♦ • *
So the works of J ames Thomson in verse and
prose are to be commended again and many times
again. He is the aggressive Matthew Arnold of
English literature—of the same critical kin, the
same poetic race—but as positive as Arnold was
negative, and nestling as closely to one’s head as
Arnold to one’s heart.
E. E. Slosson gets home on log-rolling Andrew
Lang in Chicago Dial —
It is to be expected that a critic will err, but we wish
he would not boast of his errancy as Mr. Andrew Lang did
a few months ago. His attention was called to the fact
that a book he had condemned in a few careless words
as unworthy of notice had proved a literary success and
m his gracefully facetious way he explains that a critic
has so little time to give to reading that he cannot be
expected to know whether a book is good or not, and that
for his part he does not care whether his Judgments are
correct or false.
This confession disturbed me a good deal, for I had been
relying on Mr. Lang’s criticisms for many vears. A book
he condemned I always read ; and if he attacked a book
savagely I bought it at once, for I knew it must be
worth ovyning. By following this rule I have acquired
ase ect library of the world's best literature with not a
trashy volume in it. But when he says he does not know
and does not care whether the books he reviews are good
or bad, my faith in his negative infallibility is rudely
shaken. I may miss some important work through a
neglected condemnation on his part.
* * * *
Literature says the right word about Alice Mey-
nell s “ precious ” essays :
n? 1 . 6 * act rerna ' ns that Mrs. Meynell is not, and never
will be, an essayist of the first rank. The world takes an
essayist to its heart not because of the writer's amazing
cleverness, nor even because of original thoughts dec£
rated with the most charmingly chosen epithets, but
because it can feel the personality behind the leaves of
the book Behind “ The Spirit of Place ” the world may
possibly delect some shadow beuignly condescending from
a higher sphere, but jf will hardly feel in touch with a
human personality. We are privileged, as it were to
partner a dainty figure, in antiquated but becoming cos-
tume, as she walks gracefully though the intricacies of
some old-fashioned dance. It is excellently well done,
and we do not withhold our admiration, but with all her
courtesies we have riot advanced to any intimacy. We
nuist still keep our distance. It is possible, in literature
this sort of politeness.
the Spirit of Place” is a dainty book, containing
seventeen tiny discourses in not many more than a hun-
dred pages. The writing throughout is after the best
models ; now we come upon a passage that might have
been written by Lamb, now upon a phrase that
" av ® sapped out of “Virginibus Puerisque.”
Mis. Meynell could, without doubt, write you an essav in
the manner of Bacon, or of Addison, and do it to admira-
tion. Possibly she would gain more readers if she wrote
more naturally, and less with the air of one who was
weaving her words, with curious art, into a rare pattern.
But even then—the defect lies deeper than mere words—
the mind is aloof from this world and its poor humanity,
bhei can write tenderly of beggars, but the tenderness is
academic. It is the academic spirit, we fear, that will
prevent Mrs. Meynell from writing anything of permanent
value in the essay form.

OF SUFFERING.
Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “ Ligmolliene,’'
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
1 naturally look same “ Cum Grano Snlis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Mai,yon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M'Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY lIOLTHOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Queen of Yore.
Slowly she hobbles past the town
Grown old at heart and gray,
With misty eyes she stumbles down
Along the well-known way.
She sees her maiden march unrolled
By billabong and bend,
And every gum’s a comrade old
And every oak’s a friend ;
But gone the smiling faces
That welcomed her of yore,
They crowd her tented places
And hold her hand no more.
And she, the friend they loved to trust
To serve their eager wish,
Shall show no more the golden dust
That hides in many a dish.
And through the dismal mullock-heaps
She threads her mournful way
Where here and there some greybeard keeps
His windlass-watch to-day.
Half-flood no more she looses
Her reins, as once of old,
To wash the busy sluices
And whisper through the gold.
She sees no wild-eyed steers above
Stand, spear-horned, on the brink ;
The brumby mobs she used to love
Come down no more to drink ;
Where green the grasses used to twine
Above them, shoulder-deep,
Through the red dust, a long, slow line,
Crawl now the starving sheep ;
She sees no crossing cattle
That Western drovers bring,
No swimming steeds that battle
To block them when they ring.
She sees no barricaded roofs,
No loop-holed station wall,
No foaming steed with flying hoofs
To bring the word “ Ben Hall ! ”
She sees no reckless robbers stoop,
Behind their ambush-stone,
No coach-and-four, no escort troop—
But, very lorn and lone,
Watches the sunsets redden
Along the mountain side,
Where, round the spurs of Weddin,
The wraiths of Weddin ride.
Tho’ fettered with her earthern bars,
And chained with bridge and weir,
She goes her own way by the stars —
She knows the course to steer !
And when her thousand rocky rills
Foam angry to her feet,
Rain-heavy from the Cowra hills,
She takes her vengeance sweet,
And leaps with roar of thunder
And buries bridge and ford,
That all the world may wonder
When the Lachlan bares her sword.
* * * *
Gray River ! Let me take your hand
For all your memories old,
Your cattle-kings, your outlaw-band,
Your wealth of virgin gold,
For once you held, and hold it now,
The sceptre of a queen,
And still upon your furrowed brow
Your royal wreaths are green ;
Hold wide your arms, the waters ;
Lay bare your silver breast,
To nurse the sons and daughters
Of your kingdom in the West !
WILL H. OGILVIE.
“The Mallee Fire.”
I suppose it just depends on where you’re raised.
Once I met a cove as swore by green belar !
Couldn’t sight the good old mallee-stump I
praised :
"Well —l couldn’t sight belar, and there you are !
But the faces in the fire where the mallee-stump’s
a-blinking
Are the friendliest I ever seen, to my way o’
thinking !
In the city where the fires is mostly coal —
There ! I can’t abear to go and warm my feet !
Spitting, fizzing things as hasn’t got no soul !
Things as puffs out yaller smoke instead of heat!
But at home well, it is home when the mallee-
stump’s a-burning
And the evening’s drawing chilly and the season is
a-turning !
And there’s some as runs them down because
they’re tough.
Well ? And what’s the good of anythink as ain’t?
No. It’s nary use to serve ’em any bluff,
For they’d use up all the patience of a saint.
But they’ll split as sweet as sugar if you know the
way to take ’em.
If you don’t, there isn’t nothink in the world as’ll
make ’em !
They’re tremenjus hard to kindle, tho’, at first:
Like a friendship of the kind as comes to stay.
You can blow and blow and blow until you burst,
And when they won’t, they won’t burn, anyway !
But when once they gets a start, tho’ they make
no showy flashes,
Well, they’ll serve you true and honest to the last
pinch of ashes !

Hankee poodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteei s,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘Correspondence" column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing'with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

applying to MS.

SATURDAY, MAY 6, 1899.
Some Federal Precedents.
Precedent, as a rule, is a dreary and back-
ward thing, and no community which is a
slave to it was ever progressive, or is ever
likely to be. For the doctrine of precedent,
when carried to its fullest development, is
based on the theory that no one should have
a perfectly new idea, and that the world in
general should keep close to the safe but
mildewed ways of its forefathers. Still,
after a fashion, precedents have their uses,
and The Bulletin proposes to dig up a few.
The New South Wales provincialist party is
never tired of lamenting in the huge general
ear that certain things in the proposed
Federal Constitution are new, outrageous,
unprecedented, contrary to the old tough,
corned and seasoned experience of this great
world, and calculated to bring the proposed
Commonwealth to ruin. The principal of
these are the equal representation of the
large and small States in the Senate, the
fact that the Legislature or the people can't
alter the Constitution at its pleasure by
a bare majority, and the fact that the
capital isn't to be planted on the open sea-
coa’st at Sydney. And it is interesting to
look up the practice of some of the old,
time-worn, weather-beaten Federal States
—the ones that have braved the battle and
the breeze—and see whether the provin-
cialist argument is true, or whether the
brothers Dibbs and Heydon, also Haynes,
M‘Laughlin, M‘Laurin, Kohen, G. Pile’s
dog, the editor of the Telegraph, and
other raw wiseacres who are now trying
their prentice hand at constitution-making,
and, therefore, know all about it, are really
even more foolish than they look.
There are, in this world, one federal em-
pire (Germany), a semi-federal empire (Aus-
tria-Hungary), a federated colony (Canada),
and 13 more or less federal republics
(Switzerland, United States, Mexico, Argen-
tina, Bolivia, Chili, Columbia, Ecuador,
Nicaragua, Peru, Uruguay, Venezuela,
and Brazil). Of these, Switzerland is
the oldest federal State, the most
liberal and progressive, and incomparably
the best governed. Also, it is practically
the creator of the Federal idea in modern
times. Switzerland gives every canton equal
representation in its Upper House—Berne,
with 541,000 people, Zurich with 351,000,
and Vaud with 256,000 have exactly the
same representation as Uri with 17,000,
Zug with 23,000, and Glarus with 33 000.
The difference between Bern at one end and
Uri at the other is four times greater than
that between New South Wales at one end
of the Australian scale and Westralia at the
other, but the old enduring Swiss republic
finds the arrangement work well enough.
The United States also, has equal State re-
presentation in its Upper House ; so have all
the other republics mentioned Mexico,
Argentina, Brazil, Bolivia, Chili, Columbia,
Ecuador, Nicaragua, Peru, Uruguay, and
v enezula. In the United States there are
great discrepancies, but old Delaware, with
168,000 inhabitants, and its close neighbors,
New York with 6,000,000, and Pennsylvania
with 5,250,000, are equally represented—and
yet nothing bursts.
* * * (
Austria-Hungary also gives equal repre-
sentation to its two kingdoms in the supreme
Legislature. And the only two Federations
where the States are not equally represented
in the Upper House are two of very short
standing the new German empire and
Canada. In the German Bundesrath, Prussia
has 17 members for 32,000,000 people, and
Schaumberg-Lippe has one for 41,000, so the
latter has, in proportion to population, 750
times its arithmetically-correct share of re-
presentation in the Upper House, while
Westralia, as compared with New South
Wales, is to have eight times its arithmeti-
cally correct share. So, as far as equal State
representation in the Federal Senate is con-
cerned, every Federation in the world, bar
Canada and Germany, is based on the al-
legedly unfair and impossible system pro-
posed in Australia, and Germany is success-
fully built on one vastly more impossible.
And the inevitable dreadful consequences
don’t come.
* * « »
But the Australian Commonwealth Consti-
tution is really more liberal in its treatment
of the large states than that of any Federal
republic at present existing. In the pro-
posed Australian Senate, undoubtedly Tas-
mania and Westralia could outvote" New
South Wales by two to one. In the Lower
House New South Wales could outvote them
by five to two. But the deadlock thus
caused would be met by a joint sitting of the
two Houses, and at that joint sitting New
South Wales could wipe out both Tasmania
and Westralia and have nine votes to spare.
In other Federations the House of equal
State representation can hold its own against
the second chamber—in the proposed Aus-
tralian Constitution it can’t. These points
may not matter much, but so far as pre-
cedents go they at least amount to this :
(1) When the provincial party talks of the
equal State representation in the Senate as a
new and hideously unjust innovation, it is
lying: out of the 16 Federations of the wmrld,
14 are built on that plan, including the most-
enduring Federation in the world and the one
always quoted as the model Commonwealth
of the crowd—Switzerland. (2) When the
same crow r d talks of this system as meaning
inevitable ruin it is lying, else Switzer-
land, the United States/ Mexico, Chili,
Argentina and all the other states on the
list quoted above would have broken
down long ago. The Federal Convention,
when it devised the scheme of equal State
representation in the Senate, stuck to the
old, safe, conservative, long-established pre-
cedents of Federalism—the precedents which
the wear and tear of ages has proved to be
workable. The only departure which it
made was in favor of the large States—by
authorising the joint sitting, at which the
equality of the States in the Senate can be
over-ruled.
• • « »
As regards the provision for possible
alterations of the Constitution, the Federal
Convention adopted the Swiss model—the
model of the only Federal State which has
braved the storms of centuries, and one
which is quoted all the world over as an
example of liberalism. In Switzerland the
Federal Constitution can be altered with the
assent of a majority of the States and a
majority of the voters who go to the poll.
In Australia it is proposed to adopt the
same principle. The Swiss Constitution has
been amended under this system, though
the anti-Bill crowd says that the system
makes amendment impossible. In Germany
the Constitution can only be altered with
the assent of two-thirds of the Bundesrath—
the House where the small States are so
enormously over-represented. In the United
States amendments of the Constitution must
be demanded by two-thirds of the States, or
two-thirds of both Houses of the Federal
Legislature, and must be accepted by three-
fourths of the States before they can become
law. Most of the South American Federa-
tions followed the United States model in
this respect, so here again the proposed Aus-
tralian Constitution stuck closely to prece-
dent—only among two or three old-estab-
lished precedents it selected the most
liberal.
* * * *
But one thing for which there is practi-
cally no precedent is the truculent demand
of Sydney—only the second largest city of
Australasia—to be the capital. In Great
Britain, France, Spain, Portugal, Sweden,
Norway, Russia, Denmark, Belgium, Tur-
key, Greece, Austria, Mexico, Brazil, Chili,
Peru, Argentina, China, Japan, and the
great majority of other countries, the capital
has been fixed in the largest city, and, ac-
cording to the vast majority of precedents,
Melbourne should be the political centre of
Australia. On the other hand, the great bulk
of precedent is against the insane idea of
planting the Federal city on an open and
practically indefensible sea-shore ; this tells
against all the Australian capitals, but as
Sydney is the only one of them that is de-
manding to be made the political centre of
the Commonwealth, it tells against Sydney
especially. In all the world outside Austra-
lia the only political capitals which have
been planted on the open shore, so that the
Government could be scattered and the
Treasury snatched by the first invader, are
Lisbon, Stockholm, Copenhagen, Rio
Janeiro, Buenos Ayres, and Monte Video.
And, as most of the great capitals of the
world were the outcome of much experience
in stormy days, and were selected by men
who knew a good deal more about the exi-
gencies of war and invasion than Dibbs and
Heydon and the other unwarlike exponents
of anti-Bill, it is just possible that these
ancients weren’t all quite unwise. Also,
there seems a chance that Dibbs and Majah
Neied don’t represent all the concentrated
intelligence of the earth. At all events, the
bulk of the world’s precedents and the
world’s experience are against the little
howling Cornstalk cult of anti-Bill—what-
ever that experience may be worth.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Westralia and Federation.
Premier Forrest is the only member of the
Westralian Ministry who has expressed him-
self in favor of Federation. The others, headed
by Throssell, Minister for Lands, are likely
to follow the W.A. motto of “ wait a while.”
Westralia clings to its traditions. The typical
groper—the embryo farmer—is held up by the
anti-Federalists in the guise of a sacrifice at the
altar of Federation, and as the greater portion
of the Customs duties is said to be derived
from interprovincial imports, the consequences
of the interprovincial freetrade are made the
most of. But the truth is that though
Westralia, geographically, represents about a
third of the continent, it is very small in other
respects. Individual interests are supreme.
Federation won’t suit the meat-ring for in-
stance, nor the Bunbury potato-growers, whilst
the members of the Ministry themselves would
become very small fry indeed in the Common-
wealth. The majority of the people are sound
on the question of Federation, and on the
benefit of Commonwealth union against the
small thing represented by the local article.
But the majority has not an adequate
voice in the matter. Parliamentary repre-
sentation does not always represent people,
but territory. Alec. Forrest, for instance,
represents 25 electors somewhere in the Nor’-
VVest, and a goldfields member may be sent in
by 2000 electors—but both members have equal
voting-power. Perth represents the Adminis-
tration, and the policy has been to make all
roads lead to Perth, regardless of distance.
But surely the vast seaboard of the West will
stand more than one port, and the Common-
wealth would soon find out if it could. Pro-
bably one of the effects of Federation would be
the opening of the splendid port of Esperance,
and the construction of a railway thence
through Norseman to Coolgardie goldfields, the
distance being only 250 miles, and a saving
from the East at the present time of 500 miles
by the ocean round the Leeuwin to Fremantle,
and of 150 miles by rail from Perth. The bare
suggestion of such a prospect is enough to
freeze Federation within the circle of vested
interests predominating in Parliament. Out-
side the gold-yield, and that goes out of W.A.,
the province has nothing to pool. The sacrifice
of the interprovincial duties, and the interests
of the pork and potato growers, were ostensibly
the lions in the path. Among a few people—a
mere handful—holding a vast territory, the in-
dividual is stronger than the common interest.
The rest of united Australia will not lose much
by the possible action of Westralia, but if that
province should lose the coming opportunity of
joining the union it will not be long in re-
gretting it.

Pishing for Flatheads.
The N.S. W. political Labor League, which
makes a partly-recognised claim to state the
official N.S. W. Laborite policy, held its annual
conference the other day. The party in Par-
liament has for long had a large capacity for
snapping at other parts of itself, but, lately,
Federal issues have disorganised it worse than
ever ; so that the party’s whip made a reckless
dash away from the difficulties which he saw
ahead, and proposed that the conference should
declare the official Labor attitude with regard
to the Commonwealth Bill. Just then the un-
representative character of the conference
dawned on some of the members ; so, to escape
a fiasco, a large part of it suddenly developed
a strong desire to catch an early
train. Ihe official attitude therefore went
undetermined. But now a characteristically
anti-Billious thing has happened. Twelve
months ago the P.L.L. did declare a policy—
one of straight-out opposition to the Bill, which
it wanted to get amended. Laborites therefore
formed part of the triumphant minority on
Referendum-day. After that, the party sup-
ported Reid for all it was worth—became, for
electioneering purposes, a joint in his party’s
tail. For Reid was the chap to get the
amendments. Again and again it saved him in
the House ; he was still the chap to get a
proper Bill. Afterwards, the party entrusted
him with the mission to the Premiers’ Confer-
ence; he was still the chap for them. He
went to the Conference and got what he
could—got the three-fifths majority clause
knocked out and the provision for a,mending the
Constitution liberalised—and those were the
two things that had troubled them most. Yet
now some of their number and several of their
“ official ” papers have the anti-billious gall to
say that, inasmuch as the official Labor attitude
to the original Bill was one of opposition, and
inasmuch as that motion has not been wiped
off, the official attitude is still one of opposi-
tion, and the loyal Labor-men must vote No.
When some kind person asks the anti-Billious
party to come in out of the wet, he doesn’t in-
vite them to stay at his house altogether; he
expects them to shift when it stops raining.
And when some other kind person tells them
to go straight on till they get to a corner,
even G. Pile’s dog knows that they aren’t
expected to go past the corner. If it isn’t
inferred that they are to turn round the corner,
it at least is inferred that they shall seek fresh
directions there. The members of the Labor-
party wanted the Bill amended ; they voted
against it in order to get it amended ; they
chose a chap to do the job ; and he did it as
well as he could. Now they not only want to
repudiate the result, but they want to proceed
as if there had been no general election, no
Parliamentary .votes of confidence in Reid
(carried with their help, too), and no confer-
ence. There might be some sense in arguing
that the official Labor attitude is in support of
the amended Bill, inasmuch as tliev hung up
the old Bill till it was amended and then
appointed a man to amend it; but to say, in
those circumstances, that the official attitude
is still opposition is such a stupid perversion
that those who use that bait must have a poor
opinion of the sense of the fish they hope to
hook with it.
The Bill for the Bush.
“ Bungara ” to The Bulletin :
Why waste shot upon the Shellback paper ! In its own
blundering' fashion it is converting all its readers who are
anti-Billites, and strengthening the resolution of tired
Federalists to vote once again for the Bill. Last year
present writer took a two days’ ride to record his vote,
and spent the day on sentry-go before the court-house to
intercept all the hayseeds who “couldn’t read print with-
out their specs., which they had left at home” (same old
lie of the illiterate). The agony of explaining over and
over again that Snooker, the local member, was not on in
this Act, and that writer was neither asking them to
vote “ for him or agin ’im,” is still a sore memory, re-
lieved by the mental picture of Bill going into the ballot-
box with a copy of the “ brand he was to put a blanky
bar through.” Filled with enthusiasm and bush whisky,
present writer returned home with a large head and a
small, hoarse voice, and, when he saw the result of the
poll a week later, he resolved to let the referendum slide.
But the Shellback paper, and those who howl with it, have
altered that resolution, for what man on the land
can read its shameless appeal to the pockets of the
Riverina pastoralists and not take the trouble to vote for
the Bill, even though he walked 40 miles to do it! By
pointing out that the border pastoralists will lose by the
abolition of the accursed differential railway-rates, the
Shellback paper touches the great bod} - of producers on a
raw place by reminding them that they are already pay-
ing the “ £4 or £5 per ton ’’ to make up the heavy losses
the railway commissioners incur by their insane competi-
tion with the other provinces. I, with hundreds of others,
must pay £4 per ton to have ray wool carried 300 miles so
that the Commissioners may drag the Riverina wool 500
miles for 40s. Why ? Simply that a Sydney broker may
handle it instead of a Melb. broker. Thej 7 have us under
the whip, and squeeze us both ways. Fancy paying ±lO
freight on £2O worth of goods for 300 miles, yet the rail-
way will drag them 200 miles further towards the border
for less money, so that we may neither buy nor sell any-
where but in your accursed octopus of a city, Sydney.
Why, Northern storekeepers have their goods sent 200
miles past their stations and brought back again, because
by so doing they land them cheaper than by having them
consigned direct! We all pay our share of a State-owned
railway, yet the greater portion of the shareholders have
to submit to be robbed in the interest of pot-bellied city
parasites. And this is the system the Sunday Shellback
wishes to perpetuate. Mouthing patriotic sentiments, it
howls “ Who’s for good old N.S. W. ?” and at the same
time insults the patriotism of the border people by im-
ploring them to vote against Federation—not, mark you,
as men who may honesty think that the Federal Bill is
not calculated to advance the prosperity of N.S.W., hut
as wretched beings who should sacrifice all patriotic sen-
timents on the altar of self-interest, by voting for the
maintenance of a system which they admit is an in-
famous injustice to every producer, large or small, who
is not subsidised by differential freights. The same
paper’s efforts to influence the country vote would be
laughable if its arguments were not so irritatingly ab-
surd ; and its terrible forecasts of selectors’ and farmers’
troubles “ under the Blot tariff ’’ are insulting to the
average pastoralists’ intelligence. Why, even the much-
libelled “Bill the bushman ” remarked, when I read
what would happen to him if he voted for the other Bill,
“ Well, we know we’re mugs, ’cos if we wasn’t we’d live
in Sydney ; but we are not blanky, blank flaming mugs.”
To which the present writer fervently adds : “ No, not all
the time we ain’t.” And if Sydney fell into its beautiful
harbor some morning, the producers could easily find
another seaport where men do congregate to live by sell-
ing things to each other on commission. Not being
proud, any sort of a capital would suit us.
Closed Roads.
Attention has frequently been drawn to the
new grip got by the Yic. squatter on the un-
used roads of his province by means of the
carelessly-drafted provisions of the Vermin
Destruction Act. That the danger was no
imaginary one is now shown by a judicial
decision. Judge Hood, in retusing an applica-
tion for a mandamus to compel the East Lod-
don shire council to open certain roads in the
Serpentine Estate, obstructed by vermin-proof
(i.e., selector-proof) fences, said :
These fences were erected by the lessees of the Ser-
pentine Estate, purporting to act under the Vermin Des-
truction Act. By section of 58 of that Act the owner of
land intersected by roads might, with the sanction of the
shire council, enclose any of these roads under certain
specified conditions. This section limited the operation
of Section 428 of the Local Government Act, and enabled
shire councils to allow roads to be closed, with fences and
gates, in order to prevent the spread of vermin.
The italicised words are dangerous ones. Sec-
tion 428 of the Local Government Act above
alluded to really enshrines a great popular
victory. It represents the results of a genera-
tion of agrarian agitations against the squatters.
It renders reasonably safe the inalienable
popular right to the used and unused roads of
the province. Briefly, its effect is that councils
are hound to keep open all roads required for
traffic : that all roads are presumed to he re-
quired for traffic unless the Cabinet decides
otherwise ; hut that where no inconvenience is
caused, temporary swing-gates may be erected
with the permission of the local council and the
Cabinet. The Cabinet may at any time revoke
the permit. But, by an extraordinary, over-
sight, a proviso was allowed to creep into the
Vermin Destruction Act, which enabled the
shire council alone, without consulting the
Cabinet, to permit wholesale fencing across


closed roads in all vermin districts. No merely
temporary swing-gates, but practically per-
manent ones are allowed. Under the old
system a Ministry could be questioned, and, if
necessary, turned out if it allowed undue inter-
ference with roads. Under the new system
the Minister can do nothing whatever, the
whole power in the matter being given to coun-
cils, perhaps composed wholly of squatters —
probably dominated by squatters and their
friends and tradespeople. And the squatters
are annexing the bye-ways of Victoria whole-
sale under this proviso, and peacefully adding
them to their properties, and paying neither
purchase money nor rent for them.

Dangerously “Just Debts.”
Melb. Herald, commenting on the case of a
man who had met with what turned out to be
a fatal accident, and who cried out during his
suffering, “ For God’s sake write my will, as I
don’t want my wife to have any trouble after
I’m gone,” moralises excellently on the duty of
will-making. But, most unfortunately, it pro-
ceeds to tell people how' to do it by giving a
form of will which, if used, as it probably will
he by hundreds, may result in greater loss to
the wives and children of the testators than if
no will had been made at all. For the Herald
will-form contains, as do most forms sold by
stationers, the words, “after payment of all
my just debts,” &c. These words are unneces-
sary, for, whether used or not, the debts must
be first paid; so that the conscientious testator
needn’t alarm himself on that score if he leaves
the words out. But they are not merely un-
necessary, they are dangerous, for the Courts
have put a meaning into them which no tes-
tator in his senses ever would intend without
further minute specification. In most up-to-
date communities there is a law which makes
insurance-moneys up to a certain amount free
from the debts of the testator or intestate.
But the Courts have decided that the words,
“after payment of all my just debts,” in a will
amount to a voluntary foregoing of the benefits
of the law exempting insurance-moneys, and
that they charge the debts on such moneys as
well as the rest of the property of the deceased.
So that, in all provinces where provisions have
not been made correcting the judicial decisions,
those about to make a will are strongly advised
either to leave out the “just debts ” dangerous
surplusage, or not to make a will at all.

A vigilant Port Melbourne constable the other
day hunted round to find a case. Observing a
man, wno, six-and-a-half years ago, had been
convicted of “receiving,” but had since, so far
as is known, been living honestly, entering the
hotel, the zealous “ cop.” forthwith laid infor-
mation under the Licensing Act against the
landlord for “ harboring a convicted thief.”
At the local court, although the individual in
question was proved to have been years
ago !) a receiver, and not, technically, a thief—
and though there was not the slightest proof
adduced that he had since acted dishonestly—
the usual absurd J.P. fined the publican 40s.
without costs. The finee, who imputed to the
constable the harboring of a personal grudge,
has appealed, and the ridiculous decision will
no doubt be upset with contempt ; but there
is no appeal possible against the outrageous in-
justice done to the man who, long years ago,
committed his first and only offence. This per-
nicious persecution of erst criminals is not only
shameful and cruel, but exceedingly unprofit-
able to the community which pays policemen.
If a man is earning his living honestly, or,
at least, legally, it is simply utter foolishness to
badger him back into crime and make a felon of
him, w Inch is what th is disgraceful action amounts
to. And if the Crown Law authorities and the
heads of police approve of this sort of thing, it
is quite time they were forcibly persuaded that
the business of the ordinary “ trap ” is to pre-
vent crime, not to manufacture criminals with
a view to their subsequent capture and punish-
ment. Surely the police exist for the criminals,
and not the criminals for the police. Further-
more, if a man be a convicted thief, or receiver,
and have served a sentence therefor, it is none
the less libellous to publish the fact to the
world at large ; yet apparently the ordinary
constable, for his personal advantage or to
gratify his spite, is entitled to do so with im-
punity. Which is surely altogether too deadly
a weapon to be placed in the hands of the
policeman and his friends, the fizgigs and
bludgers.
In Melb., recently, two women were, upon the
same day, dealt with—one in the criminal court,
the other in Carlton police court—for irregular
disposal of the undesirable infant. In the
former case the accused, having been convicted
of concealment of birth, was reasonably and
mercifully committed to the care of the Salva-
tion Army instead of to gaol, whereas the other
poor creature (whose infant the defence alleged
to be the resultof drugging and outrage) received
from a Carlton tin-pot Draco the savage
sentence of 6 months hard labor for leaving her
infant, warmly wrapped and with an explana-
tory note, upon the door-step of a house in
which she supposed her sister-in-law was living.
And, even at that, the “ abandonment ” was
not complete, since the unhappy woman could
not bring herself to leave the neighborhood and
was consequently promptly arrested. And
then, though the Wesleyan mission people were
anxious to take charge of the woman, the un-
speakable Carlton magistrates, to the astonish-
ment and disgust of everybody in court,
flattened her out with three times as much gaol
as they’d have given to a hulking “ pushite ”
for nearly battering his old mother to death.
Does the Carlton bench ever get lynched ?

Constitution, was severe on the clause which
provides that members of the Senate are to be
elected by the province voting as one electorate
—surely one of the many Liberal provisions of
the Bill. His argument-in-chief was that on
the Referendum for the Convention delegates in
’9B the Labor-party was hopelessly beaten and
could not secure a representative amongst the
first 10. He did not mention that in Victoria
Labor-leader Trenwith was elected in a most

who coolly nominated 10 candidates and ex-
pected N.S.W. to elect the bunch ! Also,

members.
One of the recommendations of the Royal
Commission on N.S.W. Charities is, that in
future no institution of a semi-public nature
shall receive the Govt, subsidy unless its books
and management are open to inspection by a
Govt, official—probably a surprise to many
taxpayers, who would naturally suppose such a
stipulation a sine qua non. A case has lately
been unearthed where Govt, support (,£3O a
year) has been paid for an inmate in a Sydney
institution (whose object is to relieve temporary
distress) since her admission in 1863 ! The sum
voted to the establishment is claimed and paid
in a lump sum, names and dates not being
furnished. It is quite possible that this old
person has neither kith nor kin in N.S.W., and
no one to inquire whether she is alive or not in
what is practically a living grave. Indeed,
whether she is really alive is only known to
one or two persons, whose main, if not sole,
interest in her lies in the fact that her life is
worth £3O a year to the institution !

“ A Wage-Slave ” to The Bulletin :
You talk of that bone-grinding monopoly, the Melb.
Tram Co. ; what about the Ocean-street (Sydney) cable-
tram service? Standard working time in this is 9 hours
10 min. per day, and, for conductors, the wages BJd. to
9d. per hour ; no overtime being paid until 120 hours per
fortnight have been worked. So that N.S.W. Govt, is not
only not above infringing upon the eight-hour principle,
but has also an extra roller in its mill for the purpose of
grinding out 10 hours per fortnight which it is too dis-
honest to pay for. It is fair enough that conductors’
cash shortages should be deducted from their pay, but no
account is kept of any overplus which a conductor may
hand in. A conductor may, if he wishes, book-up at end
of day his own cash and tickets, and thus set-off his own
surplusage and shortage; but, after 9 hours 10 min. on
the tram without any meal-interval, he is not likely to
care about a quarter-of-an-hour additional work. Be-
sides, if the department caught him pocketing its lawful
perks it might mean gaol. The day’s work of nine hours,
or so, is made up of journeys of 20 minutes ; two minutes
allowed for shunting at either end. As to lunch, rules
say, “ Eat it at rear of trail-car, and if a passenger gets
aboard—put down your food at once and collect his fare.”
If Nature calls—miss a trip, and get fined or sacked, or
do best you can out of the two-minute shunt, and run
fast to make up time—again at risk of fine or sack. No
sanitary convenience is provided for the employes any-
where but at Ocean-street terminus, while, as for the
fine-system, there isn’t any. An may be fined
for running fast, running slow, not running at all,
neglect, over-otfieionsness, not stopping, not starting
sharply: in fact, he does not know when, where and for
how much he may be liable. A conductor gets one day
off a week at the convenience of the department, and
thereby hangs a tale. The department sends the con-
ductors intended for the new electric service over to the
Mosman’s Bay line for training-. The electric department
sends to the cable department stating that on, say,
Wednesday it would be convenient to send a squad of men
for training, and the cable department reciprocates
by granting the squad Wednesday as its day off ;
result —a day’s work for nothing; a day’s riding about on
an electric tram for a change, or in the fitters’ shops
among wire, motors, and grease, and if a man fails to
turn up, explanation is demanded. Again, several men
have been injured at the steep termini by the bufferless
dummies, owing to want of sand on the rails in wet
weather. Reward —half-pay while on sick-list. Buffers
are necessary, anyhow, and would not interfere with the
working of the trams ; and even a Govt, should
not be expected to handle sudden death for a living,
especially as N.S.W. Factory Act compels private bone-
mills to provide safe working for their drudges. The
ticket system of fare-collecting-, again, does not protect
the department against dishonesty on the part of em-
ployes, because the hours and movements of the two
inspectors are well known ; and the tickets are no pro-
tection to the conductors, as a malicious passenger,
desirous of working off a grudge, may substitute an
expired for a current ticket, and have a whole carful of
passengers honestly willing to swear to his having paid
his fare. So that, on the whole, the methods of the local
bone-mill don’t appear to be much less ill-regulated and
tyrannical and dunderheaded than those of the Mel-
bourne one.

One reason for Tasmania’s belatedness in the
matter of Democracy is the press-gag law which
compels any would-be newspaper-proprietor to
find bonds amounting to £l6OO for his good
behavior. Thus it is sought to prevent those
persons who haven’t acquired property from
passing remarks as to how the old nobility of
the Flyspeck acquired their titles and estates.
The ancient law is practically a dead-letter in
most civilised communities, including even
Britain and Australia, but in Tasmania it is
enforced to the uttermost farthing. No more
eloquent witness to the soreness of the spot that
newspapers not established on a property-basis
might irritate could well be heard from than
Fatman’s rabid desire to "penalise a news-
paper-man before he commits any offence. The
man who beats his wife is bound over to keep
the peace, after he beats or threatens to beat
her ; a newspaper-man has to put up £l6OO as
a guarantee that he doesn’t intend to say dis-
respectful things about a dog. According to
the rulers of the Land of Baleful Shadows, a
wife-beater or a bludger is far less dangerous to
society than a newspaper person. After all,
The Bulletin isn’t quite sure that a man who
wants to start a paper in Tas. doesn’t deserve
to be treated as a criminal-lunatic. [And now
comes the news that, after the £l6OO security
has been remorselessly exacted for half-a-
century, it has just been discovered by the
Supreme Court that only £BOO was legally
exigible.]
The ghastly foolishness of an Anglican Church
mission to convert the local Jews made itself
heard in Melbourne lately, when “ missionary ”
L. Abramovitch was appealing for funds to
build a Kerristian night-school and a temporary
shelter for the needy Chosen. A recent article
in the Jewish Herald reads like an uninten-
tional satire on Abramovitch’s maunderings.
The Herald opposes a suggestion that the
Jewish Church should be closed against pro-
selytes who are prepared to marry into the old,
old Faith. Many an intelligent Jew will be
lost to the faith, says the Herald, if he can’t
get the conversion of his Gentile wife duly
recognised :
We yield to no man in our condemnation of mixed
marriages ; we deplore them as one of the greatest
offences against our faith and race ; but when such a
marriage has taken place, or cannot be prevented, let us
by all reasonable means try to retain the Jewish partner
and admit the non-Jewish one if she earnestly desires it.
. . . Instances are not wanting of persons who might
have become creditable Jews, but were turned away
through harsh treatment and unnecessary severity.
The time is surely coming when the Jews will
open a branch religion for the partial Hebrew-
isation of all Christians who can’t believe in the
divinity of the Nazarene. In the natural
course of enlightenment, the world becomes
more Jewish every day, physically and
morally. Its nose grows bigger, it is kinder to
its women, and it is adopting Israel’s
opinions on the subject of physical work. One
hears the impecunious loafer, with his back
against the pillar-box or verandah-post,
lamenting his bad luck in not having been born
a blanky Yiddisher. Well, they didn’t talk
like that at the times of the Crusades.
“ J.S.” on a sanitary matter :
At Redfern Station (Sydney) every morning in the
rabbit-season it is disgusting to see the bundles of rabbits
tossed out on the platform like offal, there to be pawed
and turned over by the hands or feet of the not too clean
and wholesome hawkers. And the usual hawkers’ carts !
The system under which they are hired makes no restric-
tion as to their use, so that when rabbits are “ in ” they
are used for rabbits ; another time for fruit; then for fish;
and perhaps even for bottles, old iron, rags, and garbage
in general. Hardly ever washed out, either, these carts ;
so that all sorts of germs are liable to be disseminated-by
them, especially as many of the rabbits come from
country where there is pleuro, anthrax, or tuberculosis.
Butchers’ carts also, which go to the abattoirs for entrails,
are afterwards sent round with the family steak without
any attempt at purification. Then in the grocer’s cart
are piled higgledy-piggledy kegs of butter, cases of eggs,
sacks of potatoes that have been dragged all over dirty
wharfs and stores, and your butter and general groceries
share the dust and rubbish resulting. The milkman and
the carcase-butcher are compelled to keep their premises
and conveyances clean, and the fish-market is well looked
after. Why should these other abominations not receive
the attention of the Health Board?
“Nicko” to The Bulletin on a subject which,
though of vital importance, could only be dis-
cussed in a medical paper :
The enthusiastic females who rush into print and howl
furiously over an allegedly-unfounded objection to lady
surgeons on the part of a section of the Melb. Women’s
Hospital Committee, appear to possess a profound ignor-
ance of their subject. The objection is a tacit one, of a
kind which no w’oman well put into words, but experi-
ence shows that it is—or should be—fatal. In all the
chief European hospitals, and in several Australian insti-
tutions, stringent rules are in force to obviate the risk of
septic trouble in surgical patients, arising from physio-
logical causes in the female staff of nurses. Listrr,
during his earlier experiments in antiseptics, was for long
at a loss to explain certain septic infections of wounds
which appeared at regular intervals among his patients.
By experiment and check-experiment he solved the pro-
blem, and a simple arrangement of relieving nurses,
at certain intervals and for certain periods, stopped
the mischief. In a midwifery hospital the risk,
for obvious reasons, is greater than in an ordinary surgical
ward, and the records of such institutions show the dan-
ger. In an Australian instance the regularity of the out-
breaks was too convincing to be disregarded, and the
lady-surgeon was hastily got rid of. The subject is
naturally a delicate one for consideration by a mixed
committee, but there is no doubt that, for the safety of
surgical or obstetric cases, a woman-surgeon should be
made to conform to the same rules as the nursing staff.
The risk of infection in her case is far greater, and until
this is done periodic septic outbreaks will oecur. Medical
men are strictly observant of this rule in their private
practice. Therefore, the objection of the committee is a
ver3' valid one, and, by the persistent howling of par-
tisans of the lady-medicos, a few more helpless mothers
are about to be made to run the risk of puerperal fever
as sacrifices to the fetish of the Advanced Woman. Hang
the A.W. ! Ask the next male-medico what he thinks—
after a w’hisky for preference.—P.

Raising the “age of consent” in Maoriland
has raised the work in the Courts. It was to
be expected. Repentance follows hard upon
the heels of passion. Give it a show, and it
exhibits a practical way of putting the guilt on
another’s shoulders. Up to 1894, “the age”
in Maoriland was 14. Then it was raised to
15. And recently—the white-chokered being
now high-up in the land—it has been raised to
16. Mr. Justice Dennistoun remarks from his
Supreme Court eminence, that—
The original law was intelligible. It protected the
ignorant girl. But girls of 16 were perfectly cognisant of
their action. Under the present law, a girl immediately
under the age of 16 might be the actual seducer of a lad,
say 17, and then might bring criminal proceedings
against him, and be able to extort money from him.
They had heard a great deal lately about the protection
of their girls, but it seemed to him that there was con-
siderable necessity for the protection of their boys. It
was an extraordinary thing that a girl could hale a lad
up for a crime of which she was the cause.
Which is good, wholesome talk, and sensible,
and appeals to knowledge and experience. It
is certain, anyway, that before the world is
much older, man’s virtue will be considered as
precious an article as woman’s. And so it is—
since rarity enhances value.
The perennial outcry, “ Australians a dying
race,” has made its reappearance. It is a
charge remarkable for absurdity, as, to begin
with, it presupposes that there is an “ Austra-.
lian race ” old enough for comparison to be
made between a few generations. This is
not the case at all. It is only a small stock of
a particular origin that is so old. The great
mass of the native Australian population is of a
later superimposed stock, of very different for-
mation, diluted with the progeny of undis-
tinctive subsequent arrivals, and, even exclud-
ing the latter element, is still principally in the
first generation. Some fluctuations of type are
sure to follow the settlement of a new country,
and to exhibit both improvement and deterior-
ation for two or three generations, in the pro-
cess of adaptation. There is also bound to be
some relative deterioration following the tran-
sition from a “ new’ ” to a “ settled ” country,
on account of the increase of sedentary and
unhealthy occupations, and the greater cramp-
ing of individual life by social environment.
Taking which things into account, the amounts
of deterioration and otherwise at all computable
from the very short data as yet available go to
show, if they show anything, that a remark-
ably vital race is in course of evolution in Aus-
tralia. But no doubt the first remark made by
ie original descendant of the missing link was
to the effect—“ The race is played out.”

about removing the scandals from its own service.

“ A. L. W.” to The Bulletin about leprosy :
As we are so strict about the isolation of leprosy, a
doubtfully communicable disease, why are consumptives
allowed to move about at will exuding microbes at every
breath, and scattering deadly sputum as they go? As
for leprosy, I have lived for years amongst leprous per-
sons in Fiji, where lepers marry clean-skins, have pro-
geny, live and die unheeded by Govt, or by anybody else.
Neither is any fuss made about the so-called dreadful
disease in any of the other Pacific islands. As a trader
in the Fijis I handled trade and goods generally with
lepers, and I do not know of any white in Fiji becoming
infected in this way. Grossing Vanua Levu once had
occasion to stop at an inland village for two days. My
host and hostess were clean and healthy looking—certainly
no external sign of leprosy, yet their 16-year son,
the sole leper in the village (having been infected for
three years), was literally rotten with it. This boy passed
me food frequently with his unaffected hand, and, for all
I knew, I slept on a mat he may have used. Also
I dressed his affected parts. Also, a sleeping-
mat used by a leper would be readily slept upon
b .V a hale Fijian. Yet the disease is not spreading in the
Fijis. Sometimes it appears in the denizen of an island
whereon not the oldest inhabitant can remember a pre-
vious case—the p dient also never having left his home or
encountered a leper.
Even here, when a case of leprosy is discovered,
it has generally been treated for months as some other
disease before its true nature dawns upon the doctor,
so that if it (leprosy’) were contagious, it would have been
all this time scattering itself broadcast. Also, there are
people in Australia who have for years secretly housed a
family-leper. “ Blood-poisoning, you know ! So very
sad ! ” So the secret is kept between the family and the
doctor until, by some accident, it is discovered, when
doctor feigns previous ignorance, and another family is
shamed and outraged.

The most beautiful thing this paper has met
with for a long time is the demand of the
Newcastle-Wallsend Sun that Newcastle
shall have a great International Exhibition :
Some months ago we advocated the necessity of a New-
castle Exhibition for the year 1900—a fitting close of the
old century and a brilliant opening of the new—to be de-
frayed by Government. At that time we pointed out
only an exhibition on a grand scale can advertise the
great national wealth of the Newcastle District and the
grand opening there exists for sound investment by
capitalists of the world. Our idea is a World’s Fair —
something likely to establish great industrial works on
the reaches of the Hunter, something to make Newcastle
what it should be—the great industrial seat of the
Southern Hemisphere. And, we repeat, until a plan of
this kind is carried out, Newcastle will always remain in
the background. A local show will only have a local
effect : almost every small town in N.S.W. has its little
show. We should look to something higher and nobler
than a country show if we desire the world to take notice
of Newcastle.
Australia chatters even in its sleep about the
“ boundless resources of this great country,”
but the above is a more than usually beautiful
sample of the disease. Newcastle district is
ap ordinary sort of place which produces coal
and vegetables, like many thousand other
places in this world. The coal is black and
the vegetables are green, like other coal and
vegetables. Nobody outside Australia wants
to buy the vegetables, and there are none to
sell, because N.fS. W. has never grown enough
to supply itself. The outside world buys the
coal because it is combustible, and doesn’t much
care where it comes from. It doesn’t “ take
notice” of Newcastle. It is so like poor old
Parkes’s query put to a visiting German :
“I)o they talk much about us in Berlin?”
Parkes was a riotous young man of about
65 then, and lie was also worried because the
world didn’t “take notice.”
Evidently Victoria is to endure a lot of
twaddling details re Taverner in Hingland.
The rotund Minister of Agriculture had no
sooner reached his field of missionary labor than
the cable reported—“On his way to England
Mr. Taverner stayed for a while in Rome. He
had a satisfactory interview with the Italian
Minister for Agriculture, and obtained valuable
information on the treatment of phylloxera, on
sericulture, technical education, and the packing
and grading of lemons for export.” Are the
public deluded by these hollow political tricks ?
Taverner didn’t need to go to Rome for any
hints that he may have gathered from another
Taverner in Italy. It was all obtainable from
books and pamphlets which the Italian consul
in Melbourne can procure by post. The cabled
suggestion that two official heads of agricultural
departments have to put themselves together
in order to pass along “ information ” from one
to t’other, and that farmers, orchardists, and
wine-growers depend upon theTAVERNERSof this
world for wisdom, is calculated to make any
intelligent reader tired.
The Bookfellow this month takes another
leap in size; and the contents are more varied
than previously. There are articles on the French
poet, Baudelaire, and the Australian one, Mary
Footfc, with portraits; a notice cf artist Tom
Roberts, with portrait and reproductions of his
work ; the Quatrain forms the subject of numerous
quotations ; the Brownings’ love-letters and E. J.
Brady’s book of verses are reviewed at some
length; Victor Daley contributes a satire in
verse ; and there are numerous illustrations and
portraits. Prizes are offered for French transla-
tion and an English quatrain ; and altogether the
number is well worth its modest price of 3d., post
free from Bulletin office.
