St. George’s Hall Dante.
Somebody has said somewhere, “There is
more material for a life of Falstaff than for a life
of Shakespeare, though for both there is a lament-
able dearth.” Shakespeare is accountable, per-
haps, for much of this —and the critics for much
more. The Falstaff of the Henry V. cycle is not
the Elizabethan Falstaff written (if rumor be
correct) to please the whimsy of a queen. The
fat, greasy swashbuckler of the elder story is far
different from the almost Quixotish knight—who
is not nearly so degraded as some of his down-
takers—of the piece written to please Elizabeth
Tudor. The play was produced at Her Majesty’s
in Nov. ’9O. Then, the Falstaff was William
Rignold—a great, genial, convincing Falstaff,
whose impersonation carried so great a measure of
conviction that one imagined he was witnessing
Shakespeare’s big, gross, jovial, sunny humorist
himself, as every lover of a most loveable
character could conceive him perhaps, not
one whit less loveable, in the whole circle
of literature, than Don Quixote himself,
and that is saying much. The performance
of brother George is round, rich, and unctuous.
But it is not the common act of nature, so much
as a great intellectual effort. One reads brain in
every line of the last Falstaff’s work, and it is
work pleasingly intellectual.
* * * *
In the old performance, Mr. G. Rignold
played a very capable and satisfactory Ford. This
part is now sustained by Mr. H. W. Diver.
Curiously enough, it is the comedy side of what is
mentally tragic in Iago; but Mr. Diver is in-
finitely more satisfactory as Ford than as
the cunning Ancient of the jealous Moor.
A very capable performance was that of Mr. John-
son Weir (Sir Hugh Evans)—played with broad
humor, but also with shaded delicacy : a part which
might, in less artistic hands, become gross. But,
place aux dames ! Emphatically the Mistress Page
of experienced Watts-Phillips and the Mistress
Ford of Miss Lilian Wheeler gave a nuance of re-
fined distinction to the whole performance. Mr.
Nunn worked hard as Doctor Caius, but his broken
English was the broken English of a Frenchman
whose Gallic confines were those of Stratford-
atte-Bowe. Miss Roxy. Barton was not the Dame
Quickly of our imagining. Mr. S. Grainger’s
Page was eminently respectable—and nothing
more. It’s a long cast, and space is limited ; and
the run we anticipate for this last edition of Fal-
staff will furnish opportunities enough and to spare
for mention of some folk now unavoidably left out.
A feature of the present production is the splendid
scenery painted by Mr. Phil Goatcher and Mr.
Whaite. In stage scenery it is difficult to imagine
anything possibly finer than the splendid scenic
vista of Windsor Park.

The farewell performance to M. Ldon
Caron prior to his departure for Europe took place
at Her Majesty’s Theatre last week. A long pro-
gramme was submitted. The Royal Comic Opera
company appeared in the first act of “lolanthe,”
the second act of the Napoleonic play, “Madame
Sans-Gfine,” was played by the Brough Comedy
company, and M. Caron conducted a concert ala
Julien, comprising the combination of 75 pro-
fessional instrumentalists. Miss Dorothy Yane,

* # *• *
“ Pygmalion and Galatea ” introduced
Julius Knight and Ada Ferrar again to Sydney.
The statuesque proceedings opened to a matinee
house on Saturday afternoon. The mingling of
burlesque and pretty pathos that characterises
this lightly-elad play makes it a touchy little piece
to interpret satisfactorily. Half the Knight-Ferrar
coalition leaned its weight on the pathos. The other
half preferred to support itself on the burlesque.
This scribe was visited by the mixed emotions that
come to the man who finishes up The Geisha with
the Lord’s Prayer. Ada Dewar’s Galatea was a
sweetly silly creature whose appalling beauty was
the only excuse she could offer for her silly
sweetness. She played delicately and carefully,
with the old, old weepy modulations trailing up
and down her voice at accurately timed in-
tervals, and made the artistic success of the
piece. Julius Knight, as Pygmalion, was merely
a gentleman in a chiton of his own design
who humped himself on every possible and
impossible occasion. Oily Deering looked rather
astray in the Greek atmosphere. For all that,
Oily is a droll comedian ; there was a note of
modern vulgarity about his Chrysos which was
probably realistic, but which cast slurs on “the
Glory that was Greece,” and all that sort of
thing. The slur was further emphasized by one
of the largest, most Hibernian-looking ladies ever
seen on the stage, who answered to the name of
Daphne instead of Biddy. Cyril Keightley,
as Leucippe, was just one degree ahead
of other performances of his. Miss Linda
Raymond played Myrine with an agreeably
distinct voice, and a nice, bright little way of her
own; but Emily Hughes, as Pygmalion’s wife,
seemed to be overcome with her efforts to settle
into somebody else’s voice. Only an extra-stagey
speech, such as her purple renunciation of Pyg-
malion, "brought her into touch with her stagey
conception of the part.
• * * *
On Monday, at Her Majesty’s, Mr. Julius
Knight and Miss Ada Ferrar, assisted by talent
vigorous and varied, continued the kindly function
of “ farewelling ” that section of the British race
resident in Australia with a performance, rich
in costume and radiant as to scenery, of
“ The Prisoner of Zenda.” Everything connected
with Anthony Hope’s romantic play is known
to everybody ; but it was none the less enjoyable.
On Wednesday the awful plagiarism perpetrated
by Wilson Barrett on Massinger’s “Virgin
Martyr,” to wit “The Sign of the Cross,” was
successfully achieved ; and to-night, Thursday
(May 4th), the “farewell performance in Sydney ”
of “The Lady of Lyons, which we are kindly in-
formed is “Lord Lytton’s evergreen play,” takes
place.
* * * *
Carton’s admirable comedy of “ Lord and
Lady Algy ” comes to a regrettable end to-morrow
(Friday night). Saturday (May 6) sees the first
performance in Australia of “The Adventure of
of Lady Ursula,” by Anthony Hope, whose
“ Prisoner of Zenda ” has only just been revived
at Her Majesty’s by the Julius Knight-Ada
Ferrar combination. “Lord and Lady” have
been doing something more than good business.
Which is only what it deserves. There are too
few plays of this true comedy type produced in
Sydney.

Peggy Pryde was accorded a right royal
welcome on her re-appearance at the Tivoli. Mr.
J. Bain, an Australian comique, also received dis-
tinguished attention ; and the others were
as entertaining as heretofore. The Keziahs,
Manuel Woodson, “Charlie” Cogill, Wallace
King, et hoc genus omne, as the poet Sayers would
remark, were all wool and a yard or so wide.
* * * *
The Opera House re-opened, after a fort-
night’s silence, with an alleged musioal comedy
called “The Two Scamps.” “The Scamps” were
named respectively Bert and Bob, who were
monotonously troubling or in trouble. A number
of the songs had somewhat of merit, and some of
them were kindlier unspoken of. Miss Minna
Phillips and Mr. T. Edmond Leonard were the
scampy ones. The parody of “Off to Phil-
adelphia ” was excellently rendered by Mr.
Leonard, who brought down the house with his
references to the military poet of Paddington,
Premier Reid, and other prominent warriors, civic
and otherwise. Mr. Fred. Wentworth deserves
mention for his rendering of “The Deathless
Army. ” Mr. J. E. Shepherd, Miss Hilda Fraser,
and Miss Miriam Lewis also did excellent work.
The production was, moreover, adequately staged,
ifc * * *
To gild refined gold, to paint the lily, to
throw a perfume on the violet, to smoothe the ice,
or add another hue unto the rainbow, or with
taper-light to seek the beauteous eye of heaven to
garnish, is wasteful and ridiculous excess ; but the
Anderson - Holloway combination in “Man’s
Enemy ” has essayed this with more than mode-
rate measure of success. The play is not appal-
lingly brilliant from a literary point of view; but
for sumptuousness of scenery and radiance of
limelight it leaves nothing for the most expansive
imagination to claw upon.
* * * #
It is well-nigh impossible to say anything
fresh about Fitzgeralds’ Circus. Beaconsfield, in
a happy moment, remarked that Eclipse was first
and the rest too bad a second to make a note of.
Sufficient for the day is the Circus thereof—but
only when the Fitz. Brothers are doing it. If it
were possible to say anything new about the lion
and the elephant and the bicycle, it should be
done —and the “ bunyip ” should also not be for-
gotten. And let it be enthusiastically recorded
that Cicero once remarked, “I may contrive to do
without the necessaries of life; but give me my
circuses. ”

“The Belle of New York” will have
ceased to be at the Princess’s when these remarks
appear, but “in response to numerous requests ”
The Bulletin herewith expresses Melbourne’s
gratitude to a certain youthful member of the
male chorus who impersonated a warbling cook in
the first scene. The young man under notice was
the cook who stood nearest the footlights on
the o.p. side, and patiently bore the endearments
of two nice parlor-maids. The impassiveness of
that young songster amounted to the best
joke in the show. He gazed out into the
distance whilst the dear charmers wooed him
for all they were worth. They put their respective
heads upon his shoulders, but he observed them
not. The more their bright eyes glanced the
less he troubled to beware. Finally the words
and music gave him the cue to place a couple of
hard, unsympathetic arms behind a couple of
slender waists, whereupon the damsels took joint
action to compel their inflexible “ mash ” to sway
gently with the movement of their bodies. It was
hard work for them, and the writer hopes they
were paid something extra.

The farewell season of the Knight-Ferrar
co. opens on Saturday in Melb., and will extend
over 13 performances and five dramas. Time-table
as follows: “Pygmalion and Galatea,” for three
nights; “Lady of Lyons,” day and night on
Wednesday; “Harbor Lights,” three nights;

“ Sign of the Cross,” to play Marcus and Mercia
off the premises.
* *
Meanwhile “ Woman and Wine ” continue
to demoralise Walter Baker at the Royal, and the
stiffened jockey falls off his educated horse with-
out fail. The naughty, but otherwise irrational,
melodrama is running till further notice with all
its original scenery and effects, except that the
French tenor has set himself to a new tune.
** * *
Every now and then manager Rickards in-
troduces a very big novelty with a medium-sized
advt., leaving the excellence of the show to make
itself famous. Cinquevalli, who came to Melb.
Bijou last Saturday as “the most finished per-
former in the English-speaking world,” is—for all
that one can reasonably suppose to the contrary —
the cleverest juggler of any time or place. If
there was ever another man who could do things
as astonishing as the things this man does, m
the same casual manner, The Bulletin would
like to know where the other fellow was,
and why his name doesn’t live in history.
Cinquevalli’s feats range from the marvellous
to the miraculous; moreover, he is a juggler of
infinite jest. He turns the audience’s gasps into
guffaws with a humorous movement of the head,
arm, or body, and he gives them no time to con-
sider that his performance must be seen more than
once to be fully understood. Sometimes it is
appalling, but in a general way it is a neat and airy
sequence, of surprises. Perhaps the quaintest, if
not the most extraordinary of the Cinquevallian
proceedings is the “ eye-glass ” joke. The juggler
puts on his foot a metal disc, which might
be half-a-crown. He jerks his foot and the half-
crown flies to the place where an eye-glass is
carried, and sticks there as though to the manner
born. He jerks his head and the disc is lodged in
front of the other eye. If Cinquevalli had a glass-
eye he could pick it from the floor with his toes
and shoot it into the vacant socket every time.
That’s the sort of juggler he is. Having seen
Cinquevalli you feel almost prepared to die, for
there is no more jugglery left to live for.
* * *
The pedantic assertion that Friedenthal i s
not a ‘ ‘ great ” pianist, in a loud way of speaking)
seems very characteristic of the musical-critic cult.
“The soundness of his method” is sometimes
open to question, writes a Melbourne authority ;
but he “is beyond all dispute thoroughly com-
petent to give an audience keen enjoyment by his
own unassisted efforts.” What are the methods
essential to “greatness ”? Friedenthal appeals to
the ear. He makes the least possible display of
manual labor in the matter, and the skill and
intelligence of his playing rely solely upon musical
effects for public appreciation. Therefore the
public reckon Friedenthal an exceptionally sound
performer.
** * *
Dante’s magic-supper, and the cremation
of the adorable lady (whose skull was described
as “ skill ”in last issue), and the manufacture of
mystic billiard-balls, &c., are happening seven
times a week at St. George’s Hall to the satisfac-
tion of large audiences. Present writer can’t
quite comprehend what the lady does to deserve
her doom, but seeing that she returns to life With a
brighter smile than ever, the burning incident is of
no consequence, except that it tends to explain
why she can subsequently “ scorch ” on a bicycle
upside-down.

Grattan Riggs is still holding out at the
Alexandra, unless something has dissuaded that
estimable comedian from further exertions, for the
present. Grattan on Saturday made a slow and
difficult appearance as Tracey the Detective, and,
although he warmed-up after a while, in response
to sacrilegious exhortations from the gallery,, his
efforts suggested footsteps on the sands of time
when the evening sun is low.

Melbourne’s annual music boom will get
fairly going on Saturday, when Allan and Co.
start a series of afternoon ballad concerts at the
Town Hall. The co. for the first concert includes
Mrs. Palmer, Miss Ida Osborne, basso Barker,
Armes Beaumont, Brownlow, and two long-lost
singers in James Wood and Miss Christie Fuller.
On the same evening Friedenthal —who is only a
great favorite, and not a “great” pianist, the
critics say —is to give a farewell concert in the
big hall; and on the next Saturday afternoon
Marshall - Hall’s orchestra will take the floor.
This is the season of the year when Melbourne
grows its hair long and talks of upper-registers.
At Poverty Point.
“ Buskin ” : Oh ! “ Why does he (Rignold) stab, after
smothering, Desdemona ? " Now let the locust-swarm
of correspondents who have seen all the tragedians from
Kean to Salvini arise and testify that G.R. is not an
innovator in this respect. And “ The text gives no war-
ranty” for the stabbing, eh? (vide Bulletin 22/4/’99).
No, the original folio does not specifically direct, “knifes
her here,” but, then, Shakespeare usually did leave some-
thing to the reasoning faculties of stage-managers in
futuro. As a guide-post, he gave Othello the lines (after
smothering her), “ Not yet quite dead. . . . I would not
have thee linger in thy pain . . . so, so.” No warranty?
Any amount of it. Desdemona speaks again, twice !
After being only smothered? And the text says, after
her second utterance, “Dies.” Does the merely
“ smothered,” after regaining enough breath to speak a
dozen or so words, die —of smotheration ? Will some
medico kindly step up and orate ? Trust me, Shakespeare
knew better than that. Tragedy demanded that Desde-
mona should die, and poetic conception required that she
should accuse herself of her murder in answer to Emilia’s
question. Therefore, when Will was stage-managing, a
little while before The Bulletin was started, the Othello
first smothered then stabbed. [Thus Othello, so firm, so
constant to the very last, that we cannot readily imagine
a deflection : “Yet I’ll not shed her blood.” Salvini
dragged his Desdemona all over the bedchamber. There
might here be an excess of art; hut in the “So, so!”
why not Fechter’s method, the quick strangular hand of
a strong man ? —Ed. B.]
“ B.” : The announcement about Rignold playing
Falstaff revives recollections of that veritable ideal “ ton
of sack,” his brother William. How will the local histrion
compare with his relative in the part ? Then Rignold
threatens to play D’Aitagnanin “The Three Musketeers.”
He seems impossible 1 The hero of old Dumas was long,
lean, gaunt, nervous, vitriolic, swift of speech—a true
Gascon. Scot Inglis could have played it. Rignold might
manage Porthos, but Brian Dariey would have fitted the
part to a nicety. For Aramis, say Dot Boucicault, petit,
refined, and chockful of s avoir faire. Athos would be
safe in the hands of Alfred Dampier. We haven’t a
woman in Australia capable of playing Miladi. How
would Olga Nethersole fill the part?

Press-circle seats for the Amy Castles concerts at Ben-
digo this week were auctioned, the result being i'l3o tor
179 seats. In the light of these fancy prices it seems an
extraordinary thing that no one should have discovered
the “ wonderful” quality of Miss Amy’s voice during the
last four years when she has competed for various scholar-
ships in Bendigo and Ballarat and lately in Melbourne.
The judges, themselves professors of music, who heard
her, merely remarked, “ A ver3 r good voice.”
Hosea Easton, so well known for his impersonation of
Uncle Tom, can appear on the stage no more. He is
stricken with cancer of the tongue, and has undergone two
terrible operations. Easton was not only a good actor,
but one of the finest banjoists Australia has ever heard.
The poor fellow’s case is emphatically one for a benefit.
Mrs. Watts-Phillips, wife of the famous dramatist and
novelist, died recently in London, aged 67. Miss Roland
Watts-Phillips, the Emilia in Rignold’s production of
“ Othello,” at Sydney Cri., is a daughter of the dramatist,
who, by the way, was the author of “ The Dead Heart,”
and of “As in a Looking-Glass,” the piece in which
Bernard-Beere scored so happily.
“ Blister” : Best bit of character-acting in “ The Belle
of New York” is the Tart in the Torn Jacket, who comes
on with the crowd when the hallelujah lass enters. She
acts and walks the part all the time, and looks like a
“half-tanked” dollymop who has just pushed in from
Little Bourke-street to see what’s up.

A Bulletin man was located next a stern female at
Dante’s Melbourne show one recent night. The stern
female had brought her small, mild hubby out foratreat,
and the little man was sitting next the gangway.
Presently the miracle-worker rushed along, and gathered
two half-crowns from behind his collar. “ You little fool,
you !” hissed his red, indignant missus, “haven’t I told
you scores of times to be more careful with your money ?"
Then Dante began plucking half-crowns out of other
men’s whiskers, and just saved himself from an attack
hotly meditated by the deluded lady.
An anachronism in “Our Guardian Angel,” which
flings it right back to the days of Defoe, is the serious
use of the phrase, “ A true-born Englishman.” But
didn’t Daniel effectually slaughter the value of that ex-
pression when he pointed out that a true-born English-
man was a mongrel of all the races on earth ?
“D.”: No wonder the Broughs felt sore over their
Melbourne season. Was sitting up amongst the gods at
a performance of the “Second Mrs. Tanqueray.” When
the pathos was most intense, some of the Indians on the
top-shelf yelled with amusement. One nautical-looking
idiot, each time that Paula contrasted her present life
with her past (and she strikes a truly pathetic note at
times), got up on his feet and laughed till the tears rolled
down his cheeks.
All the Ned Kelly relics, including a lot never before
shown, were brought forth in Melb. gaol warders’
exhibition at St. Vincent’s Bazaar. First, the real Ned
Kelly armor, complete, weighing over 901bs. Also,
the armor of Dan Kelly and Hart. This was found in
Glenrowan Hotel, and resembles Ned’s, except that the
nail-can helmets are padded inside, whereas his is not.
Byrne’s armor was not shown ; Supt. Hare gave it to
Lady Janet Clarke. A photo, of Ned Kelly’s fine spirited
horse was novel and interesting. Bushranger Morgan’s
hat was shown with a bullet-hole through it.
“Melb.”: The gaol warders’ show clinched my atten-
tion. Some catalogue : A perfect model, one-quarter
scale, of Melb. gallows, with working drop, and a dummy
figure to illustrate the scrag. Deeming’s two-edged knife
—the most murderous-looking, little, thin, worn wretch
ever made, and yet razor-sharp. It has got more murders
on its face than he had on his. This atrocious dagger
was shaped by some one, maybe in Italy, on purpose for
secret homicide. A tempting blood-red rose on a bush in
the Botanical may say “Steal 1” and this says “Kill!”
Then there is Deeming’s tiny, greasy-looking, black-
handled hatchet, a toy of a thing, very old to look at,
and you would say it was only meant for a five-year-old
boy to play with—but for its workmanlike blade, like a
reduction of the headsman’s. The cast of Deeming’s
outspread hands, lying on their faces, made one think
they might fly up to your throat. They recalled to me a
frightful tale by Gdrard de Nerval, where a dead man’s
hand was cut off and ran away like a tarantula.
Wanted —Want.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Immediately the date was fixed for the referendum, the
following cablegram was dispatched to London by N.S.W.
Colonists’ Anti-Convention Bill League to Mr. Want, the
ex-Attorney General: —
“To Want, c.o. Agent-General, London.—Referendum
in eight weeks. Can you take., part. Urgently desire
your assistance. Eagar. Reply paid.”
The reply was as follows :
“ London, 29/4/99. Eagar, Sydney.—Cannot arrive in
time protest or refuse (V refute) Bill. Resigned directly
saw Hansard. Bill getting worse. Justified my mistrust
other colonies. Means death-warrant Sydney. Give
publicity.”
“We want you, Jack ! Come back, come back !
We want you mighty badly ;
Our cause is queer, our prospects black —
We need a leader sadly.
Our friends, we fear, are very few ;
Our funds, alas ! are meagre ;
For hope forlorn we turn to you ;
Come quick ! We’re waiting—Eagar ! ”
“ I’ll come ! I’ll come—to burst the drum !
So let the. muskets rattle !
I’m with you, boys ! I’ll come, I’ll come—
After you've lost the battle.
The Bill, I’ll swear, is most unfair ;
Get in the funds, and spend ’em ;
Keep up your hearts ! For I’ll be there —
After the Referendum.
‘ ‘ I fear’d awhile, when on the Nile,
My bogeys all were answer’d ;
But pluck’d up courage (I should smile)
Directly I saw Hansard.
Bill has my curse. It’s getting worse—
I can’t explain exactly ;
But wolves are wolves, and Jack’s perverse—
And that’s my case compactly.
“Now, see how just was my mistrust
Of folk beyond the Murray ;
Concession only proves their lust
To loot us in a hurry.
Who could foresee that they’d agree
To all we ask’d- or nearly ;
Our best-laid plans they sent “agley ”
It shakes one’s faith severely.
“ Sydney’s death-warrant has been sign’d !
Might save her —if I hurried.
To start at once I’ve half a mind—
But why should / be worried ?
Sorry can’t come to funeral,
But please express my sorrow;
Bid comrades all go bear the pall,
And I’ll be back— to-morrow .”
GAMA.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The National Bank of Australasia publishes a
balance-sheet which certainly doesn’t look violently pro-
gressive. The profits for the half-year ended 31st March,
1899, are £12,324—a heavy drop as compared with the
previous half-year, and the worst showing since 1896.
Recent returns compare as follows :
March, 1894 .. £31,312 March, 1897 .. £14,712
September, 1894 .. 25,231 September, 1897 .. 18,275
March, 1895 .. 15,126 March, 1898 .. 15,471
September, 1895 .. 13,763 September, 1898 .. 17,602
March, 1896 .. 10,523 March, 1899 .. 12,324
September, 1896 .. 11,706
The usual 5 per cent, is paid to preference shareholders,
which absorbs £7645, and the remaining £4679, with pre-
vious balance, raises the amount at credit of profit and
loss to £52,117. But altogether the circumstances, unless
the National can show some special reasons for them, are
unaccountable. Also, they certainly look lament-
able. The net earnings are only equal to £1 4s. 4d.
per cent, per annum on the shareholders’ funds (capital
£1,976,880, and reserve, £50,000), which is so miserably
small as to strongly suggest that many of the assets are
yielding a very small return and that there is need for a
heavy writing down—or else that the National is reserv-
ing a large part of its earnings and applying them to
providing for losses and depreciations which the share-
holders have not heard of. At all events this very solemn
fact stares the shareholders in the face. The National
Bank holds £837,920 of Government, municipal and other
stocks. Butting them all down at a minimum interest of
3 per cent, (and no municipal and very few Government
stocks yield so little as that) the half-year’s interest
received would be £12,568. And as the bank professes to
earn only £12,324, the inference is that the hanking busi-
ness itself hasn’t been doing any too well. The National
has been steadily paying off its burdensome 4J4 per
cent, reconstructed deposits (only i 825,000 remain
out of the original £6,844,000). Each payment made a
great saving' in interest, but instead of this making the
profits larger they are smaller now than on any occasion
since the ’93 smash, the 1896 returns only excepted. If
the profits shown are all the National’s earnings, they
certainly seem to suggest the advisability of writing the
capital down largely. If they are not all the earnings
and the remainder is being applied to write down the
assets, it would be better to avoid misconception and keep
up the credit of the institution by stating the fact.
So far as concerns the depositors, the National’s
position is very strong. Cash and liquid securities
(£2,808,038) are equal to over 37 per cent, of all liabilities
to the public. In spite of large repayments of recon-
structed deposits, the total deposits have increased during
the half-year by nearly half-a-million. The liquid assets
have increased during the half-year by £300,000. But
unless there is some good reason, apart from anything
that appears in the statement of accounts, for the small
profits the National looks at present a remarkably unpro-
mising spec, for the ordinary shareholder. After the
preference shareholder’s div. is provided for, the balance
only amounts to about 11s. 3d. per cent, per annum on the
ordinary capital.
* * * *
The report of the Colonial Bank (Melbourne)
for the half-year to 31st March, 1899, shows a most
remarkable improvement. Some recent returns are as
follows
For the period during which the Colonial paid
4J per cent, on reconstructed deposits.
September, 1893 .. Profit £5,966
March, 1894 .. ~ 9,853
September, 1894 .. ~ 2,247
March, 1895 .. Loss 4,694
For the period during which the Colonial paid 2|
per cent, on reconstructed deposits. Profits :
September, 1895 .. £7BO I March, 1897 .. £3,043
March, 1896 .. 2,564 September, 1897 .. 3,312
September, 1896 .. 2,004 | March, 1898 .. 3,898
For the period during which the Colonial paid 3
per cent, on its reconstructed deposits. Profits :
September, 1898 .. £853 | March, 1899 .. £5,601
The writing-off of lost capital has now been formally
authorised, and the alteration will appear in next bal-
ance-sheet. After allowing for the writing-off, the paid-
up capital stands at £366,882 (preference £304,044, and
ordinary £62,838), and the profits equal £3 Is. Id. per
cent, per annum thereon, which is well up to the average
and seems to suggest that the Colonial has now provided
for all losses and put its affairs on a sound basis. At the
same time, the writing-off has left the Colonial a very
small institution indeed compared to what it used to be.
A 3 per cent, dividend is paid to preference
shareholders (the first they have received since the 5 per
cent, of March, 1894), which absorbs £4561, leaving a pro-
fit balance of only £lO4O. Considering that the Colonial
has now no vestige of a reserve-fund, that the entire
shareholders’ funds are below £367,000, that this is the
first time in five years that the institution has earned
enough to pay even 3 per cent, on preference capital,
and that it is too soon yet to be certain that the very sud-
den improvement of the present half-year will be main-
tained, the declaration of a dividend was supreme
foolishness. The Colonial should have put : £5OOO
to reserve, and it would he time enough to start
paying 2£ per cent, dividends (not more) next half
if things keep up. The business in general is improving
greatly : note circulation has gone up during the last six
months ; new private deposits have increased by £228,000
(from £650,000 to £878,000) ; Government deposits have
increased by over £200,000; cash and liquid securities
have increased by £361,000 and now equal about 28 per
cent, on liabilities to the public ; and reconstructed de-
posits have been reduced by £114,000 and now stand at
only £1,152,823. In fact, everything looks well, except
the frantic haste to resume dividends instead of
strengthening the bank’s p osition, which haste looks very
bad indeed.
» * • *
The steady improvement in the affairs of the
little Royal Bank, the one Melbourne bank which went
triumphantly through the smash of ’93, still continues.
The net earnings for the six months, which closed on 31st
March, are £4314, which compares with some previous
profits as follows :
March, 1893 .. £2510 Stq fcember, 1896 .. £2044
September, 1893 .. 1901 March,. 1897 .. 3398
March, 1894 .. 3258 September, 1897 .. 3625
September, 1894 .. 2875 March, 1898 .. 3864
March, 1895 .. 2136 September, 1898 .. 4088
September, 1895 .. 2038 March, 1899 .. 4314
March, 1896" .. 2099
Profits equal £5 I,ls. 4d. per cent, per annum on share-
holders’ funds (capital £150,000, reserve-fund £5000),
which is considerably above the average bank-earnings
nowadays, and shows the Royal as one of the best profit-
earning institutions in Australia. The usual dividend of 3
per cent, absorbs £2250, and the remaining £2064 raise the
accumulated profits to £BO4l. Of this, £soooare applied to
start a new reserve-fund, and £3041 are carried forward.
The action of the Royal Bank management in keeping its
dividend down to 3 per cent., even while earning over SJ,
Cannot be too highly commended, and the longer it con-
tinues in this course the sooner will the Royal recover its
boom-time losses and get back to its old position. The
deposits show an increase from 1280,000 last half to
£321,000. Cash and liquid securities (£112,124) show an
increase of £26,000 and equal over 30 per cent, on all
liabilities to the public. And the Royal has no recon-
structed deposits or preference capital or other such
worries and obstructions to hamper its operations, which
is no small advantage.
Now that the Royal has again come into pos-
session of a reserve-fund there is an opportunity to
attempt an innovation which might do a lot of good. The
reserve should he separately invested in Government
securities (the Royal has no holding of Government
securities now, which is a distinctly weak point in its
defences), and it should be provided that the interest de-
rived therefrom shall not go into the general profits, but
shall be added to the reserve and similarly invested
until the fund reaches, say, £500,000. In other words,
the fund should be left to increase automatically by com-
pound interest. It might be assisted out of surplus profits
to any extent the management thought advisable, but until
it reaches a really solid figure no management should be
allowed to annex the interest derived from it for
dividend-paying purposes. With good investments the
slow grind of compound interest is the next surest thing
to the grinding of the mills of God—and very often it
happens that compound interest is the more rapid
grinder of the two.
* » * *

which shows signs of improvement this half-year. Ac-
cording to its rather dilatory balance-sheet for the half-
year ended 10th October, 1898, the net profits are t'54,148
(of this £5OOO goes to premises account). These re-
sults compare with some previous ones in I his fashion :
October, 1892 .. £80,346 April, 1896 .. £40,568
April, 1893 .. 60,742 October, 1896 .. 40,400

The late half-year is therefore the best which the ’Asia
has experienced since October, 1893, the profits equaling
£4 10s. 3d. per cent, per annum on shareholders’ funds
(capital £1,600,000, reserve-fund £800,000). A 6 per
cent, dividend is declared (' 48,000), £5OOO are written
off premises, leaving £ll4B to carry forward; this
is the second 6 per cent. div. which the ’Asia
shareholders have received after a long pilgrimage
through the disappointing wildernesses of 5 per cent.
The business looks as solid and prosperous as usual.
Total liabilities to the public are £15,270,000, and the
reserve of cash and liquid securities reaches the immense
figure of £4,800,000. Altogether, if the proprietors are
not fully satisfied, they ought to be.
* * * •
“ Thorold ” : Sydney P.O. Savings Bank gradu-
ally settles down to utter uselessness to its proper clien-
tele. It is open daily from 10 a.m. to 3 p.m. (Saturdays,
10 till noon), which hours are quite prohibitive to the
ordinary toiler, although he is allowed two hours on
Saturday nights, during which he may deposit money,
but cannot withdraw it. If he wants to withdraw from
the bank he is, seemingly, to take a day off for the pur-
pose. Govt, will not cash cheques of any kind, even at
the G.P.0., so that the man who has a crossed cheque is
put to trouble, and sometimes expense, to cash it with
some private firm. And when the writer wished to
deposit money the other Saturday evening, the suburban
official refused to take a P.O. order because the books had
been locked away in the safe during the afternoon.
* * * *
The naval coal question has become acute in
Melbourne. Admiral Pearson wants local merchants to
let their contracts stand in war-time ; but merchants
justly fear blockade, in which case they might have to
carry coal overland, which would make it cost £5 a ton.
* * * *
Falkners, Pambula (N.S.W.), has put up a
deposit for the purchase of the Mt. Gahan property, and
the local wire-hummer goes on to say that the Hidden
Treasure tributors are opening up a recent discovery,
“which promises to be very rich, if it continues.”
Nature’s hidden treasures generally do.
* * * *
The Sulphide Corporation’s success with the
Central now that it has dropped the Ashcroft experi-
ments has spurred another English co. to negotiate for
the North and Junction North ; the Junction is also in-
cluded, but the Camden Syndicate has too many teeth in
that—unless, of course, the Consolidated Mines Selection
Co., which is making the negotiations, is the Camden
Syndicate. Elaborate surveys and inspections have
already been made. If Australian mining co.’s can’t in-
dividually treat their zinc products at a profit, why don’t
they combine ? Others can do it ; there is pretty good
ground for the statement that Block 10’s zincs alone yield
the buyers something over £70,000.
* * * *
Cow Flat, N.S.W. At last something definite
seems to have happened this old-time mine. Cabled that
it has passed into the hands of the Colonial Copper Cor-
poration ; capital, £125,000 ; of which £60,000 is to be
issued in shares. Writer wishes it luck, and hopes the
English shareholders will never have reason to think of
themselves as Bull flats. Cow Flat was a great mine
some 20 years ago when Lewis Lloyd, the owner of Bur-
raga copper-mine, made big money out of it. It may be a
great mine again if it has great luck. It closed down some
15 years ago, since which absolutely nothing has been
done on it. Writer passed by it some weeks ago and
found some goats trying to feed on it; not a man any-
where. There are, of course, all sorts of stories as to
rich bodies of ore left in the old workings. The old
warrior who worked it last was asked, some time ago, if
it were true that he had left Bft. of rich copper-ore. He
simply wanted to know if he looked like a man of that
kind. Anyhow, somebody has to be congratulated on the
float. The management of other mines in the district—
notably, Mount Apsley—should be called upon to explain
why they are sinking while others are floating around
them.
* * * *
At Moonta (S.A.) mines 50 tons of vein stuff
have to be raised to produce one ton of copper, and this,
too, from a shaft 2400 ft. deep. At present 2000 tons of
dirt are raised weekly, the ore-product (averaging 20 per
cent, of copper) being 250 tons. The Wallaroo mines,
where the deepest shaft is 1650 ft., produce the same
amount of copper ore each week. About 1800 men find
employment on the amalgamated properties.
The Eveleen, the Northern Territory silver
show, still gives work for the lawyers—much more than
for miners. Registered in Sydney, a winding-up order
was made, and heavy calls struck. S.A. shareholders,
who kick against the expense of liquidation, refused to
pay. The N.S.W. officers’ order could not, it was argued,
be enforced in S.A., because there was no machinery for
serving notice. But, after long argument, the S.A.
Court has decided, in a test-case, that the order as to
calls was not judicial but administrative, and that, having
made themselves liable by taking shares, the shareholders
were compellable to pay the calls.
* * • *
No wonder the price of copper started upwards
three months ago, until it has reached within sight of £BO
a ton, in view of the facts that have come to light within
the last few days, but the incubation of which must have
commenced about the time the metal began to move. A
couple of months ago, present writer heard in a letter
from America of the gigantic operations of the Standard
Oil Go., of America, in the scrip of certain big copper-
mines of that country, more particularly in that of the
Calumet and Hecla. It was then given out that this huge
organisation, which controls the world’s petroleum supply,
contemplated taking a hand in the world’s copper busi-
ness, compared to which the transactions of the French
Copper Syndicate would be found insignificant—that its
policy was to buy up copper-mining shares, and thus con-
trol productions instead of contracting for the outputs
themselves, to which the collapse of the syndicate was
largely due. Now cabled that a colossal ring, calling
itself the “ Amalgamated Copper C 0.,” has been formed
in New Jersey, controlled by the Standard Oil Trust.
Present capital, 74,000,000 dol., which it is proposed to
increase to 400,000,000 dol. Copper is not likely to go
down for a while under these circumstances. Some
six weeks ago the Calumet and Heela mine paid the
biggest cash div. ever distributed. at one time by one
mine—namely, 4,000,000d01., making 7,000,000d01. distri-
buted during the year.
Obvious that it takes a lot to start a copper-
boom, or we should have had one throughout Australasia
months ago. Signs of one coming appear pretty fre-
quently, but they seem to collapse as suddenly as they
begin. It is marvellous, too, how wonderfully rich the
new copper shows are—on paper. Nearly all reports and
prospectuses give assays of from 20 to 50 percent, copper,
and, of course, there is plenty of it. Everything below
10 per cent, is regarded as rubbish, or mullock—quite un-
mindful of the fact that Mount Lyell and Cobar, the
two biggest and steadiest producers of Australasia, are 4
percent, mines and enormous div. -payers at that; that
Lake George appears to be making a substantial monthly
profit with under 2 per cent, copper ore, containing little
gold and silver ; that the lodes in the Moonta mine are
small and worked at a big profit with copper contents
only under 3 per cent.; that Burraga, probably the rich-
est long-worked producing mine in Australia, is under 10
per cent. Yet these delusions in the way of “gilded-pill ”
prospectuses continue to come out, and fools find food
for enthusiasm in them. There is not a copper-mine in
the world where rich copper samples could not be ob-
tained for assay purposes, and there is a mighty differ-
ence between assaying a copper-mine for the purpose of
floating it, and working one in such a way as to pay even
miners’ wages.

It isn’t “ nice ” to find a Mines Minister
(Bulletin isn’t talking of N.S.W.) borrowing large sums
at interest from a big mine-owner, who has occasion to
make frequent application for suspension of labor-condi-
tions, and so on. It places the Minister in a false position
—and it would be far better if he borrowed from some-
bodv else.

What does it matter if a generation ago some
scientist did say that Bendigo wouldn’t give any-
thing payable below 400 ft. ? The dictum used, locally, to
be attributed to Murchison, not M‘Coy ; but, whether it
was the one’s or the other’s or neither’s, it was probably
true enough as things were then. Compressed air, im-
proved pumps, light steel ropes, boring machines, “ high”
explosives—these and a few other things, the product of
the last 20 years, have made all the difference. And in 20
years more? * *
“ Mount Mercer, b. Is., s. Is. 6d.” raises the
question as to whether the Vic. Minister of Mines hadn’t
better refrain from beating the drum at “ first-crush ”
luncheons. Shares were quoted at 40s. or more when
Minister Foster poured his benediction on the first crush
and assured the company and newspaper reporters that
he felt as proud of the Mercer success “ as though he were
a shareholder.” A drop from 40s. to a bare “ bob” within
three months is rather rough on any investors who put
their faith in Foster’s pride. Foster must curb his pride.
Not very long ago he was proud, he said, at_ being asked
to lead a deputation of Mount Deddickskites in their
brazen demand for a railway to certain alleged mines.
With the repulse of that deputation by Premier Turner,
the last spark of vitality in the Deddick business flickered
out. The mountain remains where it was, but the mines
have more or less vanished.
Vic. Govt. Metallurgist Jenkins, who was lately-
dragged up to Mt. Deddick by the eternal Begelhole, ha?
published a report which, if placed side by side with the
boomsome “ reports from the field’’ of 18 months ago,
might suggest to the Vic. Govt, the advisability of blush-
ing. In the light of Minister Foster’s official barracking
for a Deddick railway, and Minister Peacock’s legal
management of Deddick properties, the Gov. metallur-
gist’s report has a ghastly appearance. Says Jenkins :
“ Out of a large number of specimens collected by me on
the field not one contained more than the merest trace of
gold ; some were quite barren, and whilst there was not
enough lead in the quartz lodes to be remunerative,
silver was only present to the extent of an ounce or two
per ton, and copper, where present, is in very small
quantity indeed. One impression remains after going
over the field. Quite apart from the expenditure of
money in leqitimately proving the barrenness of reefs, one
has the impression of there having been a great expendi-
ture of money in unskilful and sometimes quite needless
work, doomed from the outset to failure.”

Mt. Morgan : 135th div., 7d. ; total to date,
£5 os. sd. per share—£s,o2o,ooo. Where is there such
another gold-mine? Shares worth about £5 Bs., a
market-value a little more than that paid in divs. When
the mine had not one-third the record the shares were
three times the present price. Mine now worked purely
as an investment, perhaps more conservatively than any
other gold-show in the world. Many years’ work in sight.
It would not be difficult for the directors to double yields
and divs., but how long would it last ? And they are not
built that way.

• * * *
Bayley’s Great Oversight is a badly-named mine
in Coolgardie. Meeting last week in Melb., when the
only consolation shareholders received was the statement
that the management of Bayley’s were determined that
no information concerning a recent find in that mine
should be conveyed to the directors of the Oversight, and
employes had been threatened with instant dismissal if
they exchanged any words with the Oversight men. No
doubt even this yabber went down, and induced a few
people, who admit that they have been living on hope for
years, to put up a long-delayed 2d. call. It will be a
great oversight if others are not made.
This year’s Victoria Cross for nerve ought to go
to the vendors of the Balia Balia copper-mines, a Westra-
lian proposition which was plumped before Bull during
the recent copper excitement. The vendors wanted
£200,000 in cash and shares (£25,000 in cash certain), and
asked for another £50,000 to be subscribed. They owned
up that if Bull “ parted ” they would do pretty well out of
the deal; but beyond that they gave no very cogent
reasons why he should do anything Of the sort.

J. Bull has been tempted with still another
Westralian jarrah concession ; for a trifle of £25,000 in
cash, £85,000 in paid-up shares, and £5OOO as the direc-
tors please, he has got hold of eight square miles of Quin-
dalup jarrah country (half the 28 years’ lease of which, by
the way, has expired), Yelverton’s mill, and some other
Bunbury options. The venture was apparently under-
written ; capital, £175,000. This makes the tenth Wes-
tralian wood co. floated in London since the Millars'
Karri Co., a £350,000 affair, swam off in May, ’97.

Wired from Greenbushes (Westralian) tinfield
that there is room for 3000 men there. Yet, when there
were less than 200 on the ground, all the country any-
where near the old workings (i.e., anywhere near the
eyes) was taken. It is just as well to remember that
Greenbushes has been well known for 12 years known to
miners, scientific men, and boonisters. During the
Euriowie boom of eight or ten years ago several Broken
Hillionaires inspected it, and passed. The presumption
regarding every tin discovery is that it won’t pay ; yet
few “ shows ” are more alluring than a tin lode.
Lake View Consols, Kalgoorlie. About to
despatch the largest and richest consignment of ore ever
received in one parcel by any smelting works in Austral-
asia, consisting of a thousand tons of sulphide ore,
averaging from 25 to 30oz. per ton. Estimated gross
value, £120,000. Dapto works (N.S.W.) are the successful
tenderers for the parcel, arrangements for which were
made six weeks ago by a Lake View representative of the
co. who visited the different custom smelting-works.
Some tall yarns have been wired as to the width and
length of the lode, from which the 1000 tons were taken
as an average. No doubt it was the accumulation of
selection extending over five or six months when a rich
bonanza was struck in the 300 ft. lode, and it has been
considered better to send such rich stuff away than to let
it go through the co.’s own sulphide works.

Peak Hill, West Australia, Wired that the
jury in the alluvial case were discharged, failing to agree
after being locked up 16 hours. Both sides making a big
fight in this matter. Whispered that holders who got
out at much less than present prices a<re now fighting on
the alluvial side. Truly a nice kettle of fish, for which
the indefiniteness of Westralian mining law is respon-
sible. Shares in London £B, representing a value
of £2,000,000. Last crushing, 1800 tons, 54000 z. This
reads remarkably well when given without details,
but writer can find no record of any previous
clean -up since the beginning of Feb., and, taking
the record for the last 12 months, this yield W'ould
represent about the average for the time, namely, pearly
three months. During Feb. and some of March they had
the old ten-head battery running; since then the
new 20. Even if the ten closed down after the
20 started, the tonnage is altogether too small
for so many heads running on unusually soft
stpff, the gold in which is so coarse as not to necessitate
fine crushing ; and where all the conditions are favorable,
certainly, in the time, the tonnage should be double.
Even if the co. can maintain such a gold production, what
interest will it pay on two millions of money? Some
mystery in this market somewhere.
Kalgoorlie Mine Managers’ Association has a
committee now at work drafting a scale of wages. It is
laboriously explained on the spot that it is not proposed
to “ cut ” wages —only to get over the trouble experienced
by some managers who are asked for higher pay for Sun-
day and special work on the ground that certain other
bosses pay this higher rate. Boiled down, then, the
present movement seems to he one to ascertain the lowest
rates paid and to scale them as the standard.

From London: In Westralians, several im-
potent attempts made to take advantage of the overflow
from South Africa. Great Boulders still being talked
into better prices on the reported success of the Kone-
man process (the failure of which has since given the
directors the pip). Golden Horseshoe goes merrily on,
but those speculators who are regretting that they were
not in the movement may condole themselves with the
reflection that, if the public had been in, the price would
never have risen as it has done.
N. Territory men lost no time after the TJ.S.
occupation of the Philippines to prospect the islands as a
stock market. The experienced W. Laurie, who went
over, is apparently rather doubtful, but has by now sent
a trial shipment of horses to Manila. As for cattle, for
which a new market is badly wanted, they will have to
wait till things settle.

The long-ton business is practised everywhere,
perhaps nowhere more so than in Westralia. “Now,”
said the managing-director, “ I want you to crush 100
tons.” “ But,” said the boss, “ there are 300 tons here.”
“ Never you mind ; make it 100 tons, and don’t leave a
barrowful behind.” “ And what did you do ? ” “ Well I
gave him long tons and very short ozs., and I panned off a
lot of the scrip as we)l meanwhile.
* * * *
The Austral Otis Engineering Go. Ltd., South
Melb., has completed and delivered a very fine set of
winding engines for the Oobar Gold Mines, N.S.W., capable
of hauling a load of two tons from 1500 ft. at a speed of
1000 ft. per minute. The engines are a pair of 14" cylin-
ders, geared to 7ft. loose drum, and are fitted with link
motion reversing gear, vertical indicators, and powerful
brake gear. The pair of engines are set to one side of the
drums, and all the driving levers are brought behind the
drains. The co. has also just delivered a fine set of
pumping plant for the Paddy Lackey mine for taking ,the
shaft down to 1000 ft.

The smuggling of 30,000 sheep over the
Murray at Goo ram ad da hy the manager for
David Mitchell (Melba’s canny dad), throws a
significant light on the extent of the border
evasions. If the removal of a slip-panel near a
Customs station can account for 30,000 head from
one run, what numbers must be smuggled into
Vic. yearly by other means ! The cost of pontoon
bridges would be nothing to the capitalist who
wished to get a few thousand sheep over f.o.d.
There is nothing surprising in Glassey
being deposed from the position of leader of
Queensland Labor-party. Though he founded it,
was the first Labor member, and probably stood
more abuse than the average man could stand, the
party has all along felt that a leader had yet to
come. Besides, Glassey has grown too moderate
for the ideas of the A.L.F., but probably this
development will pave the way for a seat in the
Cabinet as the representative of that department
in which he used to be employed as a letter-
carrier. Dawson, of Charters Towers, is.to be the
new hand at the bellows.

Maoriland has the lowest birth-rate and
far the lowest death-rate in all the Seven Pro-
vinces, and its birth-rate, like that of all the other
provinces, is decreasing steadily. In 1882 there
were 37 births for every 1000 inhabitants ; in ’9B
there were only 25. Marriages keep increasing all
the time, but where the average used, in ’B2, to be
nearly six children to every marriage, it is now
less than four. Maoriland in general is growing
alarmed over this state of things, and wonders if
the population will stop increasing through people
getting out of the habit of being born, and then
begin to diminish, and finally, if the M.L. nation
will miss its destiny almost before it got started.
And the country is full of people with theories
about the cause—the favorite ones being godless-
ness, drink, want of respect for “our betters,”
socialistic legislation, and too much knowledge
how to dodge the small baby-encumbrance. Yet,
after all, there is nothing so very difficult about
the problem. Twenty years ago Maoriland was
still peopled mostly by the new immigrants—men
and women in or near the prime of life —and these
are the kind of people who are in the habit of hav-
ing children. Now a lot of these people have grown
old, and as Maoriland is so healthy that people
hardly ever die there, they simply stay round and
remain old. These old iniquities have gone out of
the parent industry for various reasons. Maori-
land has the smallest birth-rate of all the provinces
because it is so healthy, and has the smallest death-
rate—it simply contains a larger proportion of
people who are too old to have children than any
other province.
That is part of the matter, anyhow, but, in addi-
tion, the baby-encumbrance grows more unpopular
as civilisation advances. In very primitive com-
munities almost the only amusement is getting
married, therefore people get married young. And
THE NEXT ISSUE.
“ Remittance men, look out for next issue. Something
important."— S.M. HERALD adit.
where there is little expense in the way of educa-
tion, and nobody objects to sleeping 14 in a
room, and it doesn’t matter much whether
the baby has anything on or not, almost
the only requisite is sufficient floor-space for the
new infant to crawl on, and a dust-hole in the
back-yard for him to wallow in, and a eat whose
tail he can drag. In a really primitive community
it doesn’t matter much if there are two children
or 14, so long as the boiled potatoes hold out and
the cat’s tail doesn’t come right off. But a com-
munity that has expensive prejudices about wash-
ing and dressing and educating the offspring, and
having his hair singed, has to limit the supply.
There is no visible remedy for it. The only reason
why the primitive races which let the naked
family crawl economically under the table don’t
overrun the earth is that they die fast in addition
to being born fast.

The M.L. Chief Justice is up-to-date. The
other day Sophie Knigge, elderly wife, sued for
divorce from her old husband on grounds of
cruelty and adultery. The Chief granted the de-
cree, but ordered Sophie to contribute ss. per week
towards the old man’s support. ' Sophie has
means ; old man naps stones for half-a-crown a
day. So the C. J. levelled things up a bit. But
as to the law of it ? Isn’t he a bit ahead of his
team ?
* » • •
“It is well known to those who have to
deal with the Sydney poor ” (writes a Bulletin
correspondent) “ that the needy sick stealthily
refuse, whenever they can, to accept admission to
the privately-managed subsidised hospitals where
they are nominally under the care of “ honorary ”
surgeons or physicians, but really of “ resident
medical officers ” newly-graduated. These people
all want to go to the state-managed and state-
supported Coast hospital, where there are no
“ honorary ” doctors, and those who are there,
having no private practices to attend to, give their
whole time and best skill to the hospital.
* * * *
S.A.’s intensely serious and self-confident
new Governor evidently runs to oratory, for he has
seized every opportunity since his arrival. His
speeches, unlike poor Buxton’s, have good literary
quality and are commendably short. The oratori-
cal fever extends to Lady Tennyson, who can
talk “ with the best of them,” and has quite cap-
tured her own sex with her unaffected manner on
public occasions.
The Buxton regime had brought vice-regality to
freezing-point, so that it needed but little guber-
natorial warmth on the part of the Tennysons to
cause the mercury to ascend. Gov. Buxton hardly
ever remembered a face ora name, and so mortally
offended heaps of people who considered them-
selves important.

* * *
The new Maoriland divorce
law removes one weird anomaly of
the old statute. Formerly if a
M.L. husband deserted his wife
and left the province the courts
held that the divorce law didn’t
apply to her ; she had no domicile
in the country even if she had been
born and had spent her whole life
there. The old man took her domi-
cile with him, and if he went to
England or the U.S. or Prussia she
could only follow him and apply in
the courts there. Under the old law
a M.L. husband could take his
wife’s legal rights with him all over
the earth and keep them in-
definitely. It was an ass of a law,
and the shadow of its long ears has
cast a gloom over the M.L. statute
book for many years.

“G.P.S.” : Re that prison-
chaplain who gave to France’s dying
President the last offices of the
Church. I was at Menzies (W.A.),
and present at the death of an un-
fortunate prospector. “Mick,” he
said, “for God’s sake, bring me a
priest.” There was none within 100
miles, but a man known as “ Mack ”
heard of the request, and walked up
to the hut. “I am the anointed of
God,” he said ; “ I am silenced, but my sacred
office never leaves me until I die. I will give you
the last consolations of the church.” Hedid, and
comforted the dying man and his mate.

Mr. Finnerty, chairman of Westralian gold-
fields Quarter Sessions, doesn’t even always make
a pretence of charging the jury. The other day,
when the Mayor of Kanowna and a few others
were charged with sooling two men on to a fatal
fight (at any rate, one of the pair died just after
the fight), it was simply, “ Gentlemen, consider
your verdict,” when counsel had completed their
addresses. In other words, the prosecution was
left vvith the last say. Surely it is about time the
goldfields had a Judge, and not a police magistrate,
to take the Quarter Sessions work.
Gov. Brassey has been responsible for
some more muffled merriment, this time at the
annual meeting of Melb. Shakespeare Society.
Newspaper reports of H. Ex.’s speeches gave no
suggestion of the humor that in them lies. As an
official joke His Excellency may have been equalled
in another province, but never surpassed.

The defence of Fox in the Dalkeith
(W. A.) murder case was made remarkable by the
aggressive attitude of accused’s counsel towards the
Crown case. He not only showed that Fox of all
ethers was innocent, but he suggested clearly
enough that one of the witnesses for the Crown
was in a predicament that even police eyes in
G-roperland could hardly fail to observe.

In N.Q., a woman who was housekeeping
for a widower had three children by him. Said
widower, who had four children by a deceased
wife, died recently, whereupon housekeeper at
once got permission from the State Orphanage
authorities to keep the seven children on the
boarding-out system, at 6s. a week each. The
Bulletin would much like to have been behind the
door when the W.C.T.U. and the Y. W.C.A. were
discussing this arrangement.
“ C.S.” : Be mention of
youth addicted to torturing
fowls and such-lilce. About
nine years back, up North, a
young man, son of people in
good social position, was con-
victed of the murder of a
German new-chum. Murderer
and victim stayed at same
boarding-house. Victim went
off to a bush job on foot.
Murderer followed him up,
and bashed his head in for
value of swag and a few shil-
lings. Subsequently became
known that murderer in
childhood used to cut off
fowls’ legs to enjoy seeing
them struggle abound on
bleeding stumps. Also de-
lighted in amputating cats’
ears and tails, and jabbing fire-
sticks into frogs’ and lizards’
eyes. Later on, at the age of
14, this young devil was in a boat
with another boy who could not
swim, and other boy was mysteri-
ously drowned. Now known that
he was thrown overboard “ for a
lark,” and hammered on head with
oar—also for fun ! Subsequently,
a solitary old Austrian basket-
maker was found murdered in his
humpy, and his poor savings stolen.
Crime never sheeted home, but
now pretty certain that it was
the work of this human fiend.
Several other disappearances put
down to same criminal, who, by
the way, resided awhile with
some semi-civilised blacks until
even they couldn’t stand his
fiendishness. All this pro-
mises a lively future for the
district to which youth men-
tioned in B. l/4/’99 betakes
himself when he leaves re-
formatory.

WHEN A LADY
MAKES THE PACE
FOR THE BULLETIN.
I’m a poet,
You may know it
By my passion for the
trees,
My emotion
By the Ocean
Weaves me verses such
as these:
I can cycle
On a bycle,
I can coast a wild-goose
whirl,
I can pedal
With one treadle,
I can dawdle with a girl.
Oh ! the pleasure
Of the measure !
Oh 1 the sunlight of her
face,
As we crack on
Every track on
When a Lady makes the
pace.
On a shady
Road a lady
Often wheels alone ivith
me,
And her ringing
Voice keeps singing
To my gentle poesy.
Oh the utter
Ceaseless flutter
Of my palpitating heart 1
Oh the slender
Hand, and tender
Glance she gave me at
the start !
Then I wondered
Ho w I blundered —
How our ride became a
race ;
Till controlling
My wild rolling
She took up and made the then-* !
pace. «
Now, quite easy
By the breezy
Roads we wheel round
Double Bay,
And we chatter
On —no matter
What we either think or
say.
It’s a rum thing
There is something
Always tender to express,
Till you whisper
Something crisper
And the lady answers
“ Yes.”
Then it changes,
Something ranges
Up between you awkward-
like.
“ Ah, now Billy,
Don’t be silly.
Do you want to break the
b ike ? ”
I may ask her
If I task her—
“ Would she sloiv a little
more ”
But she merely
Blushes dearly,
And goes scorching on
before.
Oh. the pleasure
Of the measure !
Oh the Glory of the race !
Cupid torching
Lights the scorching
When a lady makes the
pace.

“ Puyyaka ” to The Bulletin re leprosy :
Judging from the results of recent observations in
leprosy, it behoves the Queenslander 10 regard bis pro-
boscis'with suspicion, and to use a nasal douche at regular
intervals. The great length of the apparent incubatory
period of the disease (before the development of skiri-
tubercles and nervous symptoms) was a standing puzzle
until Jeanselme noted the exceeding prevalence of nose
and throat complications in the early stages. The micro-
scope showed the characteristic bacillus of Hansen in the
discharge from such lesions. Sticker followed up the
clue in India, identifying the bacillus in the
nasal discharge of 83'6 per cent, of lepers ex-
amined by him. Further research tends to prove the
initial lesion of leprosy to consist of a specific ulceration
of the cartilaginous division of the nose. This may be
present for years before any other symptom shows up,
and is an active source of infection from its commence-
ment. The ulcer is easily recognisable on examination,
and by its spread produces the disorganisation of
mucous membrane and cartilage which leads to the
almost characteristic “ binocular nose ” of the developed
disease. Both Jeanselme and Sticker, together with
Laurens, another well-known observer, have stated that
occasional Vmt persistently-recurring nose-bleed is one of
the earliest evidences of leprosy, and that in an infected
locality it is probably quite as indicative a symptom as
blood-spitting in early lung-tubercle. The importance of
such a discovery is enormous, as affording a means by
which lepers can be detected and isolated at an early
stage. At present an infectious person has a term of
years in which to spread the seeds of his ghastly burden
through the community.
» * * *
Women and girls may be met going about
in trousers in and around Dubbo, N.S.W. Bee-
keeping has become a great industry there among
women-folk, and the exigencies of the business
necessitate trousers. Getting used to wearing
them in business, the wearers stroll around uncon-
cernedly at other times.

A Hobart Mercury scribe let himself
loose, the other week, on the subject of festivities
at a place called Dulcot, which, in the ordinary
way, is as calm and restful as its name. “If the
beauty around tended to produce meditation,”
said the Mercury fainter, “such was not the case
with the special scene on which I gazed. Here
was unflagging energy. The occasion was the in-
ception of what was to be the annual school-treat
of the Dulcot State scholars—and aright merry
scene it was. The youngsters in all their finery,

Of all Westralia, the Boulder district
alone still lives in the champagne age. When
Premier Bigjohn went there the other day there
was a mayoral reception at 11, a champagne
luncheon at 1, a champagne public reception at 4,
a champagne banquet (at Kalgoorlie) at 8. And
a few of ’em had a little supper afterwards.
**. * *
Although Jawbone Neild has doubtless
made a superlative ass of himself, his superiors are
no better. The N.S. W. Vohmteer Act gi ves no real
power of punishment, and Neild’s offence (if any)
not having been committed when on duty, he is
not amenable to said Act and regulations. Neild
is an unpaid volunteer, and it is very doubtful if
he can be called to account for insubordination
when not actually on duty. “Insubordination”
is an elastic term, and has a meaning quite dis-
tinct from disobedience on duty or backwardness
in action. The good soldier who “grumbles and
goes ” is “ insubordinate,” and even a black look
may be “ insubordination,” if the superior officer
likes to call it so.

Chief Justice Way, of S.A., who revels in
nice distinctions, attacks the common expression,
“concealment of birth.” No such offence, he
says, is known to the law ; what is known to it
is the secret disposal of a body after birth.

A Sydney woman had an adventure with a
burglar last week. She woke up about 3.30 a.m.
and saw a man in the act of getting through the
bedroom window.
When she informed
hubby of the fact, he
sleepily declared it
to be all “ rot.”
The burglar was
half-in and half-
out, nicely bal-
anced on the
window-sill
with a 14ft.
drop beneath
him, and, be-
fore he could
make up his
mind what to
do, the woman,
begging her
husband to tell
where he had
put the revol-
ver, seized the
long narrow
Defries lamp-
glass, and,
clicking the
burner - catch,
' made a noise
_ like cocking a
pistol, stepped
out of bed and
covered the bmglar
with thedeadlyweap-
on. With a shriek
the' brigand fell into
the room and lay on the floor conjuring
her in the name of Heaven not to shoot.
Then the husband woke up in a rage,
and requested his pet-lamb not to make
such an infernal row, but to get into
bed, and not try to shoot his only suit ;
facetiously remarking that if she fired
that thing through it it would be utterly
ruined. At last he was persuaded to
look inside the curtains, and there, sure
enough, was a man with his head be-
tween his knees. The startled husband
seized a razor and loudly proclaimed his
intention of cutting the marauder’s
throat. That scared party crawled as far
away as possible and declared that he was
merely an inoffensive drunk who had got
through the wrong window, and only wished to
depart peaceably; but the husband said he
thought he had better cut his throat before he let
him go. At a word from his wife- he reluctantly
consented to let him go with his jugular un-
severed, and opened the door. In order to keep
out of range of the wife’s lamp-glass the visitor
was departing on his hands and knees when the
impatient host closed the door with violence, and
hit the visitor hard on the soles of his feet and
his. hinder parts generally, and fired him down the
stair.
♦ * * *
The two escapees from Hobart gaol, after
over a month’s absence, were both recaptured.
One of them, who apparently hadn’t been able to
get rid of the prison clothes, was re-arrested in a
starving state within 20 miles of Hobart; the
other had never left the city, and for weeks had
hobnobbed with all, small and sundry, including
numerous policemen. Hair-dye, a good suit of
clothes, and plenty of pocket-money supplied by
friends, seem to have done the trick and to have
enabled him to lodge “ unbeknownst ” at a cottage
not 300yds. from a police-station. The pocket-
money was his undoing, for he got on a mild sort
of spree at a central hostelry, and spent the whole
of one day “shouting” for casual callers. As
Hobart is no longer a busy whaling port, or the
home of the remittance-man, the spectacle of a
well-dressed stranger, whom nobody seemed to
know, but w ho appeared to know everybody, treat-
ing all comers, naturally created suspicion ; and
the police came, saw, and conquered. It is only
fair to record that not until prisoner had been
lodged in gaol, and the disguise peeled off him,
were the arresting constables quite sure that they
hadn’t collared some beery belated bagman in-
stead of the “ man who was away ” without
leave.
It was a monstrous thing to sentence those
escapees to six months in irons and one month’s
solitary confinement. Such a penalty is worthy
only of Lagland in Lagtime. The love of liberty,
even under such circumstances, should be treated
rather as a negative virtue than as a positive
crime; it is undoubtedly the Law’s duty, when
putting a man into gaol, to see that the gaol is
(unlike that at Hobart) tolerably secure. The
escapees committed no violence when absconding—
they simply walked out on the roof and went off
quietly—nor did they behave otherwise than as
very harmless citizens while away from custody.
One of them, at least, seems to have preferred to
starve rather than steal. Yet the two well-fed
aldermanic Jaypees have seen fit to impose a
penalty which a mutilated warder or a half-mur-
dered policeman would scarcely be an excuse for.

The leading feature of the Mansfield (Vic.)
murder-case has been Detective-Sergeant Dungey,
of course. Dungey went to the scene of the
crime ; Dungey came back to Melbourne ; Dungey
returned to Mansfield, and so on. Finally an
arrest was made during Dungey’s absence, and
then Dungey rushed back to Mansfield. Just
now there is an unwritten police departmental
law, requiring that all murders and other notorious
crimes must be committed for the immediate
benefit of Dungey, whose character as an active
and intelligent officer is under orders to loom large
in the public Eye.

Ex-prisoner to The Bulletin :
Mr. Panton, Melli. P.M., in recently sentencing a man,
rebuked prisoner’s friends for offering to give hiin em-
ployment if he were let off—said it was shameful that
a man, when discharged from gaol, was received with
open arms by his friends. Seemed to think that all a
once-convicted man was good for was to he as speedily as
possible brought up and gaoled again. The Pantoniau
added statement that Australians generally think it no
disgrace to a man to have been gaoled is a stupid fabrica-
tion. I have been in gaol over a money-misappropriation,
and since my discharge have travelled far but have not
yet been able to get employment, although 1 am willing
for any sort of work ; and I know that more than once the
knowledge that I had been in gaol was the bar. How-
ever, I have managed to keep honest with an odd job and
that assistance from my friends which Mr. Panton so
absurdly snorts about.

The King of the Belgians is an experi-
enced admirer of female beauty; and by this
characteristic he is chiefly known to fame. It was
in a moment of sublime rapture, after gazing
upon the effect of the P.D. corset in improving
the figures of Belgian Court beauties, that King
Leopold knighted the gentleman who gave P.D.’s
to the world.*
The most powerful bird in the Southern Hemi-
sphere is the Albatross, while the most powerful light is
given by the “ Albatross Lamp Wick.” Neither “ require
trimming; ” the former, however, has a strong odour,
while the latter has none whatever ; the former makes
oil, the latter saves it.*
The appointment of Sewing - machine
Kingsbury as a Crown Prosecutor has caused
considerable ill-feeling in Brisbane legal circles.
Kingsbury has not been practising five years; was
called in Dec., ’95 ; is No. 61 on a roll of 66 bar-
risters of Q. Supreme Court; and his experience
of criminal business is limited to several police-
court eases and one appearance as a junior in the
Supreme Court.
