A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, May 2, 1899.
My Dear Myee—
The stories about Lord Beauchamp’s alleged
English attachment are still thriving. As I write,
she was a housemaid (next week she will be a cook,
no doubt), and the Earl sent her to school, and was
sent out to Sydney for doing so. To Sydney—
where the women would never dream of setting
their caps (or aprons) at a lord.
His Excellency’s career is going to start a day
earlier than was expected. He arrives in Sydney
May 18th, and I hear that there will not be all the
processional rites that ushered in the Hampdens.
Among the aides is Lieut. De Crespigny, whose
father was a queer, duty-worshipping aristocrat,
who hanged a man with his own hands just to
make himself familiar with his duties as sheriff.
If the coming De Crespigny makes himself familiar
with his duties as aide m the same zealous way,
the blood of some fat and aggressive persons will be
shed on Govt. House steps this winter.
To start with the supper of a ball isn’t delicate ;
but really, I feel as if I cannot describe the French
ball until I have made my bow to the artistic
collation contributed so generously by the Messa-
geries Co. The ball is a charity for the French
poor of this city, and the gift of the supper was a
great help towards funds. It was a superb effort,
graced with all sorts of cunning foreign salads, and
sweets one never sees anywhere in Sydney, and
French men-of-war’s men who never turned an
eyelash when asked to “donnez-moi two cream
shoes,” or “a little pulley for a dame.”
From this you may—or may not—gather that
the dance was not all “Society.” There were a
good many smart people there, but there were
others who seemed to have been let out with the
hall, which isn’t alivays used for swagger charity
hops. The Frenchy element revealed itself in
pallid men with boned moustaches; and in charm-
ingly-gowned women. Local color betrayed its
vulgah origin by turning restive when the ball
was stopped for nearly an hour to wait the coming
of the vice-regal people. Indignant young men,
who had been frozen with one duty-partner on an
arm for an hour—and the first two dances are
always thrown away by men of sense—broke into
angry clapping as time went on and the orchestra
wasn’t allowed to tune-up. It was a moral lesson
to see the Nicest part of the ball stand patiently
in two long lines, its eye on the door, unable to
laugh or dan.ce or chatter until Govt. House
hacl come.
On the whole, the ball was not well-dressed. So
far in the season no ball has been. But after May
18th there is going to be an outbreak of frocks of
the most dreamlike beauty. Mrs. Lassetter wore
one of the most satisfying dresses in the room.
This consisted of two or three white billowy
chiffon skirts, emerging from a polonaise of black
Breton net, let in with insertion and tucks.
That gifted Melbourne lady, Mdlle. Dreyfus,
sported a Frenchy confection of grey and pink.
The frock proper was of delicate silver brocade.
Over this fell a lace skirt slashed up in
long battlements, that waved about as she danced.
The corsage was cut a good way “off,” and ended
in a row of pink roses. Pretty Madame d’Orgeval,
hon. sec., wore pale green satin, with a diamond
star in her hair. Madame Levic’s frock was a
thing of mind and beauty. Black satin, panelled
with deep gold embroidered chiffon, was the skirt,
and the bodice was brightened with huge revers
of cerise velvet embroidered in gold. Madame
Aengenheyster was in cream silk with little red
flowers on it, and bands of red velvet running
round the skirt. I liked the art of the pretty girl
from Brisbane, who decked the corsage of her
lamp-shade pink silk frock with deeper pink roses.
People are just finding out how becoming a very
deep pink can he. It is the soft, light, tranquil
pink that makes the face look greyer than day-
The night before the French ball, there was
another dance at the same hall. This was given
by Signor Fabris’ friends, and attracted a good
number of people, including a big party from
Mosman’s Bay, where the Signora is now living.
Speaking of singers, people often ask what has
become of Florence Schmidt. The latest news I
have of her is that she is still with the Steffanis.
Accompanied by them, she has been hearing opera
in Paris, and, so far, Steffani has not found any-
thing in her that needs undoing. The only trouble
is that the gifted Schmidt, who is as lazy as all
Queenslanders, has not yet mastered French and
Italian. By this time, the party will be in
London, where arrangements for a public debut
will soon be made. Nobody has heard her sing
yet; a hobby of Steffani’s, no doubt. He always
believed in keeping his pupils for a “ psychologic
moment. ”
The usual crowd rolled up to the Orient Co.’s
wharf to see the Ophir depart on 26th. Some
persons evidently mistook the occasion for a gar-
den party, as they appeared at noon in fete attire.
The fact that Lord Gormanston had booked his
passage from Hobart interested several on board.
Flowers were much in evidence, especially in the
case of Mr. and Mrs. Young, and Miss Lomer,
M.A., late hon. sec. of Sydney University-
Women’s Society, who has gone to settle down in
Westralian wilds with Mr. Yidler for life-partner.
Mr. and Mrs. Howard Willoughby returned to
Melbourne per same boat, and were seen-off by
Mr. Edmund Barton amongst others. The Melb.
e,ditor does not like the federal look of things in
Sydney.
. Seldom has Y. M. C. A. Hall looked more attrac-
tive than on the occasion of Miss Violet Jackson’s
debut before a fashionable and critical audience
last Wednesday (26th). What subtle connection
is there between atmosphere and temperament ?
At first the applause was generous but not enthu-
siastic. When someone arose and closed the win-
dows, immediately encore after encore was de-
manded. Miss Jackson was heard to best advan-
tage in “The Bell Song,” the general opinion
being that she possesses a beautiful and very
powerful but almost entirely colorless voice.
Refinement, strength, and accuracy were all there
—only magnetism is wanting. Madame Keller-
mann played some Wagner-Liszt soli charmingly.
Mr. Burns-Walker warbled of love in all its phases
and cadences, and Mr Campbell, who was not in
very happy vein, contributed some rather impos-
sible recitations.
That delightful body of artists, the Pleyel
string quartette, are giving their first concert of
the season on Wednesday, May 10th, at Sydney
Y.M.C.A. Hall.
Miss Eva Mylott’s concert comes off on Mon-
day, May Bth, at Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall. Tom
Roberts’s picture of the copper-haired contralto is
on view, as an ad. for the concert, in Nicholson’s
window this week.
Last week was devoted to matinees. “ lolan-
the ” on Wednesday ; “ Lord and Lady Algy ” on
Thursday ; Leon Caron’s successful benefit on
Friday—and what a long programme ! It lasted
nearly four hours. “ Pygmalion and Galatea ”on
Saturday. Between them all, half the theatrical
world has been seen out without its war-paint.
It’s a truth that many of the profession look
better as God made them. One newly-imported
lady in particular, who doesn’t cause any flutter-
ings when the hare’s-foot is applied, turned out to
be, in the natural, a pretty, piquante, profiled girl
in white frock and sailor-hat. Picturesque
Joseph Carne, from the Brough co., looked like a
younger Irving when you saw him without a
moustache. Miss Italia Conti flashed a handsome
pair of black eyes out of a clever, broadly-hewn
face that has not yet been judged by Sydney play-
goers. And then, at the Caron matinee, Julius
.Knight stood round while Brough played
Napoleon. Nap. No. 1 wore a very red tie and a
generous smile, but, after a bit, the smile faded,
and he was seen to go out. Evidently one house
couldn’t hold two N apoleons.
A second visit to “Lord and Lady Algy”
showed me some lovely frocks that escaped notice
in the first fascination of the play. In the fancy-
dress ball scene Mrs. Brough is delightfully
dressed in the new purple-red shade of velvet. A
long-trained skirt falls gracefully round her. Over
it is a jacket of the same velvet that reaches to
her knees. Bold revers of silver embroidery
lighten this dashing costume, and a three-cornered
hat and riding-whip suggest an admirable Di
Vernon. Mrs. Brough looks charming, too, in
her last frock—a black net over white silk, with
long black lace sleeves, and a white hat turned up
from the face with touches of light blue. Grace
Noble’s fancy dress is a not very original one of
pale spangled pink, with a polonaise of deeper
pink brocade, a white wig, and pink roses on the
shoulder. Vara Renny—a black-haired girl with
a wide smile—fits well into a snappy brown and
yellow costume with coat-tails. As for the men,
there is something very jolly about Percy Brough
in his purple suit and white curls. And Morley
as the Young Pretender, with his stocking coming
down, is one of the funniest sights you will see,
even at a fancy-dress ball.
In “Pygmalion and Galatea” there is no dress-
ing to speak of. I hear that Pygmalion designed
his own clothes, and is very proud of his dove-
grey. draperies with a border of Indian red. On
the whole, these are the briefest clothes that Julius
has ever been seen in. Ada Ferrar’s Galatea is
quite her most likeable personation, to my mind.
There is generally something cold and diligent
and worthy in Miss Ferrar’s acting that steals
insidiously from her charm, but her Galatea was
both light and bright, and the gold hair and a
Mary Anderson knob helped out her long white
profile.
Need I tell you that there was a thunder-clap ?
Wilson Barrett left the tradition behind him at
Her Majesty’s, and H.M. Heavens have obeyed
ever since. They rumble sympathetically when-
ever a man is laid under a curse, or a woman wants
to make a long, loud speech at the man who has
treated her so badly. The thunder drives things
home as a fragile woman never could. Lucky for
the New Woman in drama that there is going to
be a New Heaven as well as a New Earth.
Between acts, I looked through the playbill, and
found it was not the light, frivolous, rechauffe
thing most playbills are. The paragraphs are
quite readable, and incline towards literary gossip.
As for the careful blending of advts. and photo-
graphs, it suggests a deep intelligence. Julius
Knight looks out of a halo of coffee and tea, just
the beverages for the good young man who died a
Kerristian; and “ Goody ”is surrounded with the
food dyspeptics may eat with impunity.
“ A White Stocking ” was the name of the little
curtain-raiser to “Pygmalion.” Edward Ferris-
known here as an actor—and Arthur Stewart, are
the joint authors of this nice young effort. As far
as dramatic interest goes, the authors have rather
let it slip through their fingers. Beginning with a
pretty and pliable plot about a dashing young
captain who made a bet to act highwayman to a
rich Somebody and plundered the father of the
girl he loved by mistake, many opportunities offer
themselves, but are not asked in, and the play falls
to pieces at the end. It was charmingly presented,
too. Linda Raymond, in a pretty sea-green chiffon
frock restrained her aggravated comedy methods,
and ,played the Rector’s daughter with just
enough abandon. Harry Plimmer made a hand-
some Captain Faversham but was unreally slow in
posing for the final kissing tableau, and Hawtrey
as Rev. John Stapleton suggested a converted
Sapt who still clung to damn and old Port.
Madame Soldene’s Benefit came off at the
Criterion on Tuesday afternoon to a crowded,
gaily-dressed, gaudily-hatted house. Circle,
stalls and gallery were all packed, and the
society element came out heavily. Miss Ida
Chantry was one of the attractions of the
ben. A tall, auburn-tressed girl, she got
herself up & la Johnny Coleman in a brown
suit, red tie, and long sandy whiskers—a trying
costume for a pretty girl. In a faint little voice,
but with a dash of clever diablerie, she sang, “I
appeared to be there, ” and pleased the house im-
mensely. Phil Newbury sang “The Message”
in his own inimitable style. Then “Les Cloches
de Corneville ” brought out the beneficiare, in
black-and-white striped skirt to the ankles, nut-
brown curly-wig, black velvet corselet, white
hpron, white cap, cerise bow in front. She
brought down the house in the kissing song, and
delivered herself of one of the neatest, funniest
speeches heard in the much-benefiting city of
Sydney.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Here is a belated tale of a Christmastime pic-
nic which makes one shudder awfully now that
winter fires have set in. The picnic happened
somewhere not far from Melbourne on a very hot
day, so warm that one of the perspiring matrons
suggested a dabble in the adjacent stream ‘ for
ladies only.’ A secluded spot was available, and
the men of the party having, been told off to range
themselves around the district and guard them
from any risk of a surprise, the women proceeded
to get lovely and cool in the water, But, before
the sentinels had time to light their pipes, they
were shocked by a volley of shrieks. In another
moment there came a rush of damp, unclad
females up the rugged slopes, and the cry was,
‘ Take them off ! Oh, take them off ! ’ In their
frantic terror the matrons appealed to the first
husbands who came handy, and the single girls
didn’t care which boy they struck first. Every
woman Jill of the party had a host of horse-leeches
sucking her blood. Modesty was out of the ques-
tion, so the curtain fell on a tableau of sympathetic
men picking leeches from their respective patients
with the best possible pretence of being blind to
the skinfulness of the situation. By this time, of
course, a good deal of sorting-out had taken place.
Family groups had been formed, and, what with
coats hung across bushes and other hasty arrange-
ments, the situation was not too tragic.
“ The small stir caused last year at that fashion-
able Warrnambool hotel by certain young women
in Melb. society going down for race-week, and
amusing themselves at dead of night by filling with
water boots left outside the doors, is likely to re-
peat itself ; the same high-spirited party having
engaged the same rooms with the object of ‘let-
ting themselves go.’ On the occasion referred to,
a philosophical indifference to wet feet was strik-
ingly absent. Of all the injured, but one man
kept his temper, a R.O. priest, who, supported by
the consolations of religion, simply remarked that
‘ women were rarely, if ever, known to add to the
comfort of any man.’
“Some Melb. illustrated weeklies lately pub-
lished photos, of the two persons most prominently
connected with the Mansfield tragedy, but—
horror of horrors —tlie young lady depicted as
being “ Anastatia, daughter of the deceased Butler
—since arrested and charged with the murder, ” is
none other than a popular local girl who, years
ago, presented the unfortunate family with her
picture. The alleged representation was annexed
by an over-zealous reporter, with disastrous re-
sults ; but the ruffled plumes of the damsel have
been smoothed by a £3O cheque—a joint bonus for
the blunder. Miss Anastasia Butler, a blossom of
34, boasts of having never faced the lens of a camera,
and obscures herself behind a dark veil to avoid
the efforts of amateurs. So far, she has succeeded.
_ “ One Australasian pro-consul persistently pro-
vides in his own province material for such harrow-
ing real-life romances. To this day, after most
expensive warnings, he winks the other eye, when
he gets the chance, at the naicest and most-care-
fully-hedged-in spinsters of his realm. ’Tis said
that the lamentations of one enraged parent (a
sitter in high places, too), whose chee-ild went a-
jaunting in vice-regal company, rivalled Shylock’s
taking-on re absconded Jessica and his stolen
ducats. In the more recent case, however, Pa
was not bereft of both girl and gold. In fact, if
cash pays for pother, the injured patriarch in
question has no more cause to regret the episode
than his dowried daughter, who, in the thick of
the fuss, abruptly took to herself a husband and
set up housekeeping.
“The craze for sending local talent ’Ome has
taken a literary turn. Mrs. Robert Harper, Mrs.
Cornish, and Mr. levers invite subscriptions for a
volume of poems to be published in London by
Miss Frances Tyrrell-Gill, who hopes to get floated
this month. Miss T.G-. has twice as much ability
as some Victorian scribesses now quartered at the
other end of the world, and she is showing a ten-
dency to grow into a businesslike woman, other-
wise her friends wouldn’t have taken her latest
scheme seriously. The idea is to put the bardess
on the London market with a tidy little sum of
cash in hand and valuable letters of introduction
in her pocket that she may add to her list of sub-
scribers for ‘ln Silence and In Sound,’ and push
her claims to consideration as a magazine writer
and paragraphist before her finances are exhausted.
Tickets (i.e., subscriptions) for the ‘ benefit
volume are on sale at 10s. and ss. each.
“A Melbourne lady, whose husband is in the
bike-trade, lost a gold-nugget brooch the other day
whilst whirling along on her wheel. A St. Kilda
hotel-keeper got a puncture in his tyre the same
day, and took the bike to the shop of the husband
aforesaid, with the result that wifey’s lost brooch
was extricated from the puncture. Providence en-
joys a little joke occasionally.
‘ ‘ Apropos the strange story of a post-card stow-
ing itself into an Argus, travelling to England,
and 08 days later returning to the addressee in
Victoria, here is another, still more extraordinary,
relating to a letter. A document from London
was the other day handed into a Melb. office.
Caught in the flap was a tiny envelope, the face
of which, partly destroyed, left only the name of
a town in England decipherable. Opened, the
note was found to contain a tiny gold heart, and
an offer of marriage from ‘ A Gentleman of
France ’to a lady in England. Letter and trinket
are by now in the hands of the person for whom
they were intended—probably to arrive only to
disturb the loved one’s preparations for marriage
with ‘ the other fellow.’
“To be beautiful it is necessary to suffer. The
small daughter of a small fashionable Melbourne
mamma is experiencing the truth of this French
conclusion at the singularly early age of 18
months. Once a week, swathed in gossamer veils,
and other things of a still more protecting char-
acter, she visits the city to have her nails mani-
cured. So far, no expressions of gratitude are on
record. These will doubtless flow readily enough
later, when maturer years will enable her more
fully to appreciate the parental care and fore-
thought reflecting itself in every polished filbert.
“ Louise Hepner, of New York, packing her
Saratoga trunks, promises only four more per-
formances hereabouts. Longer acquaintance
strengthens the visitor’s claims to belle-hood.
She’s quite the stunner in that snappy white satin
uniform, picked out with gold, while as a bold bad
Parisienne in brief black skirt and bodice slipping
off her broad satin-smooth shoulders, the masquer-
ader reveals enough of her personal charms to
prove herself six times as fine and large a woman
as she seems in her first rfile of a round-backed
Salvation Sal. Sipping tea and nibbling ‘biscuit,’
at the Austral Salon, recently, Miss Hepner
seemed one of the quietest, least self-assertive
women, on the premises. At the eleventh hour,
theatre-goers regret ‘ the Belle’s ’ approaching
departure. Things have crisped and lightened
vastly since her Melb. debut.
“ The claims of she theatre-goers are completely
ignored in the ‘ Belle ’ caste. Heaps of blooming
girls, but not a man capable of thrilling the most
susceptible bust. Girard’s a plum, of course, but
provocative only of Platonic admiration.
“ When Louise Hepner wants to seem a scrap
naughty, she gives a funny little back-kick, just
tilting her brief rear skirts enough to display—
neither piles of laces nor satin tights—just a
glimpse of white calico and a neat pair of knee-
frills. Marguerite Ferguson, the acrobatic ‘Pell-
street girl, ’ can’t economise in her dessous ward-
robe. What with somersaults and such, her
nether toilettes are more to the fore than her
frocks.
“ If hospitality is the passport, Melbourne’s
Mayor M‘Bacharn will get his title. Even during
his flying visit to Westralia, at the opening of
Coolgardie Exhibition, he astonished that weary
place with the magnificence of his ‘invitation
dance.’ For one brief evening it took the pioneer
goldfields town back into the thick of its cham-
pagne days.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Does Premier Reid really go away for a
fortnight’s holiday leaving unoonsidered no less
than five death-sentences?
Vic. Judge Gaunt, who lately sentenced a
man to 14 days’ solitary, claims to be the last of the
Plantagenets, or something in that way.
Mayor Solomons, of Scone, lately killed in
a buggy accident, is tho fourth member of a
family of five to die within the last four years—
two of the four being killed.
Premier Kingston deliberately tempted
fortune by running for West Adelaide at S.A.
elections without a working committee, leaving
the whole canvas to a clerk in his private office.
A pathetic spectacle is that of old Jimmy
Munro, late Victorian Premier and Agent-
General, humping his own “To Let’’ boards
around Armadale (Vic.), in his capacity of a
house-agent.
Dear Bulletin. —You err in stating (B.
8/4’99) that Mr. Harrie Wood originated the idea
of a Ballarat School of Mines. Mr. Bickett—
Bickett, Wood and Walker—was the real origi-
nator. —R.L.
James Healey, dead at Emmaville(N.S. W.)
at 81, headed the Turon rush - in the early gold
days and worked round Australia since. His most
vivid memories were, however, of the coronation
of Queen Victoria, at which function he was
present in Westminster 60 years ago.
Dr. Crowley, the much-advertised medico,
whom the society dames of Melb. Women’s Hos-
pital committee made the scapegoat of their own
incapacity, can now afford to laugh at his enemies.
The will of Crowley, sen., who was proprietor of
Bendigo’s theatre and a trustee of the big Back-
haus estate, has been proved for .£39,000.
Judge Bundey, junior of S.A. Supreme
Court Bench (he has had 15 years’ service there,
nevertheless), who has taken 12 months’ leave in
the hope of restoring badly-shaken health, had
a hard battle to fight before he reached pro-
minence in politics or at the Bar. He has
taken life in desperate seriousness. When in
practice he gave liis conscience a heap of
work to do in deciding which cases he should
undertake and which decline; and since he
has been Judge he has felt it to be his duty
to cut himself off from every social enjoyment.
That and other considerations, mostly financial,
led him to drop yachting, to which he was much
attached. And it hasn’t made his position any
pleasanter that he has oftenest in Full Court cases
been the dissentient Judge.
There is but one Judge m Australia who
left the Bench for the Bar, and in turn the Bar
for the Bench, and that man is Pat. Real, Judge
of Queensland Supreme Court, who was a jour-
neyman carpenter, and a good one, before he
started law; and he is a good lawyer, too.
Thomasßurt, thenonagenarian pioneer who
spoke at last Vic. Eight Hours’ Day luncheon,
is the author of the phrase, “A paradise for the
working man.” Graham Berry once said he would
“ make Victoria a Paradise for the working man,”
but Burt is really the author of the phrase.
H. C. Dangar, M.L.C., who returned to
Sydney per Orotava, was called to N.S.W. bar in
1854, but has never found it necessary to appear
in court for a fee. Generally when legal questions
crop up in the Council he affects more than an
average layman’s innocence of their import.

THE EVENT OF THE WEEK.
Arrived in fairly good order and condition one cricket team. “Training operations begin immediately.” —( Cable.)
Hon. (freshest to date) Sydney Smith,
ex-N.S. W. Minister of Mines, denies that he goes
to England. Bathurst will, he says, take him
back next try. Bathurst is no doubt a fickle con-
stituency. _ Cortis beat Suttor there, and Suttor
beat Cortis; later, Paul beat Suttor, and Suttor
heat Paul.; later again, Sydney Smith beat Suttor,
and Suttor beat S. Smith. On the mere shift and
shift-about principle; S. Smith has therefore
ground for hope. '
Clement the Magniloquent is at it again re
country out Curinamiitla way :—“Bones there,
carcasses around, sheep in extremis yonder, tended
by the crows.so hideous and black. Lively alone
is the übiquitous goat. Not a cloud in the sky,
save a dot of cumulus to northward no bigger than
a man’s hand ; while the impish, eye-gouging
crows caw an ugly, prolonged, and doleful dirge
over this awful tableau of desolation.” Wragge’s
way of saying it’s dry out West.
James Stewart Grant, the 94-year-old
patriarch of Manly, frequently competed over the
draught-board with the lately-deceased “ Herd
Laddie.” About 60 years ago many people pre-
ferred Grant's style of play to that of his famous
rival. Grant was admittedly the best player in
Ireland.
Premier Reid has fixed Federal Referen-
dum day for Tuesday, June 20. If he had gone
one day forward he would have struck Chief
Justice Griffith’s birthday, which would have
been a not inappropriate anniversary for a
Federal poll. Then Chaffey Baker, who was
Chairman of Committees during the Convention,
has a birthday on June 22, which is also the
natal morn of Premier Dick Seddon.
The Abb£ Shurr, who in his 70th year has
been appointed Vicar-General of Armidale, N.S.
Wales, is an old Alsace-Lorraine man, of much
scholastic promise in his young days. He carried
away from French educational establishments
trophies of many kinds, among them one which
attested him to be “ master of all languages
spoken in the civilised world.’’ He came to
N.S.W. 28 years ago and buried himself in mis-
sionary work on the N.S.W. Northern rivers.
“FORBIDDEN FRUIT."
Auctioned recently in Melb., the art-
treasures of the late Dr. Fisher, of whom many
yarns are spun. Fisher’s special antipathy was
strong liquor, and the severance of his connection
with the Argus Sick Fund was caused thusly.
A notorious beer-eater applied for a certif. as an
excuse for absence from the office, and, having
received it, sent it in unopened. It read: “I
hereby certify that H. Longprimer is suffering
from the after-effects of a severe bout of drunken-
ness.” Exit comp., and likewise doc.
Mister James Fairfax has just given £5OO
to the N.S. Wales Y.M.C. A. Better for him at
the gate of heaven hereafter had he planked =£soo
to somehow stir up that wickedly slow old print
of his on the subject of Federation. Yim and
vehemence are wanted now, and there is no more
vim in the wobbly and halfdiearted S.M. Herald
than there is in a pink-eyed rabbit.
Premier Forrest is,
to most people, an enig-
ma. They wonder how
he came to be so powerful
a factor in Westralian
politics. If Eastraliana
knew how he is the be-
ginning and the end of
politics in the West, they
would be still more sur-
prised. While he was
absent from home at the
conference of Premiers in
Melbourne, the annual
financial statement was
published, and one of the
morning papers printed
a strong adverse criti-
cism. There was not a
syllable uttered in reply,
by either the Ministerial
organ or any Minister.
When Forrest returned
he answered the criticism
—after a lapse of weeks.
The Ministerial organ
published a leading arti-
cle commending the Pre-
mier’s reply, and re-
marking how odious and
cowardly it was to
adversely discuss the
finances “in Sir John’s absence.” Similarly,
with the Premier absent through illness, &c., the
Cabinet does not dare to do anything, even routine
business. The secret of it all is that Forrest
stayed in W.A. because he is a pioneer and ex-
plorer, a man of initiative and resource. Theothers
stayed there because they had not the energy to
get out. Forrest has little administrative capacity
or statecraft; but he is a decided personality,
and in a young country that is everything.
F. J. Gillen, the Centralian Pooh Bah,
having seen his aboriginal learning safely booked,
gets a spell down South, after 20 years superin-
tending of the mid-section of the overland tele-
graph. Mr. Bradshaw, of the Adelaide offices,
has gone up to take charge at Alice Springs, the
central station.
A popular movement is afoot down by the
Yarra to testimonialise Melbourne’s first sur-
veyor, Robert Russell, now 92 and travelling a
rocky road. A Govt, annuity is wanted for the
veteran planner and builder, whose wife is also
still living. Russell, who has been for over GO years
in Melbourne, made the original survey of the
Marvellous ; the city owes its wide and capacious
streets to him.
In re Tom Mann, the precedent of Don,
the Melbourne politician of yore, may occur.
Charles Jardine Don, the stonemason, who said
he “ hammered blue-stone by day at the Melb.
General Post Office building, and hammered the
squatters by night, in Parliament,’’ w r as nobbled
by the Tories. They set him up in a Collingwood
publiohouse, and he tried no more to be member
for Collingwood. He soon died.
There is no doubt about who is to succeed
“ General ” Booth in the Salvation Army’s boss-
ship. The eldest son, Bramwell, is marked out
for the job. (By the way, the War Cry an-
nounces, in its recent life of the “ General,” that
he and Mrs. B. were married on June 16, ’55, and
that Bramwell was born March 8, ’s6 which
beats even royalty records.) The boss-designate
is the only clean-shaven and white-haired one of
the crowd. The “ General,” by the way, quite
frankly defends the government of the Army by
the Booth family. “It is the government that
God has invented. It was the government of
Eden ; it is the family government; it is the
patriarchal government; it is the government of
the Mosaic economy; it is going to be the govern-
ment of Heaven.”
Reported from Cairns that Plumper
Hoolan and Ernest Hunter have joined forces.
Such a combination is sure to move something in
the North. Hunter is suspected of being the
wild humorist who, at time of N.S.W. Federal
campaign, wired “collect’’ from Sydney, to the
Mayor of every Queensland municipality, “How
is your town on Federation ? Reply.”
LEON CARON FAREWELLS.
On Saturday night, March 25, Judge
Dashwood, Govt. Resident of the Northern Ter-
ritory, passed sentence of death on five aboriginals.
The Port Darwin paper proudly claims that
Judge Dashwood holds the Australian record, so
far as living persons are concerned, for the pass-
ing of capital sentences. No wonder he has
applied for leave of absence.
Barrister John Garland, who is being in-
cidentally spoken of as a capable successor-to J.
H. Want as N.S.W. Attorney-General, is pro-
curator of Scots law in N.S.W. Presbyterian
Assembly. When that semi-ecclesiastical office
was vacant Premier Reid recommended him for
the post. Few men at N.S.W. Bar possess the
knowledge of Scots law and ecclesiastical prece-
dent which the Presbytery require. Mr. Garland
is an M.A. of Aberdeen and an LL.B. of Edin-
burgh. But he isn’t going to be Attorney-
General this time.
One of Marshall’s Lyle’s hitherto-un-
published Deeming yarns concerns the fears of
the murderer that the scaffold was not as it should
be. Deeming had studied the spot daily on his
way to the exercise yard and had his doubts.
“I’m sure,” he said, “ that the drop isn’t directly
under the beam, and that when the trap falls I’ll
hit the edge of the floor instead of dropping
through.” Marshall pulled his red beard for a
moment, then a bright thought struck him.
“Well,” he smiled, with the air of the bearer of
good tidings, “ all my other clients went through
the opening all right.”
Veteran Bonwick, industrious chronicler of
early Australian deeds and misdeeds, has dis-
covered, by his own solitary research on foot, the
obscure and forgotten graves of Hunter, King,
Bligh and Phillip, four of Botany Bay’s first
Governors. He found Hunter, King and Bligh’s
graves in cemeteries around London, and Phillip’s
in Bathampton cemetery, Bath. Some of these
discoveries involved years of plodding inquiry amd
tramping to and fro. Old man Bonwick has done
enormous service in the cause of Australian his-
tory and has been most miserably paid.
South Australia, besides its Tennyson, had
a Burns descendant, too. But the latter outlived
the new Gov.’s arrival only a few days. She was
a daughter of that only one of Bobbie’s family
who bore a close facial resemblance to the poet;
and the grand - daughter, who became Mrs.
McLellan, to some extent continued the likeness.
In certain other respects, however, there was
something of a difference between Robert and the
descendant; for she was “a valued member” of
the kirk, and several of her children—there are
nine of them living—sing, under the boss-ship of
one of them, in the local Scotch choir.
The only Hoolan (as reported by Northern
papers): “ I have my action with the Eagle
coming on. . I have instructed my solicitor at
Croydon to issue a writ for .£lOOO damages
against the Mining Standard for accusing me
of being ‘an informer ’ and a ‘Judas.’ After
these actions are over I shall make some other
paper sit up. If I fail in these actions I will know
that a paper can say what it likes about a politi-
cian without fear of consequences, and I will start
a paper in accordance with that idea.” Eminently
practical.
W. J. Horne, dead at Bibbenluke station
(N.S.W.), boxed the w'orld’s compass. Meant for
the English Church, he went off to sea ; sailed to
many parts ; struck Melbourne when the noise of
gold was in the air ; tickled Bendigo and Ballarat
with pick and shovel ; struck up to the Monaro
and founded Bibbenluke run ; raced for Queens-
land ; tramped the Palmer and Charters Towers
in their roaring days ; made money, lost it, many
times over; back to Bibbenluke once more, still
hunting treasure with avid heart. And then, out
there on the Monaro uplands, into his coffin at
80 years.
R.C. Archbishop O’Reily, who nearly made
the capitation-grant the live question at recent
S. A. Assembly election, is another instance of a
big and restless brain being encased in a weak
body. He is an excellent orator, and personally
most popular. When he ran a Northern S.A.
diocese he cleared every church within his boun-
daries of debt. He is now slowly accomplishing
the same feat in re the heavy R.C. Adelaide
Cathedral debt. “Bedad.” said an admiring
parishioner recently, “O'Reily has a heart in his
stomach as big as a wheelbarrow ! ” *
John Owen, who was an old identity of the
Lower Darling when Burke and Wills made
Menindie their depot, was found dead the other
morning just outside his holding. He was
manager of Tolarno, one of the best-known of
Western stations, 35 years ago. Old John kriew
most of the back-country pretty well up to the
Queensland border before Burke came, and made
numerous plans for his guidance—he reckoned
beforehand that they would be all wanted. By
the way, the Darling-side pub. where the ex-
plorers had their depot is still standing ; what
was their office is now a bedroom.

The biggest damper. “ Ben D.” : Hump-
ing bluey once from Aramacto Georgetown (Q.),
pulled up at a bullocky’s camp, and first thing I
noticed was what I took to be a reduced pipeclay
cast of Maoriland, with Cook’s Straits filled in,
leaning against waggon float-rail. “Yes,” said
bullocky, “ that’ll tell yon ’bout dam cooks!
Couldn’t find my polers ’smornin’, an’ had to go
back to Culloden Downs after ’em, leavin’ this
job-lot Irisher to bake a damper. On’y had about
30 pound of flour to carry me to Hughenden, an’
he goes an’ tips in ’bout half of it, an’ spreads
half-a-bucket o’ water over it, an’ of course drowns
the miller ; shoves in lot more flour, gets it too dry,
then drowns tho miller again, an’at last uses up all
the flour, an’then has it so wet that he has to

pour it into the ashes. Has his fire on the side of
the only rise within 50 mile, an’ of course the wet
dough runs down the hill, an’ he keeps follerin’ it
up with ashes. Anyhow, when I gets back he has
a damper 16 foot 4 inches long. Measured it.
That’s only half you’re lookin’ at ; the other half’s

“ Scrip ” tells The Bulletin the meaning
of Taumatahakatangihanakoauau, the township
marked on M.L. Govt, sale map (8., 8/4/’99).
Taumata (brow of hill, or edge of cliff); haka
(dance); tangihana (sounding of an instrument,
&c.); koauau (ancient Maori flute). Maori in-
terpretation : “The dance which took place on
the brow of the hill (or edge of the cliff), and
which was accompanied by the playing of a
flute.”

“A. A. A.”: Another aboriginal duck-
catching stratagem, formerly used about the
THE CAT FUNERAL.
A cat died in a house some time ago. There were six left. The children always had their dead pets buried-
in style—birds, cats, dogs, broken dolls, etc.
Ibis morning a slow procession of cats, yoked two abreast and black streamers hanging to their tails,
wound its way through the cabbages and pumpkin-vines to a remote corner of the garden where a grave was)
dug. Here it stopped ; and the mournful ceremony began. The eldest girl read •
Oh ! Lord o’ Love, look from above
Upon this poor dead cat,
Wot I ’ave fed an’ put ter bed
This three or four year back
The children here set up a dismal howl, and even the eats saw something pathetic in it, for they hung-
their heads and mewed mournfully.
At this juncture, Schneider (the house-dog) broke loose—jumped the garden fence, and cats were seen
stretching their legs to the utmost in all directions. Even the dead one got lip and ran. That house-dog
was a terror.
C.M.A
Myall. Darkie arranges large tuft of rough grass
or reeds on his head so as to completely conceal it
without preventing him from seeing around him.
Then gets into creek up to neck, and waits near
bank, whilst his companions gently “shoo” the
ducks towards the innocent-looking tuft, at which
they generally begin to pluck. No living being can
stand as motionless as an Aust. aboriginal, so
every now and again a duck is pulled under and
smothered. The others, imagining that their mate
has only dived, take no alarm. When enough
have been secured, the ducks are shooed further
along, so that they may not bo wiser next time,
and the catcher cautiously leaves the water with
his booty.

“ Pedagogue ” : Years ago, when a young-
ster, was appointed to half-time schools at Native
Dog and Bear Valley. After a lot of bumping
about with carpet-bag, I arrived at Bear Valley,
and was introduced to the secretary of Local
Board. The introducer took me aside afterwards
and warned me that the secretary was a Free-
thinker and no good. (The neighbors thereabouts
all seemed to have a bad opinion of each other,
and in general the bad opinion rested on a
good, solid basis). Next morning, when I
opened the school at Native Dog, I found an
anonymous letter under the door telling me
to keep an eye on the secretary, as he was a
specially virulent variety of atheist, and
would cub my throat if he got “ half a slant.”
Then there was calm, sweet peace for two
days, at the end of which I learned—per more
anonymous letter—that the storekeeper’s wife
wasn’t married to her alleged husband, and
that she was of illegitimate parentage. Within
the next week or so I heard, by similar sources,
that the publican had been in gaol, and that
a local young woman was a great deal worse
than she might be without hurting herself.
A month later I was informed that the secre-
tary had positively decided to cut my throat,
and had fixed the date, and that his frequent
visits to the school (ho was the only pleasant
visitor I had) were with a view to surveying
the lay of my jugular and deciding the best
place to stick the knife in before he dragged
it round. Being young and nervous, I left
then—my sudden departure was set down to
the fact that my former crimes had come to
light, and the police were after me—and went
to look for some place where anonymous letters
were less frequent. On applying at the Edu
cation Department for further employment,
was told that I should have stayed at the place I
had—stayed for £6O a year to have my throat cut
by the Department’s own secretary. That was a
good few years ago. The experience came back
to my mind because the one-time secretary cut his
own throat the other day—in fact, he nearly cut
his head off.
• * * *
Told of a Nor’-western fossil that he was,
after many years, induced to visit a son-in-law at
Perth. It needed much persuasion to coax him
on board the steamer, and more to get him ashore
along the pier at Fremantle. Arrived on the
railway platform, he caught a terrifying glimpse
of a smoke-belching locomotive, and resolutely
jibbed. After a terrific struggle, his relatives got
him aboard by blindfolding him and pushing him
backwards into the carriage. He refused to
return, and is now resident with his son-in-law.
N.B.—This yarn may be told in public without
fear, except when said son-in-law is within ear-
shot.

“ Pedagogue Once, when skull-driving
out back, got two new boy-pupils, 11 or 12 years
old, who couldn’t articulate well enough to be
understood. Thought they were tongue-tied, and
called upon the mother (in a lonely gully) with a
view of having the matter attended to. Found
mother in a similar predicament, only a little
more so, and concluded that the boys had simply
imitated her, as she was about the only person
they ever heard try to talk. Alleged father, a
Dutchman, was splitting somewhere, and only
went home once a fortnight.
* * * *
Chaff and molasses, well mixed, are being
used on some far north-west stations (N.S.W.) to
keep life in the sheep, where there isn’t any mulga
to lop or where the poor brutes can’t be travelled
back to the scrub ; but the newer idea is to soak
maize and sow it broadcast. Sheep are said to do
better on the corn.
“ Yarrum ” : An old-time squat-
ter, who had been spending a bad
night at a Yowlahmineroadside pub.,
was, in the morning, asked by a
deadbeat for a drink—he was “ dying
for one.” The d.b. had an old muzzle-
loading gun with him, and the big
fellow told him he’d give him a pound
for a shot at him at 100yds. The d.b.
consented, and planted himself the
stipulated distance away, with his
hindermost part presented. The
squatter went on one Jknee, took de-
liberate aim, and'let her go, but,
though the shot peppered all round
the mark, the poor devil came out
scatheless. Then the squatter swore
horribly, paid the sov., and said,
“You , I’ll give a fiver for a shot
at you with a bullet .”

“ A.H.T.” : Know a big Ameri-
can black who knocks about the river
Murray, a veritable giant, and splen-
did axeman. The troopers of one of
the river townships used to get him—
when “doing time” —to cut up fire-
wood. When the nig. comes to the
township with a cheque, he first
walks to the police-station and peers
over the fence. If the wood-heap is
low, he shouldershis swag and moves
on to next township. If there is a
good stock he starts “on the burst,”
because he knows that, he being a
tough person to handle, they won’t
interfere with him unless hard-up for
wood.

he gave a leap, swung round, and got most of him
over the bridge-railing. Railing held until we

M’Cooey on blackfellow’s natural-history
lore
The Australian “ blackpheller ” used to be regarded by
bushmen as an infallible authority on all questions of
Australian natural history. Even such an able scientist
as the late Dr. Bennett clung with firm faith to the
blackpheller’s ipse dixit. Said b.f., however, is justly re-
garded as quite unreliable in this direction. He has dis-
covered amongst the flora and fauna of Australia every-
thing good for his pumpkin-shaped bingie ; but about the
life-history or habits of the animals he daily devours he
knows little and cares less. He still believes that the
platypus brings forth its young alive, and when, 14
years ago, I discovered, at Coonanible, N.S.W.,
that the echidna (porcupine ant-eater) laid eggs
and hatched them in its pouch, the local b.f.
regarded me as a harmless species of idiot.
Still, our dusky friend has made o?ie useful discovery (all
alonga bingie, of course!). He has found that the gum
of a certain wattle-tree (A. decurrens) is an almost in-
fallible cure for ordinary dysentery, than which no dis-
ease was more fatal to the aborigines. In fact, but for
tfii3 discovery Capt. Cook would have met very few of
them when he struck Port Jackson. For 30 years I have
seen this guru used for dysentery, and have rarely or ever
known it fail to cure. “ Eucalyptus” Bosisto well knew
this gum’s therapeutic value, but, as he couldn’t make it,
he kept dark on the point. The gum may be eaten raw,
but is better gently stewed for a few minutes in milk. It
may be gathered at any time, and will keep for years in
a tin.
* * • *
“ Mac.” re aboriginal foods :
Nothing new in the announcement that goanna is good
eating. Most practical bushmen have tasted it. When a
boy, I delighted to steal off to the blacks’ camp and feast
on roast goanna and snake, to the delight of the blacks,
who love to see their food relished by a white. I very
often even now cook and eat goanna. Cook in hot ashes,
damper fashion, leaving on the skin, which will in due
course peel off, exposing the snow-white, flaky flesh ; the
ofteneryou eat which the oftener you want to. All the
rat-kangaroos, many of our opossums, and almost all
Australian birds are excellent eating. Perameles lagotus
(a species of bandicoot) was recommended by Gould, the
ornithologist, for domestication in England as an edible
animal.

“ H.M.” : Any of your readers know
what is the meaning of scars so common with
N.S.W. bla'ck-gins on middle of upper left arm,
cut both vertically and transversely, and covering
an area of about 2in.x2in ? The gins themselves
cannot be induced to reveal the meaning of this
scarification. They simply look at each other and
laugh. Only once did I get a hint on the subject.
Asking a 15-year-old damsel (who seemed to re-
joice in the recently-inflicted wounds) about it, her
old mother, who was in a joyous state of ruin,
said, “ Oh, dat pheller ” (the young gin), “ him
s<son get plenty pheller husband now.” _ I am in-
clined from this to deduce that the scarification is,
so to say, an advt. of arrival at puberty.

A bush authority dogmatises. Also, he
pigmatises:—
Oil is useless in snakebite, and nicotine is (like soap-
suds, salt, mustard, etc.) of use in strychnine-poisoning
only as an emetic. Pigs are difficult to poison, for the
simple reason that they very often vomit the poisoned
food before the poison has got hold of the system. Also,
difficult to kill with snakebite, because it is not easy for
the snake to inflict a deadly bite through a pig’s skin,
except on flanks, lips and inside of legs. Dogs are very
susceptible to snake-poison, and birds are quickly killed

“TimP.”: Up Mackay way some selec-
tors follow a new lucrative industry in the hatch-
ing of alligators’ eggs. They supply the young
alligators to bush publicans, who put them in the
bedrooms of customers who have melted their
cheques, with the result that the coinless ones
leave in a hurry, believing they have got ’em
again bad.

Will Ogilvie’s book of verses, “ Fair Girls
and Gray Horses,” continues to sell splendidly. It
is unquestionably the most generally-attractive
poetry-book yet issued in Australia. Price, 4s. 6d.,
post-free from Bulletin office.
Roderic Quinn’s book of verses, “ The
Hidden Tide ” (Is. 9d. post free from Bulletin
office) - has received uncommonly good critical
notices everywhere, and has evidently nestled
close to the hearts of Australian poetry-lovers.
By the way, what is probably Quinn’s finest story
—“Margaret”—will appear in next Bulletin
Xmas number.
The Commercial World (London) writes re-
garding the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society :
“ We always confidently anticipate a good year from"this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the fisrures for 1897 show no falling away
from the steady and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success by deserving it, and, that much being conceded,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of public support which it has hitherto
fallen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

less stamps (of any province) accompany.

Artist Gordon Coutts.
Artist Gordon Goutts, a young painter ««no ha»
not yet seen his 30th birthday, sails shortly for
England, after 11 years’ sojourn in Aus-
tralia, of which period eight years were
spent in Melbourne, and three in Sydney.
Herewith is given a portrait of the
artist, and a reproduction of a note-
worthy picture from his studio, “ Too
Late,” shown in Sydney at N.S.W. Art
Society’s Exhibition of ’95. Mr. Coutts
was then in Melbourne, and the encomias-
tic criticism called forth by this picture
decided him to take up his residence and
venture his fortunes in Sydney. He sub-
sequently became art-instructor to the
Society’s classes, a position which he has
but lately resigned. That his relations
with the students have been most friendly
is evidenced by the warmth of their fare-
well. At his studio, 26 Hunter-street, Mr.
Coutts has been giving a final exhi-
bition of his work in oils and water-colors
—some hundred or more subjects ; and
connoisseurs should remember that there
is here an opportunity to purchase.
Among the most distinctive of the oils
is a fine picture of half-lights and sub-
dued shadow's entitled “ A Song at
Twilight.” This is the piece dc resist-
ance of the gallery. Another somewhat
impressionist picture the eye delights to
dw'ell on, is also a “Twilight”—a land-
scape. “ Bay view,” “McMahon’s Point,”
“ After Years of Toil,” “ Once a Gentle-
man ” (the two just named fine charac-
ter studies), “The Cook’s Galley,” “A
Bacchante,” “ Summer Sun*” “Harvest
Time.” and “The Coming Storm” aro
all pictures the writer would like to
possess—and some of them are not among
the highest-priced, either. There are a
score or so of water-color drawings, many
of them very picturesque reproduc-
tions of some of the most alluring of
Sydney’s beauty - spots. A delightful
water - color is “ Outward - Bound.”
Mr. Coufcts is credited with being the most
bashful artist south of the Line. He is also
said to have made the shortest speech on record,
when the Art Society formed itself into a conver-
sazione, in order to present him with a gold watch.
The modest artist, taken entirely by surprise,
simply remarked that he would much rather paint
a picture than make a speech, seized the watch,
and hurriedly retired to hide his blushing per-
sonality among the crowd.
E. J. Brady’s book of ballads and verses,
mostly from The Bulletin, is now ready ; and
will be sent post-free from this office on receipt of
price, ss.

“ TOO LATE!" by Gordon Coutts.
[Strand, Sydney.
A stronger aroma of' —well, call it romance,
environs Earl Beauchamp, the incoming N.S.W.
Governor, than has surrounded any British pro-
consul who has yet come to Australia. Twenty-
seven years old, single, eloquent, artistic, with
the face of a “ shirt-collar poet,” a private income
of =£30,000 or .£40,000 a year, and unbounded
enthusiasm, where is there a man with greater
claims upon feminine interest—especially in view
of the rumor that he is firmly attached to a girl
of humble degree, whom he is having prepared to
take her place as his wife ? The photograph here-
with printed (now some 11 years old) represents
Earl Beauchamp at the age of 16 years. It was
taken by the London firm to whose business has
succeeded that celebrated Australian photographer,
Walter Barnett.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8,5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 246 George-st., Sydney.*

countries when two-thirds of our own country
badly wants them ; Govt, deriving practically no

above robbery only paid to Japanese.
The Finest Site.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Four Bush Politicians weird,
Touzle-haired and rough of beard,
In a Sydney bar-room sat,
Talking there of this and that,
Till at last the talk did fall
On the Federal Capital.
Said Politician Number One —
“ If a thing is to be done,
What I say is—Do it through !
Make the site my little borough.”
“ What borough?’
“ Murrum-burrah !
That’s the finest site of all
For the Fed’ral Capital.”
Said Politician Number Two—
“ Blanky rot! My town’s the true
Site, as anyone can tell.
She’s the daisy ! She’s- the belle ! ”
“What belle?”
“ Nimity-belle !
That’s the finest site of all
For the Fed’ral Capital.”
Said Politician Number Three—
“ When my City fair you see
You will cry aloud—or burst—
‘ Here’s the place for which we thirst! ’ ”
“ What thirst?”
“ Ba-thurst 1
That’s the finest site of all
For the Fed’ral Capital.”
Said Politician Number Four,
Banging with his stick the floor—
“ Want a site to suit you well ?
You had better go to ’ell' ”
“ What ’ell?”
“ Inver-ell !
That’s the finest site of all
For the Fed’ral Capital.”
O.R.

“Pickle Bottle”: “Did you notice that inci-
dent of the burglar and the piokle-bottle at Pyrmont ?
Some individual, having 100 sovereigns, kept them in his
bidroom in a pickle-bottle. Another individual, with an
abnormal sense of smell, scented the pickle-bottle, and
in the stilly night removed it. One more example of
rhan’s foolishness. If the owner of the pickle-bottle had
only placed it in the Sydney Safe Deposit in Ash-street,
at the back of Paling's, it would have been beyond the
scent and reach of even the smartest cracksman, and he
would still have his 100 sovs.*
It takes a bard to catch a bard ; and E. J.
Brady claims to have caught the popular Newbolt
tripping when he wrote that
Drake, he’s in his hammock and a thousand feet below
Brady points out that Drake was not thrust over-
board in the usual sailor-fashion, but was coffined
in lead that he might be better preserved to fight
Spaniards at the Day of Judgment. But
surely the lead coffin does not exclude the ham-
mock? Anyway, Newbolt is welcome to pick
holes in Brady’s just-published collection of verses,
“The Ways of Many Waters,” and a copy has
been sent him to that end and others more com-
plimentary. The price of “The Ways” is 55.,
post-free from Bulletin office.

A correspondent :
Dear Bulletin. — Re your recent just remarks about
the Sirdar and the Mahdi’s corpse, how different
the conduct of Emperor Charles V. After Luther was
dead, and there had gathered about his name the
hate men feel for an enemy who has been too strong for
them, Charles happened to come to Wittenberg; and,
when conducted, at his own request, to the arch-heretic's
tomb, stood gazingat it, full of many thoughts, until it
was suggested that the body should be taken up and
burnt at the stake in the market-place. It was then, the
common practice of the Church thus to serve the remains
of heretics, and there was probably not another Catholic
prince who would have hesitated to comply. But Charles
said only : “ I war not with the dead.”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
A correspondent to The Bulletin from
Manila;—
Manila is just now a city of the dead. After 7 p.m.
the military compel everyone except soldiers to be in
their houses, and this most brilliantly-lighted City of the
Orient—there is electric-light everywhere—is as silent as
the grave, save for occasional sounds of distant firing
from the trenches. Warren’s Circus cleared. They are
doing good biz. at Hongkong, but lost considerably here.
Dr. Harley (who was laid up in Sydney last July and
Aug.) occupies the Teatro Filipino with his magical, &c.,
entertainments. He can only play afternoons, and while
things are so disturbed that is hardly worth doing. The
sensational washing-out gold was a hoax. There is, no
doubt, plenty gold in the Islands, but way-back in at
present inaccessible country. A number of miners are
waiting over, but I fancy they wish they had not been so
previous, as it will probably be years ere any advance
can be made. The interior is very mountainous
and inhabited by fierce tribes, and some other gold-
bearing islands by fanatic Mahommedans. This place has
no doubt a future, but it is remote. For ordinary trades
and callings there is absolutely nothing to do, and men
would find it impossible to get a job. The authorities
are making things hot in many ways, and the burdens of
the taxpayers are unbearable. The Spaniard has his
tongue in his cheek, and smiles at the “ liberty ” the
Yankee authorities allow to the peaceable inhabitants of
the Philippines. Doubtless when Civil authorities are
appointed and things simmer down matters may improve
—there is vast room for it. You cannot get much news
from the outer world. The- press pays for cables, but
little is published. And as the press is gagged, the
straight tip for miners and workers is—“ Don’t come;
but, if you do, don’t hurry.” ’ '
E. D. Millen, Land, Mining and Financial
Agent, 14 O’Connell-st., Sydney. Applications
under Advances to Settlers Act prepared and con-
ducted.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
“ N.Q.” : “ Tatt.’s ” announcement that he will
not advise any drawer of a horse in his sweeps unless
ticket-holder wires him prepaid, giving him No. of ticket,
when he will reply Yes or No, is extra-rough on way-back
speculators who help to swell his coffers. Fancy me,
2000 miles off, drawing (say) Bobadil for Sydney Cup (2
to 1 against for a week and then scratched) ; why should
I pay Bs. or 10s. for a wire on the off-chance, whilst a
resident of Hobart gets his good or bad news in time to
lay-off in such a case ? I wrote to “ Tatt. ” instructing
him to send a wire at any time if I had the luck to get a
probable starter. I would chance the blackmailing,'but
it is no go. Either draw all sweeps on day of race, and
make it an absolutely even gamble, or else give outsiders
a show to hedge, not part. If a man in the eastern pro-
vinces draws Bobadil, or any other hot favorite scratched
at the eleventh hour, thus getting non-starter’s pittance
instead of a snug sum from hedging, this will strike like
a blow from Samson.
* • • •
Dear Bulletin. Re “ Tattersall’s ” sweeps.
“ Tatt.” has agents in different centres. There is one in
Newcastle and one in Sydney. I know, because I have
got tickets in both places, and hope to get more. Sup-
pose 1 to 1000 are for sale at Newcastle, 1001 to 2000 at
Brisbane, and so on. When the sweep closes, I presume
the agents notify “ Tatt.” as to number of tickets sold.
Suppose Newcastle and Brisbane each sell 600, they have
each 400 unsold. The sweep closes with 10,0 )0 subs.
Are marbles 1 to 10,000 placed in the barrel? If so, what
about the 800 tickets unsold in Newcastle and Brisbane?
I believe Adams is square. I have subscribed for years,
and am still doing so, but I should certainly like to know
how this part of the drawing is arranged. It was simple
enough when all the tickets were issued from one place,
but, when sold in different places, what is the system ?
Snakejuice.
* • * *
Editor Bulletin.—Sir. —“ Tattersall ” has been
publishing some information as to his methods of drawing
sweeps, but there are other questions of equal importance
which are seemingly disregarded. It has been noticed by
myself and friends that the special on Sydney Cup was
by no means extensively advertised. I have been a sub-
scriber to “ Tattersall ” for a good many years, yet on
my posting application for shares in Sydney Cup from,
Melbourne on 15th March I was surprised that my first
number (and they run consecutively) was only 313, and
that on such an important race (my shares were in No. 1
25,000) the sweep closed with only 10,000 subscribers.
Inference I will leave to all intelligent persons.—A Sub-
scriber. [What inference ?—Ed. B.]
• * * *
Re Ogilvie’s “Fair Girls and Gray Horses”:
London Sporting Times declares that it needs courage to
back a grey horse to win the Derby, seeing that from the
days of Diomed until now it has been won but once by a
horse of that color. The Seois Greys, says the authority,
would do badly if they had to look for thoroughbreds to
mount them. Singularly enough, however, when a grey
horse is found, and he is at all a good one, he is invari-
ably popular, even more so than other horses that are
possibly superior to him. Little Gimcrack is instanced,
whose fame is celebrated every year ; and, strange to say,
he is the only animal that is so celebrated, although his
reputation is nothing like that of Eclipse—yet nobody
ever dreams of giving an Eclipse, or a West Australian,
or an Isonomy dinner. Gimcrack, by the way, when in
training was nearly black. When he left the turf he
became quite white. There would seem to be a kind of
fetich about the grey horse—as there is about the red-
headed girl.

“ Bargo” t What bookioaking has come to. At
recent Randwick meet had with me a man who was owed
£4 by a “ Rocks” booky over a bike-meeting a fortnight
before. Found him betting “ scrums ” and “ zacs,” and
he asked friend to wait till the Final Handicap was run.
Did so, and when result was known bookie remarked :
“ Well, I’m hanged 1 38 bob in the bag, and that takes 19
out.” Not being able to stump up, he took my man for a
drink, and on the way was accosted by a bleary-eyed
bummer who demanded his 3d. back because he had
backed a scratched horse in the last race. “ For Gord’s
sake, give it back, Oxy,” he said, “ I’m dyin’ for a beer,”
and “Oxy,” cursing, returned the money.
• * * •
Tlio one thing needed to make the starting-
machine a great success is a steward to be stationed some
30 yards, or so, from the post, for noting the loafers.
Under the old flag system a non-trier easily succeeded in
getting a bad start without making his intention quite
obvious. The machine, as often as not, gives a good start
for them all, yet it undoubtedly tends to make bad luck
for some of the triers. Some horses that are not “ on the
job ” may get a little in front of the genuine candidates,
and, as their riders immediately begin to take th n;s
easily, the unfortunates in the rear are badly blocked.
Under the gate-system, the trier that isn’t one of the
quickest off the mark has a worse chance than it would
get if the field were “ despatched in rather straggling
order” by means of the flag, for then the “st.iff-’uns”
would mostly fail to get away, leaving the eager ones to
enjoy a fair fight among themselves. Granting that the
machine compels a somewhat level start, and saves a
good deal of irritating delay at the post, what can pun-
ters gain by the “improvement” as long as the practice
of early pulling, which amounts to blocking, is allowed to
go on unchecked ? It merely adds to the glorious uncer-
tainty, and plays into the hands of Ikey Mo.
* * * *
A common dodge of “ fly ” punters in Tasmania
is, when the tote-tickets are ss. each, during the rush for
tickets just prior to a race, to put down a two-shilling
piece covered by a half-crown, and thus beat the club for
sixpence.
• * • •
The practice adopted by M.L. trotting stewards
of putting up a rider of their own in a subsequent race
on a trotter whom they suspect as a non-trier earlier
in the day does not always turn out well (says a Bulletin
correspondent). Some horses, accustomed to the same
trainer and rider, know immediately when a fresh hand
takes the reins, and act accordingly. They may shape
far better than when sent along under their own mentor.
But the latter always has the best of it because he and
his horse understand each other, while the new-chum,
put up by the club, is, in many cases, at loggerheads with
his charge. A case in Southern M.L., the other day : A
hot favorite went down in a mile trot, cut out in a cer-
tain time. The dissatisfied stewards put up a man of
their own in the next mile ; result, less satisfaction. Then
they were satisfied.
* * * *
An M.L. trotting-yarn to hand shows how
clever some M.L. stewards are at detection of
“ ringers.” An owner of two horses, one good, another
indifferent, wired to a well-known driver to steer them
on the morrow, and to meet train that night and stable
them—sender would arrive in morning. Something
occurred, and owner didn't reach racecourse until after
first event, which his horse had won. Astonished, h ■
asked, “ How did hs win?” “Oh, anyhow.” “But”
(noticing sweat on the good horse) “ tell me, which horse
did you start ?” “ Why, this cove.” “ Oh 1 for heaven’s
sake cover ’em up, arid let’s get off the course ; he was
never entered for the race.”

Melbourne has tbe new woman of the turf. She
trains, rubs down, saddles, unsaddles, and leads to the
course her own ponies. Some early morning touts aver
that this Australian “ Mr. Jersey ” frequently rides the
gee-gees in their work— wearing bivechesand-boots the
while.

He the “ no money ” labelled-dang u-ous section
of the leather-lunged. Writer was much amused at one
bookie going about at Flemington 'last Newmarket-day
w’ith a big card in front of his hat bearing the legend,
“ No money North British or Hvmettus ’’—pretty well the

* * * *
The races were just over at Cow Flat, and
Harry the Greaser had won a Flying Handicap with a
half-Arab mare, beating, amongst others, a horse belong-
ing to Yellow Bill, a Eurasian. “ Bet you a level tenner
1 beat your mare to-morrow,” said Yellow Bill to Harry.
“ Done !” was. the reply ; “owners up.” “Yes,” said the
Eurasian, “ but -no start, no race.” But when, early
next morning, Harry went to the pub.-yard to feed hfs
mare, he found her dead-lame—both fore-hoofs rasped to
bleeding-point. “ Here’s a go,” said Harry to his friends.
“Some scab has rasped the mare's feet so she can
hardly stand.” His friends cursed liberally, but said
their money was on her and she’d have to go for it.
Harry saw no help for it unless he meant to pay up the
tenner, so at the appointed time the two starters came to
the post, and the mare’s backers looked glum at the way
she hobbled. But, to their intense surprise, she never
lost the horse very far, and in the end beat him cleverly
by a head. “ That yaller rascal’s money saved his skin !”
commented Harry, “ but you don’t catch me takin’ no
more chances with mongrel niggers 1 ”
* * * *
The Bulletin just now is not taking any yarns
re the in-and-out running of bicycle “champions.” For
one thing, the young men with the nubbly legs and im-
movable cast of countenance are not interesting to the
public at large ; and, in the next place, their perform-
ances are not “ scandalous.” The wail of the punter who
has put his eighteenpence on the wrong champion doesn’t
reach this journal’s heart. Bike-racing is much more
satisfactory than horse-racing, as regards “ form.” The
half-dozen men who took the prizes in scratch-races
last year are repeating their profits this season—lump
them together, regard them as one man instead of six,
and their “form” is quite satisfactory. When does a
rank outsider defeat the clique of “ cracks ” ?
Horse-racing swindles at the minor meets are
likewise outside the pale of Bulletin indignation. People
don’t go to such places for sport; their object is to be
“in the know” oftener than they are out of it, and the
best way of achieving the object is to get appointed to a
stewardship of the meeting.

A Yic. hotel-keeper was not long ago “reported”
to the L.Y.W. for having charged two biking tourists 10s.
for dinner. On investigation it appeared that the vora-
cious youths had disposed of one turkey, major portion of
sirloin, much cheese, more beer, bottie of claret, fruit,
and various large helpings of sweets. It was indigestion
that caused them to quarrel with the bill.

“The R.C. Church in Victoria is building
schools with great energy, and the time is not far
distant when every R.C.’s children must attend
their own school,’ or take the consequences ”
writes a Bulletin correspondent. “One R.C.
church in Brunswick has struck a levy, and every
man, woman and child must pay to the building-
fund—adults from 20s. a year, children 55.”
The anti-Federalists think they are making
the most of the fact that the present N.S.W.
Premier says he expects to be “ sent for ” by the
Govern or-General to form the first Australian
Federal Ministry, by imputing to him self-interest
in rc his advocacy of the Enabling Bill. Well, if
the N.S.W. Premier is to head the Common-
wealth’s first Ministry, what will N.S.W. have to
growl about ?
The most populous electoral division in
South Australia is E. Torrens, which comprises
the eastern suburbs of Adelaide. It contains
7764 male and 7197 female voters, and returns two
members. Encounter Bay, where the O'Malley
hails from, returns two members also, but it has
only 2355 electors, of whom 1126 are women.
Altogether there are 82,862 men and 68,253 women
on the S.A. rolls.
The fact that Want isn’t coming out
to head the Geebung party, the inadequacy of
MacLaurin, the unpopularity of Dibbs, and the
reticence of Lyne, leaves the leadership of the
Cabba,ge-tree Mob to a miscellaneous crowd of
undesirables. Which position of affairs has given
occasion to a gibe by Barton, to the effect that the
only leaders of the anti-Bill crowd are the leaders
in Sydney Daily Telegraph.
When the N.S.W. Executive consisted of
only six members, one was. A ttorney-General. and
another Solicitor-General. Now the province has
no Attorney-General, and it is intimated that such
an official can be very well done without for some
time. Its nominal Solicitor-General has gone
abroad, leaving G. C. Wade, a mere handy law-
yer, without political or parliamentary experience,
to fulfil the duties of both Attorney and Solicitor-
General.
What a contrast between the brilliant,
dashing, effective fight made by S.M. Herald for
the bill at last referendum, and the tame, prosy,
sleepy, apologetic, advocacy at present. If, in the
contest for supremacy now being waged between
the Telegraph and Herald the latter is crippled
—as seems not unlikely—there is only one thin"
for it: a Federal daily paper which will have fixed
principles and a bright and interesting way of
presenting them. O, for the day !
There have been such extraordinary “ turn-
abouts ” over the Federal Eill that, now that the
Reid and Barton parties have coalesced for the
referendum-fight, great care will have to be exer-
cised in sending speakers out. For instance,
Argyle and the Hawkesbury were turned against
the Bill by barrister Rose ; Goulburn by Ashton,
M. ; King Division by Reid. These gentlemen,
who supplied the poison, ought to be sent to pro-
vide an antidote —as an expiation.
Teetotaller Cook, in accepting the office of
N. Minister of Mines, thought he had found
his element. But in N.S.W., Agriculture goes
(stupidly enough) with Mines. Hence Mr. Cook
has to admit in one day the moral justice of grant-
ing cash compensation to vignerons whose phyl-
loxera-tainted wine-vines the State uproots, and
to put a good face upon the assertion that his con-
science revolts against compensating publicans in
the event of State liquor-prohibition becoming
law.
No false modesty in Tas. Upper House
candidates. One of them, in his published address
to the electors :
Being interested in Real Estate in this colony, I am
specially desirous of its welfare, and should be opposed to
all arbitrary or fanciful changes in taxation.
Just so. Some {£8,000,000 worth of unearned
values, which have been grabbed by fewer than
5000 Tas. landlords, is practically untaxed, and
85 per cent, of the revenue is raised through
customs.
Even Sydney D.T., with one eye (the
right) persistently closed against true federal
feeling, publishes wires from the country saying
that the general idea is that no opposition would
confront the Constitution Bill if Sydney were
made the capital. But that is the sentiment to
kill the “ Colonists’” League of the D.T. Once
the country denizens get the impression that they
are to'be used as catspavvs (which they will be
now if not careful) they will fight tooth and nail
against the greedy commercial spielers of the
metropolis.
Mildura, which lately voted a second time
for municipally-owned pubs., has drafted its
scheme and sent it on to Vic. Premier Turner,
who is asked to submit it to Parliament in the
form of an Amending Licensing Bill. The pro-
posal is that the local shire council shall appoint
the committee of management from its own mem-
bers ; net profits to be divided between charities
(including a hospital) and the local shire council
and irrigation trust. Charities, it is suggested,
shall have first call ; and it is not proposed to fix
the percentage to be paid away in that direction.

A wire from Perth, W.A., last Thursday,
announced a startlingly satisfactory test of the
local coal in a steamer-trip to Wyndham and
back. Also, the feature of the recent Melb. eight-
hour procession was the trophy exhibiting the
progress of the Gippsland coal-mines. Signs like
these should make N.S.W. coal-miners, and those
interested in coal-production, take a keen interest
in the passage of the Federal Bill. Newcastle and
Bulli would look pretty sick if Sydney Datly
Telegraph’s visionary bounties became actualities,
a fear which would for ever be annihilated by
interprovincial freetrade.

The result of the S.A. elections, so far as
known, shows that the Kingston Ministry will
go back to power at least as strong as formerly. All
the Ministers have been returned. Priestly inter-
ference in the contest failed, and Kingston and
Batchelor were returned for West Adelaide.
Editor Denny, R.C. Archbishop O’Reily’s
nominee, was beaten in his attempt to bring
about a state of things under which Protestanb
and Catholic, Jew and Gentile children should nob
sit on the same forms and play in the same play-
grounds. The double referendum was answered
in favor of Federation and in favor of the Leg.
Council suffrage —both planks in the Govt, plat-
form. South Australia could hardly have done
better.
The perennial controversy on the advis-
ableness of Opposition unity in Q. is now ragingin
the local democratic press. Progress, represent-
ing the moderates, goes the whole independent
hog.by suggesting the abandonment of the name
“ Labor ” on the ground that “ it never met a man
who was fond of the thing itself, and the word as
a talisman has lost its charm.” Wallace Nelson,
barracking on the Labor-ticket, describes Pro-
gress’ advice as “inciting to suicide,” and
vehemently thunders for the solidarity of the
Labor-party. Worker, and other Socialistic
organs, while favoring an elastic Opposition com-
bination, deprecate anything like fusion or re-
linquishment of a Solidarity Labor plank. What
the Parliamentary reps, have to say remains to be
heard, but the chances of amicable arrangement
seem more favorable than at any other time since
the coalition. And the new Labor blood is much
richer than the old.

was similarly followed in more than one Austra-
lian paper by columns of biographical stuff ob-
viously written and set-up in readiness for the
death that never occurred.

All material used in the manufacture of
Massey-Harris bicycles is minutely examined on
its entrance to the factories at Toronto, Canada,
and any portion of it that fails to answer the
searching test placed on it by the mechanical ex-
perts, employed for this purpose, is at once re-
jected. Suppliers of steel, knowing this exacti-
tude of test, are careful to furnish only the very
best quality. During the progress of construction
from first to last, Massey-Harris bicycles, in the
hands of trained workmen, are subjected to the
closest scrutiny ; and it is to the care thus exer-
cised that the name of Massey-Harris to-day
stands foremost in the world of cycling manu-
facturers.*

burn, etc. It allays instantaneously all irritation'

cleansing of wounds and ulcers. It will also be
found a thorough luxury for shampooing, effec-

all nursery and bath purposes.*

“E.0.”-: The most up-to-date men in
Brisbane are the ’bus-drivers. Counted seven of
them in Queen-street last Saturday night, each
with his “ donah ” beside him on the box-seat.
Admiral Dewey’s message to the interfering
German Admiral at Manilla—“ If he wants a fight he
can have it right now ’’—showed true Yankee
grit. Bracket that grit with John Bull’s sound
sense and judgment, and the combination cannot
be beaten. This is the case with the London
and American Supply Stores, Bourke Street, Mel-
bourne. Send for their catalogue and you’ll find
they cannot be beaten for style, quality, price, or any
other desideratum.*
Miss Duncan, the energetic N.S.W. Govt,
factory inspector, thus candidly discourses in her
latest official report:—
More than half the pressure which women-workers are
subjected to in the market-place is due to the absence of
cohesion in their ranks. As a class, they appear to be
unable to offer that united resistance which characterises
the unions of men.
The world moves ! Fifty years ago, a Govt,
official who would thus incite to unionism would
hear the bells of authority ringing.

“The White Man’s Burden”
Has during the last decade been
considerably lightened by the
advent of the Pneumatic Tyred
Safety Bicycle. It was the ad-
vent of
Duniqp Tyres
in 1888 that first brought the
advantages and possibilities of
the cycle before the public. The
adoption of detachable Dunlops
in ’92 further popularised cycling,
and brought it within the reach
of all ; and now the culminating
.point is reached by our placing
on the market the nearest ap-
proach to a Perfect Cycle Tyre
ever attained. The new tyres
will be known as the
MULTIFLEX
Dunlop Tyres
and will be speedily recognised
by all Australian cyclists as
a necessity, when Com-
fort, Speed, Reliability,
and Ease of Attachment
are the desiderata.
First in 1888.
Foremost ever since.
We have taken up the white man’s
burden,
And issued a tyre so strong,
That in future the new Multifiex
Dunlops
Will carry all cyclists along.
No need for further anxieties,
All cyclists can ride on in peace ;
The new Multiflex tyres are beauties,
Guaranteed to ensure comfort and
ease.
We have taken up the white man’s
burden,
In helping the mighty throng
Of cyclists who wheel o’er Australia
In the delights of wheeling along,
Safe on their Multiflex Dunlops,
To accomplish by deeds on the wheel
The art of seeing the country
By means of the Dunlop wheel.
We have taken up the white man’s
burden
By giving a tyre that will stand,
To those cyclists who ride for plea-
sure—
Those of the happy band ;
So that the roughest of roads have
no terrors,
Nor the puncture-fiend the power to
detract,
From the pleasures derived from
wheeling
Way back on the old coach track.
The Dunlop Pneumatic Tyre Co. Ltd.

Melbourne, Adelaide, Perth, and Christchurch, JST.Z.
