Queensland is used by the N.S.W. anti-
Federalists as a great number of stalking-horses
of various colors. According to Sydney D.T., no
Federation can be acceptable toN.S. Wales unless
Queensland is in it from the start, and the peoplo
of N.S.W. ought not to be allowed to vote for
Federation except on the condition that Queens-
land comes in. Also, that journal is convinced
that Queensland has not the least intention of
coming in. Likewise, it warns the sugar-growers
of N.S.W. that Queensland will certainly come
in and ruin them by competition. In short,
the D.T. is truly Federal, but it urges the
people of N.S. Wales not to federate without
Queensland, and it beseeches the people of the
northern rivers not to federate with Queensland.
Moreover, Queensland won’t federate even if
N.S.W. does ; but N.S.Wales must on no account
federate until Queensland does.
The Earl of Chatham, with his sword outdrawn,
Stood waiting for Sir Richard Strachan ;
Sir Richard, longing to be at ’em,
Stood waiting for the Earl of Chatham.
But the D.T. goes one better. It expresses itself
keenly anxious that the Earl and Sir Richard
should advance as soon as possible ; but it forebodes
fearful disaster if either of them starts without
the other, and instant annihilation if they both
start together.

There is no end to the anti-Federal calum-
nies of Sydney D.T. Twice within a week
—in its news columns on April 25, and in a lead-
ing article on May I—that1 —that veracious journal has
revived the well-known libel that Federation
means “ twopenny postage all over the metropolis.”
For this grotesque assertion there is not one
vestige of foundation. All that the Constitution
says about postage is that the postal department,
and the power to make laws as to postage, are to
be transferred to the Federal Govt. Everyone
knows that one immediate result of Federation
will be (as it was in the U.S., in Canada, and in
every other Federation that the world has ever
known) the reduction of postage-rates—with the
one probable exception that newspaper-proprietors
will no longer be allowed by the people of Aus-
tralia to have their wares circulated free at public
expense.

Half-caste Shipp, champion little ’un of the
nursery of Griffo, Tweedie and co. (“the Rocks”), met
that all-grit, stocky-built seven-stunner Watson at Alham-
bra A.O. (Sydney) on Saturday night. They started out
on a 15-rounds journey, but little more than a fifth of
that distance sufficed for the almond-eyed one, who, after
a sultry time, during- which he mixed things surprisingly,
quit in his corner badly used-up. Same night and same
place, second round of 10.4 novioe tournament was de-
cided. Chan tier swung his right at Wilson soon after
starting, and sent him star-gazing instanter. Latter
assumed the perpendicular twice, only to be at once
sent down each time, and was counted out on third
occasion. All over in 2min. Griffiths and Ballard went
for each other savagely-sprawling, biffing, banging, and
butting like the raw ’uns at the game they were, until
Ballard lay prone—pumped out, mostly through the stiff
pace the pair had set themselves, for very few punches
got home on either side. Five minutes saw this bout
through. Peter Nelson, fifth man, was left in, and drew
the bye.
* * • •
Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney) 9st. Novice Tourna-
ment finished Saturday night, Lenton and King met in
final and fought like two old washerwomen throughout
seven rounds, but made things merry in the eighth.
King, wearing a Mephisto-like grin set off by sundry
splashes of life-fluid, looked murderous, but wasn’t.
Though Lenton had scored a big surplus of points referee
couldn’t separate them, and ordered another round,
which was as savagely contested as a dog-fight, King
leading this time. Referee at fault again, and declared
the go a draw. Lenton and King, therefore, whacked
the first and second money (£25). Every follower of the
game is now wondering how Lenton" beat Syd. Dunn
(brother of Charlie of the perennial smile) in the second
round.
* • * •
“ Bare-’Uns ”: What game fetches more money
for the promoter than fist-fighting in Sydney just now ?
Saw Alhambra A.C. programme through Saturday night
last—returns showed 800 people present. At Golden
Gate, place packed— not an inch of squeezing room.
Ambitious embryo Foleys and Jacksons were knocking
lumps off each other. Patrons of the big-priced seats
wielded fans (supplied by management) in true Jap.
style. Sections of the house took sides, cheering and
jeering alternately, and frequently supplying a beautiful
blend of both. Profits on novice competition must be
big, as the several rounds of heats are usually spread over
four nights and the prize-money, never totals more than
£3O. New “ clubs ” are opening at Newtown, Pyrmont,
and Balmain ; the metrop. area will bristle with them
shortly.
* • * •
Pirate footballer Jack Conlon fights again to-
night (Saturday), at Sydney, when he will be up against
one of the toughest tasks set him since he started the
fistic game. His opponent, Dan. O’Connell, the Captain’s
Flat miner, is one of the right sort—a natural fighter.
His knowledge of the game was inborn, and not acquired
in a gymnasium. Writer saw Daniel fight one of the
most bitterly-contested battles ever witnessed at Seale’s
gymnasium (Sydney), close on three years ago, when he
quite smashed up big 6ft. Dawson, of Braidwood. The
pair came down country specially for the mill, and a good
stake hinged on the outcome. Dawson was very game,
but Dan. proved too shifty for the big fellow—his right
and left swings were a revelation, and carried all before
them. Cordon's superior skill may pull him through, but
he’ll know he has been in a hot mill ere the pride of Cap-
tain’s Flat goes under.

Conditions of L. Foley’s (Sydney) champion tug-
of-war tournament have induced several teams from war-
vessels in port to enter. “ Jack ” will now-be able to pull
as he likes it best—in his bare feet—and not to be dis-
advantaged thereby. Reported that A.U.S.N. team -
winners of St. Patrick’s Day sports’ tug—wore small
spades on their heels to grip the turf. Soft- hands beat
the police 10 on last occasion, but they’ll be all right next
time, as those powerful paws are being well soaked in
brine.
•W• * •
The two strongest men in Sydney just now, if
not the strongest yet seen there, Pagel and Rennert
(Germans), had no idea, a few months ago, of entering the
brawn-and-thew profession. Rennert was persuaded to
leave his billet as stoker aboard the Prinz Regent Luitpold
to meet ’rastler Harry Dunn at Graeco-Roman, but latter
wasn’t taking any, while Pagel, until recently, was
kitchen-man, or something similar, in a Sydney restau-
rant. Both performed well at Cansdell-Lorenz wrestling
match the other night. Pagel put up 701bs. sphere and
lOOlbs. bell to arm’s length with one hand ; lifted 1921bs
and 2561b5. bar-bells with both hands to arms' length
above head on separate occasions, letting instrument
drop each time and catching it on forearm. Rennert
elevated 1401bs. and 701bs. simultaneously ; lay on his back
on floor and lifted 1921b5. bell to arms’ length, square from
chest, allowed 12st. man to rest upon it and then replaced
the huge weight !

Hon. Sec. (C.): Considerable difficulty in find-
ing out. Feel satisfied your man is a well-known-top-

* • * *
A.A.A.U., made up of N.S.W., M.L., Q., and
Vic. Amateur Athletic Associations, and started with a
big flourish at the beginning of the present year, already
looks like splitting. Having initial championship meet-
ing in hand, it has fixed on two successive Saturdays,
with an eye to big “ gates’’—funds being low. Other
contracting provinces are kicking vigorously, and swear
they won’t send teams, owing to length of absence neces-
sary. Bananalanders refuse point-blank to give way.
The finish should be interesting.
• • • •
W. H. Cooper, the famous “donkey-drop ”
bowler who went ’Ome with the fourth Australian XI
without achieving anything like the success expected,
won the champion of champions trophy of Vic. Bowling
Association for 1899.
* * * *
MTnolty, who won the chopping-contest at the
recent Sydney show, has been matched against J. M‘Kay,
of Murrayville, Clarence River, in a match for £2O a side,
to come off at Maclean Highland Gathering, May 24.
* * * •
The swimming craze has its dangers, too. W. J.
M'Millan, who at 16 won the S. Aus. College champion-
ship, and at 18 was the colony’s 100yds. and 1000yds.
champion, lately died, aged 20. He had broken down
some time before death. Even as a lad of 16 his leg-
strokes were a marvel of force.
* * * *
The brown gelding, Pardon, a son of Blair-
gowrie, who scored an easy wfh at Canterbury, t’other
day, is a likely sort, and promises to go far towards tak-
ing the place of some of our departed cracks. In afore-
said race his party “ came at him ’’ at last minute, backed
him from “ any price Pardon ” down to 3 to 1 and made
no mistake, for “he finished hard-held and alone.” If
Pardon turns out half as well as his half-brother Blair, he
won’t be weighted much below “ Old Pardon, the son of
Reprieve.”
* * * •
The double at Moorefield on Saturday proved a
soft snap for the (Queenslander, Cavalier—a half-brother
to game old Battalion. Nothing could ask him to gallop
in either race, and he should have no trouble in beating
Sydney’s present collection of nags in yet another event
or two. Leperland is far behind the other provinces in
the matter of racing, but horses from there mostly'
manage to pay expenses and a bit over when they come
to Sydney.
* * * •
It is left to a pony-club to first give the much-
naaded stipendiary steward a trial in Sydney. The Rose-
bery Park people, have decided to appoint a paid man,
whose duty will be to not only watch the races, but
study the betting, and generally look out for “cases.”
If they can only hit upon a large, honest, strong-
willed, relentless individual, who doesn’t bet and is no
respecter of persons, Sydney pony-racing will boom even
more than now. What sort of an ideal stipendary
steward have the A.J.O. authorities within their reach in
starter “Tom” Watson—a straight, hard-headed man,
who knows all there is to know, and doesn’t bet!
• * * •
Refreshing to find Forest, the Newmarket
Handicap winner, vindicating the reputation of “form”
in the Hill Handicap at last V.R.C. meeting. Carrying
191bs. more than his Newmarket weight, Forest started
at 7to 4, and won handsomely. The same horse in the
hands of the average Yic. trainer would have been run-
ning “stiff” or out of condition by this time, with a
view to getting favorably treated in another big race.
* • • *
A horse who won’t be far down on the list of
winning sires at the end of the season is the Newcastle
(N.S.W.) imported horse Metal. His stock, which have
been winning races by the dozen in and around New-
castle for some time, are just finding their way to Syd-
ney, and one of them—Magnet, a big, handsome black—
was a winner at Moorefield on Saturday.
♦ • * *
The utter contempt in which Collingwood tote-
man Wren holds the detectives and all their works may
be faintly imagined when it is considered that he has
been running his tote ever since the last raid, despite the
serious charge pending against him. The authorities
will yet be compelled to bombard and shell the place
before they can suppress this indomitable bettor.
• * * *
Mr. Frank Madden’s suggestion to his brother
committee-men of the V.R.C. that a mile course should
be made inside the ordinary track at Flemington was
advisedly negatived, but had "he proposed that a straight
mile should be formed, doubtless he would have carried
his point. At ’Otne the straight mile is a feature on
every good course of the modern order, it being a sine-
qua-non with all courses that applv for registration.
* • "* *
Q.T.C. works in mysterious ways. At all the
small meetings recently hurdle-races have been included,
but coincident with a committee-man’s hurdle champion
visiting the South jumping events are dropped from
future programmes. Rather hard luck this for the half-
dozen or so owners of leppers who were waiting for the
big meetings in May and August.

* • * •
“A.A.A.”: Curious fact that all the buck-
jumpers ridden at Maitland Show are, with one excep-
tion, quiet harness horses, and never show the “ devil ”
that’s in ’em until saddled and mounted. The day before
the - Show one was being driven in a local ’bus, and
another was taking the important position of leader in a
coal-team.
* • * *
Yic. demise Editor Geo. R. Rippon, of
Hamilton Spectator. About 35 years ago he was un-
questionably in the first eleven of Vic. cricket, as a dash-
ing bat and effective bowler, and might at all events have
become a Tommy Wills did he not wisely subserve cricket,
as he did racing and all sport, to the side-showing of
journalism. He attained the respectable journalistic, age
of 61, almost that of -worthy little Skipjack Williams, of
Ballarat Star, who lately hoppyjigged over to the
bloated staff of the New Jerusalem Gazette. Had he
cared, Rippon might have had a throw-in for the sporting
editorship of the Australasian when Hammersley was
ousted, and “ Augur ” Chapman got it. First at Geelong,
afterwards at Hamilton, he made himself the only man in
his sphere. The Spec, may obtain as good a one, but it
will take local years to construct him.
* * * *
The successes of Merman, Georgic, and now
Newhaven in England seem to have brought about a re-
vival of ’Murkan competition. Yankee horses did noth-
ing very remarkable on the other side of the Atlantic
since ’BO, or thereabouts (when Parole, Iroquois, and Fox-
hall were boss performers), until Sibola captured the One
Thousand Guineas Stakes last week. Sibola belongs to-
Mr. Pierre Larillard, who won the English Derby with
Iroquois aforesaid.

first match at ’Ome on Monday next, Bth inst., when
they tackle the South of. England, led by the inevitable
W.G. S. of E., with Abel, Richardson, Hearne, and eo.,
will be a warm team.

Australian teams have always kicked at the
evening-dress-for-dinner custom on board ship, hut the
present team, it is written from Colombo, conformed to-
etiquette, and rigged up on every occasion. As Abigail i
once said, “ They dursn’t say it on the ’ustin’s.”
• * • •
Darling’s election as captain of the Australian
team (with Trumble as vice) shows how wofully out of it
were the prophets who tipped Worrall for the billet.
Darling’s selection is something of an experiment, con-
sidering his poor judgment in many eases when leading
the S.A. eleven, but Worrall would never have done.
But why wasn’t Kelly selected for vice instead of
Trumble ?
• * • •
Huge “Alick” Mackenzie, than whom, when he
plays the game, Australia has no better hitter, opened
out when playing for Paddington v. N. Sydney in Sydney
premiership final on Saturday ; smacked up 40 runs in >
•about half as many minutes—hitting no less than six
“ flyers,” all lovely strokes. If he would but stick to-
hitting be the most popular batsman in Aus-
tralia, and a certainty for ’Ome for years to come, but
always, just when his chance looks brightest, he takes to
stonewalling and ruins it.
* * • *
An interesting cricket problem was raised in &
recent Sydney junior match—Marylebone v. Invincible.
At a. critical stage of the game, a Marylebone batsman
received a hall that bumped high, and, instinctively-
throwing up his hand to save himself a smack in the eye,
the. ball hit it and was caught by a fieldsman, who ap-
pealed ; batsman was given out “ handled ball.” Puzzle :
Was he out, and, if so, how ? What is wrong with a ver-
dict of “caught,” anyhow? The rules state that a bats-
man is out if caught off a hall that strikes him anywhere
on the forearm. In above case, batsman decided he-
wasn’t out, and it took 20min. hard argument, in which
spectators joined, to shift him.
* * * *
E. Melb. won the premiership pennant with
Carlton runners-up. Both teams suffered defeat in the-
final round, East by Richmond, and Carlton abandoning
the match against S. Melb. For Carlton, Warne’s batting
figures are 140 (not out), 56, 77, 402, 17, 131, 120, 29, 49; .
total, 1011 ; average, 127.6. An aggregate of over a
thousand runs is unprecedented in Melb. club cricket.
Sundries ’ (Melb.): The scoring-board on
Sydney Cricket Ground is, undoubtedly, the best in the
world. Stoddart says so, and he is an authority on
barrackers and boards. Ned Gregory planned and built
it in all its perfection, and it is because of Gregory’s death
that I write lest people should overlook the builder and
give the credit to someone else.
* * * *
East Adelaide will probably be the “ swaggah ”
club in Adelaide electorate cricket next season. One of
Gov. Tennyson’s sons is a batsman, and Capt. Lascelles,
the newly-arrived A.D.C., brings a cricket reputation
from England.
* * * *
South Adelaide and Port Adelaide, S.A.’s two
strongest football teams, have seceded from the local
Association as a result of the adoption of the electorate
system, favored by the other teams and carried last year.
The result is chaos, and the probability of the game,
already very low in public esteem, further declining.

treatment is fully described in the pamphlet,
“Piles, and How to Cure Them,” by Albert
Sharp. Copies, with testimonials, sent free to
any colony from the depdt, 257 George-street,
Sydney.*
TELEGRAPHY WITH WIRES.
The Daily Telegraph is out of date,
“ Wireless telegraphy ”’s the theme, of late,
But not to this oar Telegraph aspires—
The same old Geebungs pull the same old wires.
THE GEEBUNG.
What is a Geebung, friend ? I prythee say.
A sickly berry on a stunted shrub ;
Its habitat, the shores of Botany Bay ;
Its rotting root it thinks the wide world’s hub.
Last Monday night some thousands of
people were sardine-packed in the galleries and
floor and platform and walls of Sydney Cen-
tennial Hall to hear Premier Reid and Australia
Barton speak on Federation. Every available
inch of room was occupied, the statue-niches, the
platform railing, and even the crevices of the
great organ. At 8.10 p.m. the crowd unable to
gain admission was so great that a late-comer
thought the show was over and the vast hall was
emptying. Inside, from the platform, the crowd
looked like the waves of an angry sea with’a hat
on the top of each wave. Just below the plat-
form was a little half-moon where the people
sat; beyond that all was confusion. Every-
one was standing on his chair with others
behind leaning on his shoulders, and their per-
spiration streaming down his back. Hoots,
yells, cat-calls, and blasphemy were pitched
at the Premier from an organised anti-Federal
gang that defied anyone to interfere with it. One
huge, grimy giant in a blue shirt and slouched hat
led the chorus of “ Well hang George Reid on a
sour apple-tree,” till an enthusiastic Australian
suddenly kicked „the chair from under him, and,
when the heap that fell was sorted out, fought a
pitched battle and laid the grimy giant out. The
big man could sing better than he could fight.
Away on the left a battle raged for fully two
minutes between a howling ruffian, who used a
chair right and left, and three others v, ho were
determined to, and eventually did, put him
out. What the Premier said was utterly in-
audible, for every outburst of hoots and yells
was met with a mighty counter - cheering
that completely swamped it. Some bold spirits
in the body of the hall proposed to rush the plat-
form, and shouted their intention; it was met
with fierce invitations to “ Come on you—Gee-
bungs ! ” “Try it on, you—curs!” And they
didn’t. At the prospect of sucli a melee, a man
in the southern gallery shrieked : “ O, my bleed-
ing country ! —O, poor Australia ! ” and he was
so overcome with emotion that he leaned over the
railing, and sadly emptied his large and luxurious
tea over the howling mob beneath. Talk about
turning on a fire-hose! This writer has never
seen a crowd scatter with such rapidity as did
that beneath the sad patriot. They simply
vanished, without stopping even to curse him.
Then the organised larynx of interruption began
to fail, and it seemed as if there was going to be a
chance of hearing what the Premier was saying—
till it was found that he was explaining about
the taxation. As this was what the Cabbage-tree
mob especially wished to prevent people hearing,
the din re-commenced, and it and the alternate
cheering lasted right up till the speaker sat down.
When Barton took the floor, a fresh crowd, reser-
vists apparently, took up the yelling—there was
no foot-stamping, as no one to speak of had his
feet on the floor, and the chairs that supported
the audience were reeling and utterly unfit to
stand stamping on. And, on the whole, it was
just as impossible to hear Barton as it had been to
hear Reid—as much by reason of his supporters’
cheers as on account of the Geebung yells. At
9.35 the “ Glory-Al.elujah ” chorus started again,
the man who led it being hoisted on to the shoul-
ders of others. He was attacked, and another
stand-up fight ensued, which resulted in his
being smashed and passed out. That scared
them a bit. When Barton had finished,
the Premier got up and called upon those
in favor of “Federation under the Bill ”
to hold up both hands ; and he waved his own
aloft. Up went a mighty forest of all sorts and
conditions of paws ; and a great cheering crashed
through the building, and the forest of paws
waved sticks, handkerchiefs, and hats, and sank
down. Premier Reid placed his hands to his
mouth and yelled : “ For the contrary—now,
then, you Geebungs ! ” And a thin sprinkling of
hands fluttered slowly and dejectedly in various
parts of the hall, and then collapsed amidst deri-
sive laughter. Apropos, anyone desirous of see-
ing how the wheels go round should compare the
reports of this meeting published in the rival
papers, S.M. Herald and Sydney Daily Tele-
graph of May 2.
That masterful and benevolent lady, Mrs.
Jane Darvall, widow (second wife) of the lato
Major Edward Darvall, a Peninsidar officer who
came to N.S.W. in the old days, and stepmother
of the late John Bailey Darvall, the notable
N.S. Wales Q.C., died last week. Mrs. Darvall had
strong political and religious opinions, and will be
especially mourned in Orange circles. Sydney
D.T. eagerly recounts how she condemned the
Federation Enabling Bill, and “ that good-for-
nothing Reid” with her almost dying breath.
Mrs. Darvall was a fine old lady and real Cabbage-
tree ; many poor people will have reason to bo
sorry she is no more.

Concerning the Sydney Dawn and Dusk
Club'
Dear Bulletin.—l write £o you as a journal with calm
horse-sense, and ask you to hereby make public the fact
that the above club‘is not—as lam informed it has been
variously represented to be a society of anarchists or an
association of devil-worshippers, or even a circle of
mutual admirers. The persons who have made these
statements about if may be highly-respected liars in their
own social sphere, but they are not Duskers, and conse-
quently know nothin” of Duskerism. And what is
Duskerism? A few' of the club maxims may explain :
Roost high and. crow low.
(This fine saying, which has been made the club motto,
is, by some authorities, attributed to the late Bishop of
Bath and Wells; by others to the recent prelate of Sodor
and Man. The origin of wisdom is, however, not a vital
question.)
Do your best work, and don’t get miserable because
another Dusker has done better.
Crow low about your own work. Crow high about the
good work of your friends.
Don’t be too proud. There are harder cases than you
alive.
Don’t look at life too seriously—it is a game.
Don’t look at life too frivolously—it is a game of keeps.
Be happy and you will be virtuous.
Be a Dusker and you will be happy.!
Duskerism is no new thing. It is old as the better sort
of philosophy. Democritus was a typical Dusker. Socrates
was a Dusker and might be a Heptareh now if he were
alive. Anacreon and Horace and Hafiz w 7 ere Duskers.
So were Shakespeare, Montaigne and Rabelais. Not so
were Virgil and Dante and Milton and Dr. Johnson. They
w'ere great men with little humor, and, therefore, not
eligible as former members of the Club, though certain
living members have worked hard to have them
placed on the roll of Deceased Duskers. But it could
not be. The line had also to be drawn at Byron. In
many respects he was fit to be a Departed Dusker, but his
selfishness blackballed him. The living members of the
club are men who write a little, or paint a little, or even
think a little, and take some interest in books and music
and pictures and the graces of life. When they meet
they criticise each other’s work with bracing l candor, and
chasten each other’s conceit by judicious chaffing. They
meet when they wish to meet. The origin of the
i , e of the club is too obvious to need explanation. The
rules of the club are 22 in number, and are printed
in Chinese so as not to give offence to-members. The
only person who understands them is the Heptareh who
hlls the office of Interpreter, and he has power to inter-
pret them when occasion arises in the way that he may
deem most suitable to the emergency. The club does not
w, ®‘ l wake proselytes. It includes the best dead men

When the tired far-west township hasn’t
its herd of pigs, it has its flock of goats, though
sometimes, if it is very tired, it has both. Round
Hill, the scene of an early Barrier silver-boom of
great dimensions, is among those doubly blest;
and, besides, it has a bit of a sand-bank back of
its pub. Lately it was noticed that the sand-bank
had a new fascination for the pigs; and when
somebody, in an unusual spurt of enterprise,
walked the three or four hundred yards to the
pig-magnet he found what was left of a man’s
body. Then the explorer remembered that, three
or four months before, his mate had gone to the
pub., and hadn’t got back to camp yet; and what
was left looked like him.

The long-suffering ’busman, concerning the
new backstairs ladies’’buses : “ S’elp me bob, the
dam wimmen stands considering on the back step
the best part of an hour. They want to go up to
show their hankies, and their men-folks objects.
Now here y’are—just watch.” A couple hailed
the ’bus and, after a protracted discussion on the
back step, the female climbed on top while the
male (at least he was attired in man’s clothing)
went meekly inside and paid for Two.

THE RANGE of Climate in California is
identical with that of Australia. The State
is subject to the same long droughts, and there is
the same need of irrigation. For these reasons,
the New Book,
“California Vegetables
in Garden and Field,”
By Professor Wickson, of the
University of California,
should be in the hand of every Farmer, Market
Gardener, and Householder who wishes to grow
his own Vegetables.
Sent prepaid on receipt of Price, 12/6, by

Theory of the Flaming Sword.
“Ah, talk of blessings ! What a blessing is diges-
tion ! To digest. l)o you know what it'means ?
It is to have the sun always shining and the shade
always ready for you. It is to be met by smiles
and greeted with kisses. It is to hear sweet sounds,
to sleep with pleasant dreams, to be touched ever
by gentle, soft, cool hands. It is to be in Paradise.
“ There came a great indigestion upon the earth
and it was called a deluge. All the evil comes
from this. Macbeth could not sleep.; it was the
supper, not the murder. His wife talked and
tallied ;it was the supper again. Milton had a
bad digestion, and Garlyle must have had the
worst digestion in the world. Ah !to digest is to
be happy ! ”
There ! —how does that strike you for a burst of
eloquence ? I quote from Trollope. If there is any-
thing wrong about the theology you must hold him
responsible. As for its physiology and pathology
(pardon all these “ologies ”) I can answer for the
correctness of these two. And so can millions of
people besides me. They speak of the curse of in-
digestion continually in every language; they
groan and writhe under it in every land and
climate.
‘ Por many years, ” says one of this innumerable
army of martyrs, “I was obliged to bear as best I
could the torments of indigestion. My appetite
was practically destroyed. I ate, of course, because
one must eat or die ! but after meals I had great
pain at the chest and around the sides.
Sleep almost forsook my pillow, and naturally I
was tired and exhausted. Sometimes better and
then worse, but never free from pain and illness, I
lived on with little or no hope of getting well. It
is hardly necessary to say that I had medical
treatment, yet no real benefit resulted from it.
Happily at this time Mother Seigel’s Syrup was
brought to my notice, and so strongly commended
that I laid aside other medicines, which were doing
me no good, and began using this one only.
“ In a short time I realised a great improvement;
food agreed with me and I gained strength. A
little later—continuing to take the Syrup regularly
as directed —the pains at the . stomach,, sides and
chest wholly ceased, and I have not felt them
since. My indigestion was cured at last, and I en-
joyed the blessing of health. My son, who suffered
severely from rheumatism has been relieved by
Mother Seigel’s Syrup as by nothing else he ever
tried. In gratitude I give you full permission to
publish my letter should you desire.” (Signed)
(Mrs.) Ann Barker, Field Lane, Braughing, Ware,
Herts. Oct. 7, 1898.
It was a fortunate circumstance for Mrs. Sarah
Gell, of Melchbourne, Bedfordshire, that one day
she had a personal talk with Mr. Smith, the
butcher at llushden. He told the lady that in his
opinion if she went on suffering from indigestion
and asthma (one of its consequences) it would be
because she neglected to use Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. “ And,” said Mr, Smith, “I speak from
knowledge. ” She had been ill with this abomin-
able ailment for many years, and had spent time
and money in unavailing efforts to obtain relief.
Acting on Mr. Smith’s advice, Mrs. Gell began
using this remedy at once, and tells the outcome
in a letter of which we have room for the con-
clusion only :
“ I was better almost immediately, and was
soon as well and healthy as one could wish to be.
Now I keep ‘ Mother Siegel ’ in the house, and it
never fails to help us when needed for any passing
complaint. ” (Signed) Sarah Gell, Oct. sth, 1898.
Judging from the force of his comment on the
disease, I should say Mr. Trollope knew some-
thing about indigestion from experience. Most
literary people do. To them, and to all other
victims I confidently commend the best remedy
yet found —Mother Seigel’s Syrup.
No Household should be without it To Farmers it is
invaluable. It should be found in every Stable.
All Athletes should use it. It Cures Speedily and
Permanently.

Aches and Pains vanish instantly. A Child may use it
without, fear. It has no equal as an Embrocation. Has
effected more Cures than any other specific ever offered
to the public.

The Case of
Mrs. CLARA CARTER.
(From the “ Penrith Times,” N.S. W.)
(BY OUR SI’ECIAL REPORTER.)
“My case,” said Mrs. Carter, of Com-
mercial street, Penrith, to our reporter, who
was questioning her as to her illness, “ was
one of simple weakness, a sort of gradual
decline, but it was a very serious tiling all
the same, and I firmly believe that if I had
not been fortunate enough to obtain a
I'cmedy which was able to supply what was
deficient in my blood, I should have wasted
and
Wasted Away Till I Died.
Tins wast ng disease (if you can call that a
disease) which showed no violent symptom,
or gave no direct acute pain, stole upon me all
unawares. If I had any pain it was only as a
result of my weakness. My blood was so
poor that my face went the
Colour of Yellow Soap—
very waxy looking- and my lips, the inside
of my eyelids, and my gums were a peculiar
white. I suffered very greatly from loss of
sleep. Strange as it may appear, I was too
weak and weary to even sleep. When I got
out of bed in the morning I could hardly lift
my arms, and in a few minutes I should he
quite tired out. My appetite was as weak
and faded as myself. No delicacies in-
duced a desire to eat, and my food was
not able to strengthen me. It didn’t digest
properly. I was not strong enough to
digest it, and from this cause alone I
suffered keen distress. I grew weaker,
more pale and wan, as regularly as the
hands of the clock went round. Little by
little
I Gave up my Work.
First, I had to cease washing the clothes,
and then came a time when I couldn’t even
wash the tea things, and in the end I was
not able to do anything at all. It took my
whole strength to sit up straight in my
chair.”
“ You must have been feeble?”
“ I got stiil more feeble, and my nerves
partook in the
General Debility of my System.
I was not able to stand anything. No
noises of any kind. 1 should scream out
with the shock if any unusu -1 sound struck
my ears. Iliad silly, nervi us terrors of all
sorts of things, and as my illness clung close
to me a heavy despondency settled upon
me. I began to look upon myself as a
hopeless invalid. I was too low-spirited
to take a hopeful view of anything. No
woman, I don’t care who she he, could have
felt more wretched than I did.”
“13ut the chemists and doctors. Could
they do nothing for you ?”
“No, indeed, the}*- couldn’t. Chemists I
tried without number, but they were all
failures; and lots of medicines and pills
were brought to me to ti y, but none of these
preparations improved my condition any, or
put an ounce of flesh upon my bones. Two
doctors also prescribed for me, and attended
me for long enough. I gave up the one for
the other, but neither of them could do me
any good, nor did I find their medicines of
any more service than the drugs I obtained
from the chemists.”
“ Well, how did you go on ?”
11 For 12 months 8 hadn’t been
able to work, but at last I
got into that state that I
couldn’t even walk*
At nights I frequently had to get into a
sitting posture, for I did not feel strong
enough to breathe properly when lying
down.”
“Did the bowel weakness coniiune ?”
“Yes ; that gave me never-ending trouble
and distress, and a feeling of faintness came
over me several times in the day. The sen-
sation was just as though I was about to
become unconscious. Then I bad to remain
in bed altogether. I thought the worst liad
now come about, and slowly my last hopes
of restoration to health dribbled away. It
looked as though I was to waste away a
Victim to Poverty of Blood.
xVledicines had proved futile, and doctors
had failed. Then a piece of good fortune
happened to me. ‘Try Clement’s Tonic.’
These were the words of a friend. ‘ I’m
sure,’ she said, ‘it will do you some good.”*
“ You took this advice?”
“ I have congratulated myself ever since,
that I did, for in five days’ time the pain in
my bowels had completely gone, and my
head felt lighter and almost free from aches.
And how thankful I was to be able to eat a
bit of something again with an appetite.
Now that I knew
I was Gradually Improving-
I felt as though a heavy load was lifted off
ny mind. The days went by quickly and
hopefully, for I saw Clements Tonic
Was Renewing; my Vitality
and filling my veins once more with healthy
blood. The rest is a matter of course. I
had no relapse to weakness. I grew better
all the time. Something like my old health
and vigor returned. Many people are
astounded. They had seen me weak, ailing,
and pale for months and months past, and
now they beheld me up and about, cheerful,
smiling, and with a healthy colour in both
cheeks and lips. The way I pulled round,
was wonderful. My spirits were splendid.
I grew stouter daily, and it wasn’t long
befoie I could do the hardest work again—
work which 12 months before I had to give
up. My meals were, by the aid of Clements
Tonic, thoroughly digested, and I could say
with truth that
I was quite well and strong
again. The course of the
disease was slow and in-
sidious, the cure complete
and rapid.
And as you ask permission to publish my
statement I shall he only too pleased to
grant your request.”

The Blood is the Life of the Flesh.—Leviticus xvii., 13, 14.

healthy state. To accomplish this it is necessary, once in a while, to cleanse the system of the many impurities which gather in the blood. The simplest way to
do this is to avail yourself of a mild and healthful medicine, which, taken once in a while, will purify the blood and keep the system toned up. Such a medicine
we place before you in DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT PILLS, manufactured from plants and roots which grow around the mountainous cl ills in Nature’s garden, for
the health and recovery of diseased man. One of the roots from which these Pills are made is a Sudorific, which opens the poi’es of the skin, and assists Nature in
throwing out the finer parts of the corruption within. The second is a plant which is an Expectorant, that opens and unclogs the passage to the lungs, and thus, in a
soothing manner, performs its duty by throwing off the phlegm and other humors from the lungs by copious spitting. The third is a Diuretic, which gives ease and double
strength to the kidneys ; thus encouraged, they draw large amounts of impurity from the blood, which is then thrown out bountifully by the urinary or water passage, and
which could not have been discharged in any other way. The fourth is a Cathartic, and accompanies the other properties of the Pills, while engaged in purifying the
blood ; the coarser particles of impurities which cannot pass by other outlets are thus taken up and conveyed ofl in great quantities by the bowels.

pain is driven from the system, for they cannot remain when the body becomes pure and clean.

Nick Vedder’s Gold.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Old Nick Vedder trudged manfully down the
cliff-track to Strahan, picking his steps as one who
had travelled far and whose pack was heavy.
Morris, seeing him in the distance, awoke from
day dreams into a sudden interest. “ Here comes
old man Vedder again,” he said to a companion,
who sat in the doorway of the Cornucopia Hotel
gazing seaward. But the latter, after turning a
pair of blood-shot eyes in the direction of the new-
comer, looked out again between the points of the
bay and said nothing.
“If I could only track him down” began
Morris.
“ Bah ! ” impatiently said the other, who was
called Maori Jack, “it’s always the same cry.
Never a soft-headed old hatter can come down
from the ranges for tucker but you Strahan loafers
must have it that his swag’s full of nuggets.
There’s not an ounce of gold on the whole coast, I
tell you.”
“But he must be on to a good patch some-
where,” insisted Morris. “It was all a plant,
him showing us those pyrites specimens and pre-
tending to believe they were the genuine. He’s
not the looney you think, I’ll swear that. It’s all
a game to hide that he’s on good gold, and it’s a
game I’m going to take a hand in before long.”
But the other, with fine inconsequence, remarked
that they’d have a bit of wind behind them this
trip, and had better be going before it died. That
it would take them two hours good to get across
the harbor, anyway, and old Yedolph had got to
have his keg that night whatever happened. And
for the traders in illicit spirits, old Yedolph, pro-
prietor of the Cornucopia Hotel, Strahan, was a
person whose wishes were not lightly to be dis-
regarded.
* * * *
Morris, dozing in the bottom of the boat, heard
rough steps on the shingle, and, looking up, with
the blink of the slanting sun in his eyes, saw
Maori Jack and Lang, called the “ Butcher,” run-
ing across the foreshore to where the boat lay
beached. On their heels followed Christian, one of
the two local policemen, and the other was panting
lamentably in the rear as he jogged through the
pig-face scrub. Morris pushed the boat clear of
the beach, and stood waiting, ready to pole-off.
Maori Jack and Lang were over the thwarts in an
instant, but as the boat swung clear Christian
grasped the oar with which the boat was being
levered out into the tide-way. “Stop, Morris ! ”
he began, clapping hand to the pistol in his belt.
He did not finish the sentence, for Morris struck
at him with the heavy end of a broken oar, so
that he fell face-down wards in the seaweed.
A gust of wind filled the jib, and the boat edged
free of the kelpage out of the lee of the sloping
cliffs. But Maori Jack, with a pointing fore-
finger to the red stain that followed in its wake,
said, “My God, you’ve killed him ! I heard his
skull crack,” and sa-t down on the bottom of the
boat, moaning.
“ Haul in that —— mainsneet, can’t you ?” an-
swered Morris, savagely, “or we’ll have that
riddling us with shot-holes,” and he cried out
derisively to the other constable, who, after firing
a couple of ineffective shots, bent over the motion-
less figure that lay with the water lapping over it
in little waves.
And then, looking ovei at Maori Jack, still
crouched in the stern with his gaze bent to the
white trail that followed the keel, “You
cowards ! ” Morris said, in sudden rage. “ You’ll
swing for this as well as me,” answering the
horror that spoke in the others’ eyes.
The boat slowly made way across to where the
shadow of Heomskirk lay on Strahan, and the
harbor spread behind them a plate of silver
rippled with leaden lines that seared its surface
ever and again.
“ They caught us with the kegs cornin' up the
gully, ’’said the “Butcher, ’’thickly; “me an’Maori;
and Christian fired at us, an’ we run for the boat,
didn’t we, Maori.” But Maori Jack, speaking for
the first time since they had left the beach, cried
out, “My God! look there! ’’—still pointing
astern. There was a white spot over against
Dead Island, silhouetted on the dark background
of the squall that was heading towards them
across tli6 harbor.„ The police-boat was already on
its way to Strahan with the news.
* * ♦ *
The moon had risen in a ghastly redness when
they beached their boat near the mouth of Stock-
yard Creek, hiding her carefuly in the dense ti-tree
which was rooted well out into the water. “We
may want her again,” said Morris, “if” .
There was a grimness in the unfinished sentence
that sent the “ Butcher ” off into a nervous
oh uckle.
They got swags and tucker at Lang’s hut, and
then, crossing the button-grass, were lost in the
dark shadows that canopied the track. A couple
of miles out they cut the Hobart wire just five
minutes after a message had passed along that
barred every port in Tasmania to them, and sent
half-a-dozen mounted troopers, a few hours later,
jingling along the Linda track on their road to
Strahan.
The slopes of the Frenchman’s Cap are honey-
combed with caves, and in the dense scrub that
clothes the gullies running in to the base of the
mountain men in search of solitude might defy an
army of seekers. The refugees could afford to
laugh at the handful who were making ineffectual
efforts to discover their whereabouts. And, after
a space, this was realised, and the hunt was limited
to a patrol-party, that moved .up and down the
Linda track investigating the little district out-
rages this side of Arrowsmith—all laid to the dis-
credit of Morris and his gang of two.
* * * *
Worn out by exposure and scanty food, the out-
laws at last had to abandon their stronghold and
make a dash for freedom. Morris recognised the
grim necessity when he saw the other two whis-
pering apart, and read their furtive looks. There
were two chances ; one a traverse through the
forest, making to come out at the Huon below
Hobart; the other, to make for the mainland by
boat. Sailor Jack, a law-abiding scoundrel of
their acquaintance, had caused them to be in-
formed through one of their “ telegraphs ” that he
was willing, for a consideration, to let them have
their own boat some dark night at the Gordon
mouth. But they had no money, and Sailor Jack,
with unfeeling caution, insisted on a spot-cash
transaction. “By God, then, we’ll risk a dash
through Strahan and get the boat for our-
selves,” said Morris at the end of a weary debate,
cutting the knot of their difficulty.
And so it was that one evening they came to be
skulking along the Linda track where it takes a
bend to avoid a curl of the Collingwood River.
They had barely time to plunge into the under-
growth that belted the track, at the sound of
horses’ hoofs. A squad of troopers jingled by so
closely that Morris could with ease have picked
off any one of thorn with his drawn revolver;
and their careless talk came plainly to his ears.'
Another bend, and the troopers were gone.
But Morris, beyond the brushwood, had seen
a little spiral of smoke rising among the timber
at the foot of the gully. The three of them
scrambled down, and, after much reconnoitring,
found in the dusk of the myrtles a rough-built
hut. And in the doorway sat “ old man Vedder,”
smoking in silent contemplation, as if never’a
living thing were near beside the “popjockeys”
whistling in the underbrush, and the little ring-
tail ’possums that lived a friendly life in the
blackwood that bent over the hut-roof.
• * * *
Out of earshot, the three discussed the situation,
and came to the one possible decision. Vedder
must have provisions and might have gold, a few
ounces of which would enable them to purchase
Sailor Jack’s proffered co-operation.
Maori Jack kept watch amongst the ferns on a
little eminence outside the timber, while the
others stealthily approached the hut from the rear.
The crack of a branch over which Morris tripped
in the gloom betrayed them, and the hut-door
was hastily shut in their faces, but not before
Lang had put a foot over the threshold. With
his shoulders he burst his way in, to find himself
gripped in Vedder’s thin, nervy arms. As they
lay struggling on the floor, Morris pulled his revol-
ver from his pouch and fired into Vedder’s side.
His grasp on the other’s throat relaxed, and he
lay still, while a little red stream ran from under
his body and followed the angles of the rough
floor.
Lang offered little thanks for the assistance.
“ You fool ! ” he cried ; “ are you mad ? This’ll
bring the traps down on us,” and he edged to the
door, holding his shirt-sleeve to his throat, where
Vedder’s nails had torn great angry lacerations.
“Fill your tucker-bag,”ans vered Morris, laugh-
ing wildly, and himself continued to ransack every
corner of the room in eager haste, tearing down
shelves and overturning boxes in wild confusion.
There came the call of a mountain-thrush from
up the gully, and Morris whistled back the same
note. Lang made for the door with his swag.
“ They’ve heard that shot, damn you ! ” he said
as he went.
Morris, as a last chance, prised up the hearth-
stone, saw a little canvas-bag, grabbed it, and
rushed out after the others. At the crest above
there were three troopers running towards them,
not a hundred yards away. The ambuscade had
been successful.
* * • *
Morris followed his mate up a cut-off, cm ving
away from the track round the extreme verge of
the cliff. At a bend which hid him for the
moment from his pursuers, he seized a sapling,
swung himself over the edge, and lay quiet as the
troopers clattered by on foot, not noticing in the
darkness the scared white face that looked up at
them out of the bed of the fan-fern on the cliff-
edge. Morris lay motionless until, in the middle
distance, two shots rang out—the last, the quick
crack of a rifle. Then he slid down from rock to
rock, helped by the undergrowth that hid in the fis-
sures weather-worn into the smooth cliff-face, com-
passing such feats as would have tried stronger
nerves than his in fair daylight. Reaching the level
where the Broken Creek wound its way, he ran
along the open avenue of button-grass flat at the
edge of the creek till his breath failed him, and
then he lay panting, with the bag of gold still
inside the bosom of his shirt.
He had lost his matches, and could only ap-
praise his treasure roughly by weight. Light for
its bulk, he thought, but there must be 40 or 50
ounces. Fifty ounces—two hundred pounds ! His
breathing grew easier, and his lair smoother
as he fondled and caressed his bundle. It
was dear to him as life itself. It meant
life itself. Plenty of assistance to be bought
with the contents of this little bag. He
rejoiced that Lang—cursed, interfering fool—
was dead. The silence that followed the rifle-shot
meant that. Shot like a trapped dog, probably,
about a quarter of a mile on, where the track
blinded n into the cliff. Morris laughed to him-
self as he pictured the “Butcher”standing with his
back to the cliff up which he had no time to climb
by the rough step-notches cut in the face—perhaps,
if fear had made him brave, chancing the steep
descent, and pitching from rock to rock till he lay
a bundle of red flesh at the foot. Morris started
in alarm at the thought. If the “ Butcher ” had
done a jump, they’d follow him down, if not now,
then in t e morning. But he quickly reassured
himself. There would have been more rifle-shots
than the one that came to him. No, Lang—damn
him ! —-was dead on the cliff above, with a carbine
bullet in him. Good riddance !
Maori Jack had said that old man Vedder was
mad, and never was on gold. Maori Jack could
die, too—curse him !—so long as he was safe.
And then, from pure exhaustion, his brain ceased
to work, and he slept where he lay buried in the
ferns.
* * * *
In the morning he awoke and shook himself ;
and, with a sudden terror, as his memory came
back to him, put hand to side to feel if the bag was
secure. He opened it and poured out a couple of
ounces of the contents on a plate of bark. And
then, suddenly, as he played with his fingers
among the little heap of shining yellow that
gli-tened in the sun, newly breaking above the
Frenchman —with eyes dilated and a smile of con-
tent set on his features in ghastly petrifaction—he
plunged his hand into the bag and held the palm-
ful of metal to his eyes. Then, with maniacal
laughter, he rose and fled, holding tight in bis hand
the little canvas bag, while he climbed and climbed
like the lost traveller whose wits have left him in
the horror of the big bush. And ever and anon
came his horrid laugh, growing wilder and wilder
in the distance as the mountain sides bandied it
back and forth in quaintest echo.
The troopers wound their way back to Scrahan,
bearing among them a motionless burden on a
rudely-contrived litter. One of them had his
upper arm bound about with a bloody handker-
chief.
Sergeant Leggath, who was in advance, sud-
denly reined back his horse, and pointed dumbly
to a big granite boulder alongside the track.
Outlined against the dawn stood a figure
familiar, although the clothes hung upon it in
strips like the bark of a swamp myrtle. “Stand,
in the Queen’s name !” called the sergeant, loudly,
and a wild laugh and a revolver-shot came in an-
swer. A raw young private, who had been fin-
gering nervously at the trigger of his carbine,
sighted and pulled.
Morris stood for two seconds, and then plunged
heavily forward and lay still in the undergrowth
20 feet below, with one empty hand dabbling in
the current of the pool that washed the base of
the boulder.
Down the surface of the stream floated a torn
canvas-bag, to which there still adhered some
specks of yellow, glittering pyrites.
A. CONWAY.

A Woman’s Letter.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How strove this feverish heart of mine
To melt that stony heart of thine
With passionate desire;
And, oh ! with such fierce ardour burned
This thwarted soul, it almost turned
As pitiless as fire.
You never knew—men never do,
We must be won and mastered, too;
And feel, however stinging,
The hasty words. There is a heart
Behind too big to take their part,
To which our own is clinging.
You never knew, with all your learning,
That woman’s heart is always yearning
For love that is as fire.
We must have gods, but not like you
Who are so passionlessly true
Of whom we soon should tire.
We dally with the flame, afraid,
Then singe our wings and drop dismayed
With an unending aching,
With shrivelled hopes and frightful fears.
And with—oh !—such suffering and tears
As set our poor hearts breaking.
You never knew that far within
Our souls we love what you call Sin,
And hate your idol —Duty ;
We praise your good men with a smile
Because it’s right, but all the while
We worship power and beauty.
You never knew that woman's heart
Is of all things things the strangest part
Of that you call the whole ;
With cat-like instincts and a will
To marshal them for good and ill
And switch them on a soul.
You never knew—great man of mind,
We love the man we cannot find
Or is beyond our reaches ;
And take on trust whate’er we can,
Provided it is like a man,
Then—battle for the breeches !
You never knew that, once subdued
By one great soul, we are imbued
With something that can tame us ;
We groom him up and feed his brains
And brisk him on with praise and reins
And drive him till he’s famous.
We are, dear me ! a strange, queer lot:
Some hardly worth an ounce of shot;
Some very weak and human ;
Some winging up so high you deem
They are not earthly—men do dream,
And some like me, a woman.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.
The Betrayal of Martin Carr
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The short winter day was merging into the night,
and a misty rain was dropping lazily. Neverthe-
less, a group of bare-headed women stood in the
light that shone through an open doorway, and
gossiped loudly and eagerly. A man approached
unheard, and stood listening a seeming giant
against the light behind him. Silence fell upon
th u women as they saw him, but the man passed
on without comment. The women watched his
great form dwindle through the heavy dusk into
the doorway of an adjacent house ; savv a light
flash and waver through the window ; and, after
an interval of darkness, the man, standing discon-
solate in the lighted doorway. With common
accord the women went to him.
“Martin Carr,” said one, stepping out from
the rest; “it’s sorry indeed I am for you this
night. But I knew it—l knew it would come to
this.”
The woman’s words seemed to rouse Carr from
a stupor.
“ What do you mean ? ” he asked, huskily.
The dirty, little, cross-eyed wasp !” the woman
went on, excitedly. “He was hanging around
here for the last two days, while you were away.
And this evening, late, they went that way-
together.”
Carr looked mechanically in the pointed direc-
tion ; then he went in, extinguished the light, and
came out again.
Without a word, and unheeding the others,
he started off towards the black outlines of scrub
towards which the woman had pointed. Hur-
riedly he had reasoned that those he sought would
make to the nearest railway town. He would
seek them there.
The short way was through the bush, over
rough hills and deep gullies ; but Carr thought
nothing of that. His blood was afire, and one
idea only dominated him—revenge. The night
had fallen, and the rain had dwindled into fog.
On the crest of the hill Carr turned and threw
a glance at the fog-blurred lights of the town.
Mentally, he was a- year younger. He had met
her—a fair, slight, vivacious woman—and he loved
her. She was a widow, she told him ; her husband
had died a few months after their marriage, and
the world was very cold and cruel. And he pitied
her—loved her—married her. The memory of
that time almost blotted out the present pain, but
realisation returned in an instant, and he went
forward. And through the night he blundered on
and on.
• * • *
A luminous tint in the grey wall to the east told
Carr that day was coming. The light extended
rapidly overhead, and to the north and west and
south he could dimly discern, through the drip-
ping foliage, that he stood on the verge of a preci-
pice, the depth of which was hidden by the fog.
A sullen, persistent roar, seemingly far below,
suggested a rushing torrent. He turned to skirt
the brink of the chasm, when a sudden gust of
wind dashed into his eyes a shower of icy drops
from an overhanging bough. The same wind
stirred the fog like a fleecy curtain; lifted it for
an almost imperceptible moment, and, ere it had
swung back, he had dashed the spray from his eyes
and sprung forward, with a triumphant cry. He
had found them : the fog had given them up ! In
that momentary glimpse Carr had seen them
standing on the brink of the precipice ; hesitating
like people who feared to move, waiting for the
fog to lift.
In another instant he was beside them. The
woman cowered as if she would flee ; then she
turned, and her arm went protectingly around the
cross-eyed man’s shoulder. The latter, with an
impatient movement, shook her off, and turned
his face, livid with cold and rage, to Carr. For a
full minute the three seemed to be listening to the
moaning of the water beneath ; then, with un-
spoken assent, the two men met, their faces white,
determined -for a death-battle. The little man’s
hands shot out and gripped Carr’s throat, and
they struggled backward and forward. Suddenly
Carr’s wife sprang forward with a warning cry—
Hiey were on the very edge of the chasm. But
Carr had seen his danger : with a supreme effort he
wrenched the hands from his throat, and recoiled
quickly. The little man lurched back ward—snapped
uselessly at the air with his hands—and vanished
in the fog. . . . Afar down there was the sound
of crackling brushwood, and the faint echo of a
splash leapt along the crags.
Carr, his face ashen as the enshrouding fog,
turned to the woman who was huddled on the
ground.
“ Who was he?’ he said savagely.
“My husband ! ” she wailed. “It was he who
was betrayed. We quarrelled, and I left him—
and married you. But he sought and found me,
and my love for him revived ”
But Carr had turned, and the fog had engulfed
him—and it never gave him up !
P. T. FREEMAN.
At a Broken Hill mine, recently, a night-
watchman caught a man stealing coal, and, as
said man wouldn’t “stand,” proceeded to take
pot shots at him with a revolver. This is his own
statement, yet up to now there is no word of this
dangerous person being arrested for shooting at a
person unknown with intent to do him bodily
harm. Some fine night a watchman will succeed
in mortally shooting a man who won’t wait to ex-
plain, when it is to be hoped a jury will bear in
mind that it is not a capital offence to decline to
be taken into custody by a private-watchman.
Iri a conversation which followed upon the
reading in Melbourne of a paper upon commer-
cial education, many original ideas were trotted
forth. Mr. E. Scarlett said that the ordinary
schoolboy bookkeeper was an unfortunate creature.
Also, that all the education in the world would
not make a smart buyer and seller. What was
wanted was technical education—the trained
mechanic was the foundation of the business of
the banker and the merchant. Mr. Geo. Bruce
said that good writing was the prime essential in
the boy who entered a merchant’s office. Given
good writing, and a knowledge of arithmetic
everything else would come to him—as, say'
language, knowledge of men, and manners, arid
national idiosyncrasies, etc. etc., not to speak of the
faculty of trained thought, just conclusions, and—
er—so on, you know. After all, what large,
heavy, clay feet these fetishes possess !
The hon. member for Turniptown (Banana-
land) is renowned for the length and sinuousness
of his Parliamentary career. Secret of his suc-
cess—a slavish devotion to the personal interests
of his pettifogging constituents. T’other day,
farmer Brown interviewed the legislator re a com-
plicated piece of land business. The elected of
Turniptown consulted Ministers, obtained legal
information, and, after a world of trouble and
some expense, set Brown’s business to rights.
Brown, gratefully : “ Well, Mr. Downybird, what
do I owe you?” “Owe me, Brown? Let’s see.
Well, call it tenpence for stamps, and the thing’s
square.” And the Brown vote is secure for the
next ten years.
A “Second-class Passenger” writes from
s.s. Barbarossa, near Aden, under date 26 March :
Dear Bulletin.— ln October, ’9B, a certain consumptive
shipped (apparently without official objection) on the
N.D. Lloyd’s s.s. Bremen for Australia ; but, not improv-
ing-, shipped back again (still without official objection)
on the Barbarossa. Got much worse on the passage, and
was informed day before arrival at Ceylon that he must
leave the boat there. As the man was known to be
almost penniless, passengers deputationised captain, but
patient was sent to Colombo Hospital, to be kept a month
at co.’s expense, and then sent on by next steamer— if
better, and if there were room (Prince Regent Luitpold’p
passenger-list reported full before Barbarossa left Syd-
ney). Passengers tried to see Sydney manager of co.
(first saloon passenger), but, oddly enough, he was
always sick, or having a bath, or otherwise engaged.
Evidently the ship’s doctor gave the patient less than a
month to live, but doctors often err, and if the unfor-
tunate young man survived, and the next boat were full,
what was to become of him—friendless and penniless in
a strange land ? Shipping co.’s reserve to themselves the
right of landing afflicted passengers, but the question is,
how far may they avail themselves of this privilege? I
had intended saying more, but perhaps I had better stop
here.
“ W.L.C.” to The Bulletin, from
Noumea: —
Capt. Pentecost (late of Lady St. Aubin) asserts that
“ 0.J.” (8., 11/2/’99) is much “out” about the finding of
the La Perouse relics, his Dad and Dillon notwithstand-
ing. Dillon never found any remains of La Perouse’s
ship, though, at an island some 100 miles to the S.E. of
where the navigator was wrecked, Dillon found a sword-
handle and part of a sword of French manufacture, and
embossed in gold. These articles had been taken there
and bartered ; the second item to serve the natives as a
gouge or chisel wherewith to hollow out their canoes,
which are here very large, and intended for long voyages.
Nothing else was ever found by Dillon, and it was reserved
for Capt. Wooden (after whom the Wooden canal, near
Noumea, was named) to find, in one of the passes of the
reef which surrounds the island of Vanikoro (scene of the
wreck), two of La Perouse’s cannons, an anchor, and
other things, which were brought to Noumea by the
French warship Bruat, some nine years ago. During
Dick Pentecost’s last two trips he found gold, silver, and
copper coins, lead and iron bullets, pieces of china, flint,
and old matchlocks, buried in the earth on the site where
the survivors of the ship’s company were supposed to
have constructed a small vessel from the debris of the
wreck, and Dick’s opinion is that this latter craft must
also have been wrecked on one of the numerous reefs
which lie between there and Torres Straits.
[Dillon first ascertained in 1826 how, when, and
where La Perouse was wrecked ; and solved the
36-years’ riddle. Who found most relics is a
secondary matter. —Ed. B.]
